The Red Page
Kipling
This lordly book in noble type, with nice
blue capital letters, reverently enshrined in
a box, Collected Verse of Rudyard Kipling
(Hodder and Stoughton, per Angus and Rob-
ertson, Sydney), is already in its third edi-
tion. But the title is a misnomer. The col-
lection includes none of the verses Kipling
scattered through his books of short stories,
and few of those published that have hitherto
never appeared in volume form. It consists
practically only of the reprinted contents of
his three volumes, Barrack-Room Ballads,
The Seven Seas and The Five "Nations .
There are no Departmental Ditties, and,
happily, no “Absent-Minded Beggar.” But
he has revenged himself for this particular
sacrifice to his critics by stuffing his big box
with “Various Verse,” music-hall songs, topi-
cal journalism, blatant Jingoism, crude
philosophy and some poetry.
But Kipling’s achievement is beyond criti-
cal sneers. With all its faults this book is
unique; as the product of one brain it is
marvellous. Kipling has recklessly butted
into English literature, and there he stays.
Considered merely as a maker of phrases,
Kipling cannot be politely shooed off the
Muses’ premises: he has added too much to
our common speech. To take only the most
obvious: —
Lord, send a man like Bobbie Burns to sing
the Song o’ Steam !....
For a man he must go with a woman, which
women don't understand. ...
If blood be the price of admiralty,
Lord God, we ha’ paid in fu11! ....
There’s a Legion that never was ’listed ....
And what should they know of England who
only England know ?....
There are nine and sixty ways of construct-
ing tribal lays,
And every single one of them is right! ....
Oh, East is East, and West is West, and
never the twain shall meet ....
Ye fawned on the Younger Nations for the
men who could shoot and ride! ....
The flannelled fools at the wicket or the
muddied oafs at the g0a15 ....
Take up the White Man’s Burden....
O it’s Tommy this, an’ Tommy that, an’
“ Tommy, go away”:
But it’s “Thank you, Mister Atkins,” when
the band begins to play....
If you’ve ’eard the East a-callin’, you won’t
never ’eed naught else....
Ship me somewheres east of Suez, where the
best is like the worst,
Where there aren’t no Ten Commandments
an’ a man can raise a thirst....
An’ I learned about icomen from ’er!....
The Heathen in ’is blindness bows down to
wood an’ stone....
If England was what England seems ....
And most of the “Recessional.”
J*
You mayn’t like the goods he sells, but
they are popular and durable. And he is
more than a peddler of flashing phrases. He is
an artist, but not an artist in literature. His
verse Is not to be read : it is to be spoken,
or, preferably, sung, and, if possible, sung in
a music-hall. When he tries to use words
as they are used in literature, he fails. Take
a serious stanza like this : —•
Pure Wisdom hath no certain path
That lacks thy morning-eyne,
And captains bold by thee controlled
Most like to God’s design.
Thou art the Voice to kingly boys
To lift them through the fight,
And Comfortress of Unsuccess....
To give the dead good-night.
What poetry is there in that? A bald idea
feebly expressed in prose. But turn Kip-
ling on to one of those “common tunes that
make you choke and blow your nose,” and,
like his banjo, he “can rip the very heart-
strings out with those.” To parody him: —
With his “Pilly-Willy-winky-winky-popp!”
(Oh, it’s any tune that comes into his
head!)
So he keeps ’em moving forward till they
dropj
So he plays ’em'up to water and to bed.
In his knowledge of the value of the
spoken word, in his cleverness in the man-
agement of metre, Kipling has a high place.
For an example of this mastery over his
chosen medium, this ability to make his
readers beat time with their feet as they
read, it will suffice for our purpose to quote
from “Boots”: —
We’re foot — slog — slog — slog—sloggin ’ over
Africa !
Foot — foot — foot — foot — sloggin’ over Africa.
( Boots — boots—-boots — boots — movin’ up and
down again!)
There’s no discharge in the war!....
Don’t—don’t — don’t — don’t —look at what’s
in front of you
(Boots — boots — boots — boots — movin’ up and
down again!)
Men — men — men — men —men go mad with
watchin’ ’em,
An’ there’s no discharge in the war!
Count — count — count — count —the bullets in
the bandoliers; _ ,
If — yout — eyes — drop—they will get atop o’
you . ,
(Boots — boots — boots — boots—movin' up an
down again)—
There’s no discharge in the war!
’Tain’t — so — had — by—day because o’ com-
pany,
But flight — brings — long—strings o’ forty-
thousand million
Boots — boots—boots — boots — movin’ t up an'
down again.
There's no discharge in the war!....
I — 'ave — marched — six—weeks in ’Ell, an’
certify
It — is—not — fire — devils —dark or anything,
But boots — boots—boots — boots — movin’ up
an’ down again,
An’ there’s no discharge in the war!
Here, by the cheapest and vulgarest
means, by nothing but his indicated metre,
without help from a single rime, Kipling has
expressed once for all the utter dreariness of
marching. There are no records of men
going mad by marching, but Kipling has
shown why they should. And his readers
must not only read the written word, they
must keep step, they must march with that
column over Africa. Rudyard is surely the
unchallengable Poet Laureate of Boats.
As a poet laureate of a wider thing, the
Empire, Kipling does not ask for the job:
he shrieks for it. He takes the Empire as
seriously as he takes himself; he has deli-
berately adopted the Empire: at times he
thinks he is the Empire. The result is that
the billet is still vacant. There are dozens
of alleged poems in this collection in which
Kipling becomes the gramophone of Empire :
he makes a rather raucous noise. Such stuff
as “Seng of the Wise Children, “The Mer-
chantmen,” the whole of “The Song of the
English,” “Our Lady of the Snows.” “The
Young Queen” —piffle about the Common-
wealth, “The Flowers,” “The Native-Born,”
“Dirge of Dead Sisters” —the nurses in
South Africa, that unconvincing poem,
“South Africa,” with its silly refrain, “The
English Flag,” etc. —this sort of song is
gramophoned, not sung. Every one of the
seven, or 17, nations must have a poena, and
in the rest of his imperial verses each colony
or dominion must have its little verse. This
is the seventieth way ©f constructing Empire
lays. It is like trying to build a Gothic
cathedral out of toy picture blocks.
But Kipling can write a good ballad. It
is more accurate to say he could write a
good ballad, for he soon outgrew the ballad-
impulse. “The Ballad of Boh Da Thone” is
an admirable example of the true ballad
spirit:—•
The word of a scout—a march by night —
A rush through the mist —a scattering fight —
A volley from cover —a corpse in the clear-
ing —
The glimpse of a loin-cloth and heavy jade
earring —
The flare of a village—the tally of slain —
And.... the Boh was abroad on the raid
again !
And “The Ballad of East and West” has,
too, the rousing swing and the words that
pulse with the rhythm of youthful blood.
This hotch-potch of verse and other things
hardly gives Kipling the poet a chance. The
inclusion of so much mere journalism, so
much pretentious Imperial piffle, so many
deliberate purple patches, is a formidable in-
dictment of Kipling’s critical judgment.
Does the man really think that every verse
he wrote, except “The Absent-Minded Beg-
gar,” and the other omissions, should be in-
scribed on these fine tablets? Yet there is,
for remembrance, the residue: the camp-fire
“Song of the Banjo” ; “McAndrew’s Hymn,”
the root-idea of which is universal enough
to emerge from the too deliberate technical-
ities ; the fine characterisation of “The ‘Mary
Gloster’ ”; the impressionism of “The De-
stroyers,” which the poet thought to repeat
in that weak thing, “Cruisers”; the vivid
sense of landscape in “Sussex”; that rebelli-
ous and cheerfully disloyal warning against
kings. “The Old Issue,” with its reiterated
Suffer not the old King here or overseas;
the cheeky “I told you so” of “The Lesson” —
the lesson of the Boer War ; the patronising
contempt of “The Islanders”; even “The
White Man’s Burden,” in which the preacher
allows the poet little show; “The Reces-
sional,” despite the fact that Kipling’s God
is the “Lord of our far-flung battle-line,” a
comfortable private and particularly Anglo-
Saxon God whom one can without danger
patronise; the grim humor of “Danny Dee-
ver,” quite as good as anything of Pater-
son’s ; the atmosphere in “Mandalay,” got
by suggestion, and, not, as in his Imperial
verses, by snapshots of actual description;
the “Chant-Pagan” of the Englander who
knows of England because he has seen
Africa: —
Me that 'ave been what I’ve been,...
'Oiv can I ever take on
With awful old England again,
An’ ’ouses both sides of the street,
An’ 'edges two sides of the lane,
An’ the parson an’ gentry between,
An’ touchin’ my ’at when we meet —
Me that ’ave been what I’ve been?
Me that 'ave rode through the dark....
With only the stars for my mark,
An’ only the night for my friend,
An r *th ings runnin’ off as you pass,
An’ things jumpin’ up in the grass,
An’ the silence, the shine an’ the sise
Of the ’igh, unexpressible skies...*
I am talcin’ some letters almost
As much as a mile, to the post,
An’ “mind you come back with the change!
Me!....
I will arise an’ get ’ence;
I will trek South and make sure
If it’s only my fancy or not
That the sunshine of England is pale,
An’ the breezes of England are stale,
An’ there’s somethin’ gon -e small with the
10t....
The rest is pretentious vacuity, dull phras-
ing, silly refrain, clever jingle, Bret Harte,
rimed philosophy, “Bellerive,” or picture
postcards of the Empire. All this is often
interesting, “but is it art?” But there is
enough of genuine poetry of the popular sort
to make quite a nice little volume of re-
membersome verse.
Chesterton
G. K. Chesterton continues to repeat him-
self in A Miscellany of Men (Methuen, per
George R#bertsom, Sydney). This b«»k can-
sists of a selection «f those tabloid essays in
paradox contributed by him t© the London
press; and though there are good things in
the book, the topical references “date his
opinions to© much. Anyone who attempts
to consume the whole book at one sitting
will assuredly get appendicitis of the
epigram or a compound fracture of the para-
dox ; and yet, certainly, taken homee©-
pathically, G.K.C. is stimulating. Thus:
“All our controversies are confused by cer-
tain kinds of phrases which are net merely
untrue, hut were always unmeaning. We
recognise them wherever a man talks of ‘the
survival of the fittest,’ meaning only the sur-
vival of the survivors; or wherever a man
says that the rich ‘have a stake in the coun-
try,’ as if the poor could not suffer from mis-
government ©r military defeat;.... or when
a man talks about ‘government by the wise
few,’ as if they could be picked out by their
pantaloons. ‘The wise few’ must mean either
the few whom the foolish think wise or the
very foolish wh© think themselves wise....
Den’t say that Primitive Man knocked down
a woman with a club and carried her away.
Why @n earth should he? Does the male
sparrow knock down the female sparrow’ with
a twig? Does the male giraffe knock down
the female giraffe with a palm tree? Why
should the male have had to use violence at
any time to make the female a female?” And
his suggested essay on the theory, “Are the
Japs Cats?*’ is exquisite fooling, with an ab-
surd substratum of fact. “Japanese pictures
were really rather like pictures made by cats.
They were full sf a feathery softness and of
sudden and spirited scratches. If anyone
will wander in some gallery fortunate enough
to have a fine collection of these slight water-
color sketches on rice paper, he will observe
many elements in them which a fanciful per-
son might consider feline. There is, for in-
stance, that odd enjoyment of the tops of
trees; those airy traceries of forks and fad-
ing twigs, up which certainly no artist, but
only a cat, could climb. There is that elvish
love of the full moon, as large and lucid as
a Chinese lantern, hung in those tenuous
branches. That moon is so large and lumi-
nous that one can imagine a hundred cats
howling under it. Then there is the. exhaus-
tive treatment of the anatomy of birds and
fish; subjects in which cats are said to be
interested. Then there is the slanting, cat-
like eye ef all these Eastern gods and men—
but this is getting altogether too coincident.
... .It is enough for me to say here that in
this small respect Japs affect me like cats.
I mean that I love them.... But, as for lik-
ing them, as one likes a dog—that is quite
another* matter. That would mean trusting
them.”
The name of William Caine (no connec-
tion of Hall) may now be added to the
select list of worth-while English novelists.
William Caine is the author of a long list
of just-so or better novels, including Boom,
The Devil in Solution, and Save Us from
Our Friends; but Hoffman’s Chance (Lane,
per Dymock’s Library, Sydney) places him
well up among the winners. This is the
story of a musician who wrote a comic
opora, and of the vicissitudes that befell
that ambitious composition. Hoffman is a
genius who, equipped with a capacity to
write tuneful music, is out to capture the
public. Hoffman knows himself that his
stuff is not classic, but it is better than any
of the allged music in the modern musical
play. His librettist is a crafty amateur pre-
pared to put up the cash for its production in
London; and the whole interesting business
of composition, casting,' rehearsal and man-
agement of the comic opera is told with con-
vincing realism. In the end the production
is ruined by the infatuation of the librettist
for a stage-struck girl, whose “thinking”
part is gradually built up—to the ruin of
Hoffman’s music—until she. becomes leading
lady. All the people in this story are real
people, Hoffman most of all. It is a brave
bit of writing, packed with shrewd observa-
tion of character, and full of dramatic in-
terest. Willian Caine’s next work will
surely be worth reading.
The White Slave Traffic in Bnglaai
forms the theme of a remarkable Bevel,
Where Are You Going Tof (Heine-
mann), by Elizabeth Robins, author of that
powerful book, The Magnetic North. This
ugly business, as it is carried on in the
United States, was trenchantly exposed by
R. Kauffman in his serious and sincere
novel, Daughters of Ishmael; and Elizabeth
Robins’ indictment of the woman-traffic in
London is equally sincere and serious. But
the styles are far apart. Daughters of lah-
mael was in the nature of a complete sum-
mary of the evidence, set forth with authen-
ticated detail, and ending in a climax ®f
horror: a tract made literature. Where Are
You Going To? treats but one side of the
hideous question. It is a story, told with a
simplicity that recalls the method of Marie
Claire, of tw® sheltered girls brought up in
complete ignorance of the wickedness of the
world, who, living their secluded, trivial lives
in the calm of a countryside, are suddenly
precipitated into immoral surroundings in
London. The girls are about to spend a few
months in London with their aunt, who, hav-
ing quarrelled with their mother, is person-
ally unknown to them. A French dress-
maker, brought down from London to make
their dresses, sees in the poverty and inex-
perience of the tw® girls a chance to kidnap
them for immoral purposes. Thus baldly
set eut, the plot seems wildly improbable;
but the art of the author makes this thing
more than credible. The girls are met at
the station by a woman whom they take te
be their aunt, from her resemblance to the
only portrait they had possessed of her. The
French dressmaker had stolen the portrait;
and the pretended aunt is the keeper of what
one of the habitues of the place describes as
“one of the mest infamous houses in
Europe.” Hither the two innocent girls are
brought, and so strange is the demeanor of
the other occupants that at first the girls
think they are in an insane asylum. En-
lightenment comes to the elder of the girls
threugh one of the men, who, out of pity
for her plight, manages, in a scene tense with
tragedy, to enable her to escape. The girl lies
to the address of her aunt, whose maid had
come to® late to meet them at the train; and
mad with fear for the fate of her pretty
and still more innocent sister left in that
house, applies to the police for her rescue.
But the girl does not know the location of
the house; and the police, though admitting
the possession ©f a list of such places, are
unable to break through the circumlocutions
of routine in setting about their task. The
point Elizabeth Robins dreadfully reiter-
ates is that the police know all about the
traffic. yes, and every man knows abeut
it, and yet there the hideous thing still is.
Ultimately two men friends of hers succeed
in locating the house, and enter it with the
police, only to find that the girl has been
spirited away. She is traced to a similar
establishment in Paris, and there the clue is
lost. The book closes with the elder sister’s
dreadful hope that the younger girl is dead.
Whore Are You Going To? is a woman’s
indictment of the male civilisation that per-
mits this horror. It is written with pellucid
sincerity and with great art.
Three new half-crown volumes of Heine-
mann’s superbly illustrated series of “Great
Engravers” are devoted respectively to Mar-
cantomo and Italian Engravers and Etchers
of the 16th Century, Rembrandt —a feast
of art, and Hans Holbein the Younger, in-
cluding his Wowseristic “Dance of Death.”
This series, edited by Arthur M. Hind,
should be in the hands of every student of
art.
A series of little essays that display
all the charm and shrewdness and
humor of Arnold Bennett, a series,
too, that has been extraordinarily popu-
lar, includes How to live on 2j Hours
a Day (reviewed last year on this page),
The Human Machine, Literary Taste and
How to Form. It, Mental Efficiency, and The
Feast of St. Friend (Hodder and Stoughton,
per Angus and Robertson, Sydney). These
are just the kind of books to pack in the
kit-bag or the bulging dress-basket for the
holidays—wholesome common sense, sweet-
ened with genial humor.
Despite Ms creator’s elaborate preface,
one refuses to believe in Bill, of Bill’s Idees,
by A. G. Stephens (N.S.W. Bookstall Co.,
Sydney, Is.). Refuses to believe, that is,
that there is in existence one Bill who has
the. idees his author credits to him. The
idees are too patently literary idees, though
they are expressed with a quaint, rather
than a robust humor, with wit that has
im it no genial humanity. But Bill, though
there is too much Bill, was worth the creat-
ing. The chapter on Yere Foster as an
author is pleasant fooling, but several of the
incidents of Bill’s career are quite, and per-
haps deliberately, preposterous. When, how-
ever, Bill, or his author, proceeds to re-
write a chapter of Steele Rudd, the parody
is not sufficiently clever to justify the paro-
dist’s “cheek.” The hand that pulls the
strings of his mannikin is too plainly shown
in Bill’s knowledge and interest in the
psychology of the female of oq.r species. The
hopeful thing about Bill’s Idees is the
marked improvement in story writing shown
by the author since the publication of his
The Pearl and the Octopus.

Asset and Outcast, Too.
It looks as though the sacred liberty of the
subject to do as he, or she, darned well
pleases will have to be interfered with some
more if this country is to achieve the fresh
population it so badly needs. A measure to
Prevent People regarding Children as
Pariahs would be a splendid thing if it were
but practicable. As it isn’t, a similar end
must be attained by some other means. All
over the settled spaces of this vast popula-
tion-famished continent, the disquieting and
likewise scandalous spectacle is offered of
children, both locally-produced and imported,
being barred like discolored aliens. Their
presence is strenuously discouraged in flats
and boarding houses, and those in search of
furnished residences have their hopes of con-
necting with these rare prizes lessened by 50
per cent, if they are afflicted with an infant
or two. A chiidless married couple has the
best of chances to get emplovment. The
married folk who have eschewed the theories
of the late Malthus, and done their duty by
the State are turned down wherever they
seek a job “in service.” .The other day the
S. M. Herald printed this :
Some publicity has been given during the last
few days to the plight of an unassisted immigrant
who finds that, though both he and his wife are
seemingly fully qualified for country work, they are
unable to obtain employment owing to the possession
of two or three children.
It is a subject on which the a.ged paper is
competent to speak with the fullest autho-
rity, seeing that for years it has been pub-
lishing advts. calling for the services of per-
sons “without encumbrances.” What a
flamingly anomalous idiocy it is that while
the Commonwealth is paying out hundreds
of thousands per annum in maternity grants;
while all the States are fighting like tigers, to
secure immigrants and supporting expensive
staffs of officials to that end ; while we are
preparing feverishly to cope with the inva-
der, and while it is common knowledge that
we have but an outside show against him
unless our population is doubled within the
next decade —what insanity it is that under
all these circumstances a section of the people
should be allowed to selfishly stand in the
road of the best sort of potential addition to
the nation’s strength. Abortionists are
severely punished on the rare occasions when
convictions are got acainst them. Drugs and
other means of limiting the birth-rate are
seized by Customs officers and destroyed, if
attempts are made to import them, and their
manufacture locally is forbidden. The law
refuses to recognise contracts in restraint of
marriage. In every way the community
shows that, so far as the family is concerned,
it is all on the side of the big battalions.
The question, then, arises: Should individu-
als be permitted to discourage a tendency
which amounts to a valuable form of national
insui-ance? If the common sense and decent
feeling of Australia decide that they should
not it will presently be a criminal offence for
a newspaper to publish a “no encumbrance”
advt. Hotels which jib at children or em-
ployees with children will be liable to lose
their licenses. Up-country employers . of
labor who discriminate unfavorably against
married folks with “encumbrances” will be
fined, and on a sufficient number of convic-
tions being recorded against them, impris-
oned. Only by such means will the indigen-
ous child sret a fair deal. Considering the
tremendous importance of the matter, it is
worth going to even violent extremes to en-
sure that this priceless type of citizen should
get the deal in question.
—
Aldridge Evelyn writes from the other
side: —
Of all the alleged cures for phthisis, radio-active
menthol and iodine, pneumon-san, I.K. and the like
the most hopeful would seem to be the one lately
announced by Dr. Friedmann, of Berlin. It was
Koch’s dictum that when a non-virulent tubercle
bacillus could be obtained, the problem of curintr
consumption was solved. This Friedmann claims to
have done with the assistance of the ordinary turtle
of commerce, which, he has found, will, so to speak
draw the sting from any tubercle bacillus injected
into its carcase, while still permitting the germ to
live. The claim is that one injection of the serum
thus obtained will turn a dying man into a potential
football in six weeks’ time. Friedmann points to
900 cures in the same number of cases. But he re-
fuses to send his serum away ; he is disinclined even
to have it analysed in a laboratory ; but he will wel-
come any medical man to his clinics, and explain all
there is to know.
While the Berlin faculty strongly denounces Fried-
mann’s secretive methods, it allows that he has had
extraordinary success with bone and joint cases of
tubercle; but for phthisis it returns a verdict of “not
proven.” For his part, Friedmann strenuously denies
that his cure is one jot less effective for lungs than it
is for bones.
The way of the business is on parallel lines with
Nature’s mode of combating pneumonia. Therein,
the patient gradually gets worse until the eighth day’
when his temperature drops with a wump, and if he
has luck he recovers rapidly, the reason being that
pneumo-cocci in the end destroy themselves in
their own toxins. The live non-virulent tubercle
bacilli are injected by Friedmann. Being non-viru-
lent, the patient’s powers of resistance refuse to take
any hostile notice of them, and they increase within
their complacent host at the rate of millions, until,
as in the case of the pneumo-coccus, Nemesis overtakes
them in the shape of their own antigans, and they
perish miserably, taking with them—which is, of
course, the gist of the whole happening—the virulent
germs already in the system.
“Karee” on the Australian horse and
horseman: —
I think “Cavalryman’s” view of the case (B.
, 6/2/T3) is a bit too blue; and, being a foreigner, I
have no racial prejudice in favor of the Australian.
I think I can take, however, a dispassionate view,
after active service in cavalry regiments under four
flags, following experience of all kinds of horses and
horsemen in Europe. With that knowledge, my
opinion of the bush Australian horse and horseman
is very high. My personal knowledge of the country
certainly is small except so far as Queensland is
concerned. Here the bursting-up of big estates by
means of the land tax is practically non-existent;
and though the bosses use motors, they as well as the
“hands” talk horse when it’s not cattle, and on most
stations jumping is a Sunday sport, second only to
’roo-hunting. The average horse met with on most
outback runs would unseat the best European mili-
tary riders of my acquaintance. He is, however,
hardy and a good doer, like his rider, and most of
them stand shooting from the saddle at a gallop. I
grant that most Australians are bad grooms ; never-
theless the samples submitted to Kitchener (and mili-
tia horses at that) won his approval. That old war-
dog knew better than to try to follow the men of the
13th—recruits of four months’ training—when at the
Lytton Camp they charged through thick brush, down
a steep stony range, swam a creek, and then jumped
the two railway fences to attack a retreating infantry
column. He said frankly he did not think “home”
troops could face that sort of country. There are
plenty of horse-breeding stations up here, I mean of
good stock, which could supply the material re-
quired, if encouraged at all. Unfortunately, the
Indian buyers get the best of them.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Loveliness.
Mrs. Flora Drummond, when interviewed regard
ing the suffragettes’ latest outrage, exclaimed, “How
perfectly lovely of them! I am proud of suet
women!”
Winston, the Joss of the Home Affairs,
Had sate him down to dine;
The limelight gilded all the chairs
And rippled in the wine.
And while he thought, “How great am IV’
A cat that long days since did die
Came through the window of the room.
And struck him with the smell of doom.
And Flora said:
“How well it’s dead !
How proud and good and womanly!
How nobly, highly humanly!
How quite perfectly lovely !”
The Duke of Never-tax-the-Land
Stood at the holy font.
His ducal heir (brought up by hand.
As is the ducal wont)
Was in the bishop’s holy arms,
Which should be free from low alarms,
But —interrupted his “A-men”
By sulphuretted hydrogen.
Said Flo aloud:
“It does us proud.
How splendid and how womanly !
How nobly, highly humanly!
How quite perfectly lovely!”
A poet posted Christmas Cards
Unto his second best girl;
The kind beloved of youthful bards,
With springs and brooks that purl
Of loving each for all their lives —-
But girls who never would be wives
Left little use for postman’s knocks
By pouring vitriol in the box
And Flo exclaimed:
“We can’t be blamed.
So proud and good and womanly!
So nobly, highly humanly!
So quite perfectly lovely !”
The Royal Lodge of Rechabites
Had met to have a spree,
On toast and water that delights
To shriek its victory
Against the demon alcohol —
W T hen, Lo! a sound of “Fol-de-rol”
Came from the High and Worthy Grand!
He had a rum cup in his hand !
And Flo said : “Ho !
We hurt them so.
The abstinence of fifty years
We killed with surreptitious beers,
How quite perfectly lovely!”
A bishop browsed within his stall,
At peace with all, was he ;
The sunlight on the minster wall
He saw with chastened glee.
When suffragettes —all skin and grief
Despairing ever of relief —
Stood by envy of his somb-
Re peace —and ’neath him placed a bomb.
And Flora’s wish
Was: “Dash the bish-
On. Proud and good and womanly
We fired the bomb so humanly,
And quite perfectly lovely.”
The foolish thing requires no vote,
But infants by the score,
And some poor chap on whom to dote
Until she dotes no more.
The scheme’s to take the criminal —
No matter how he squirm or squall —
No matter how he fume and fret—
And wed him to a suffragette.
And Flora will
Be first to kill.
So pure and good and womanly,
So highly, nobly humanly !
And quite perfectly lovely.”
N.S.W.
R. BEDFORD.
The Web.
The witch who sits weaving behind the
clouds. , , ,
Spinning bride veils or thridding shrouds,
Leaned one day from her misty throne,
Saw where your life-reel lay alone.
Mischief brewing, the grey auld wife
Caught at the threads that make my life,
And in and out with her shining pins
There, where the scarlet splash begins
On the drab of her tapestry,
Mocking, knitted your life to me!
It is all forgotten by you to-day,
And the ravelled ends drift another way ;
Yet, at times, when the wind is in the skies,
And the silky web of the weaver flies,
I can see ablaze on its sober greys
The scarlet patch of those other days.
QUEEN SLAND.
M. FORREST.
Lay of a Lost Flapper.
But your young girls mature too early.
—Complaint lodged by an English matron.
Phyllis one short year ago
Was a flapper slender ;
Always had a schoolboy beau
Acting as a tender ;
Hair bows indicated that
She went not to dances,
And a simple muslin hat
Shadowed all her glances.
Phyllis one short year ago
Hadn’t got a “Agger.”
But the climate here ! You know!
Now her bosom’s bigger.
Many matrons haven’t got
Half what she possesses;
And her father pays a lot
For her hats and dresses.
Phyllis wears her hair up now
(She’s been duly “outed ).
Full-grown suitors to her bow;
All the boys are flouted.
There’s a slim young schoolgirl ma
In this ripe relation;
But you still detect the kid
In her conversation.
N.S.W.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.

Quarterly.. . . .. . . 0 7 0
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings Sterling you can have The Bulletin
;posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some-
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements.’ etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. ‘‘The Editor, The Bulletin/’ is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters arc liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded with-
out stamps for return it will be understood that the
copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Personal
applications for return of MS. cannot receive atten-
tion. The Bulletin desires, further, those sending
MS. to distinctly understand that it is not respon-
sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash, of
sufficient merit and interest and adapted for direct
reproduction, or any interesting photograph illus-
trative of a current event, will be paid for same on
acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea, but
unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MARCH 6, 1913.
THE LONG DRAGGED-OUT WAR.
Amid frost and snow, ar.d bitter poverty,
and cruel slaughter, the Balkan Confeder-
ates keep shoving the Unspeakable Turk
back towards Asia. What horrors they have
endured in the struggle it is almost impos-
posible to imagine, and it is a certificate to
the bad character of the Turk that even the
weakest and poorest of tha Confederate
nations regards these horrors as well worth
while if only the Asiatic goes out of Europe
at the finish. The European races which
have endured Asiatic rule for four or five
centuries have lost all the beautiful illu-
sions which gild the horizon of Bruce
Smith when he discusses the advisability
of settling our colored brother in the North-
ern Territory. They know Abdul and Mo-
hammed and Suleiman and all their clan
—not as they appear when held down to a
certain standard of political decency by
European rule, nor even . as they appear
when dealing only with their fellow country-
men and compatriots, but in all the mad
truculence which characterises them when
they are on top of a mentally superior race,
and the chance comes their way to jump on
the Christian with both feet. Then the posi-
tion suggests Sambo or Quasiiee or Jack
Johnson, suddenly raised to the position of
Dictator over a white people, and drunk with
the pride of his unwonted authority. In
fact, it is a worse position, for Jack
Johnson, as Dictator in England or Aus-
tralia, would only be differentiated from his
subjects by his descent, his ignorance, his child-
ish vanity and the glare of his gold teeth;
while in addition to these elements a great
gulf of religious fanaticism separates the
Turk from his European subjects. The
four little nations which are striving to drive
the Ottoman off the heritage of Japheth have
all had lone experience of him. Three of
them have been his subjects, and the fourth
has beggared itself for centuries in order to
avoid becoming his subject, and has spent its
lunch money on rifles and sold its boots to
buy ammunition. So they speak with some
authority on the subject of their old acquaint-
ance Abdul.
* * * *
The odds, on paper at all events, were
vastly against the Confederates from the
first. Compared with the 24,000,000 or more
Turkish subjects, all under one flag and one
Government, they mustered a mere 10,000,000
people. These were embodied in four
detached groups, and lived under four separ-
ate Governments, and they spoke at least
three different languages. They even fought
separately, save when, now and again, one
army lent a division or two to prop up the
cause of a hard-pressed ally. Excepting the
Montenegrins they hadn’t even the prestige
of old-time victory over their fezzed foe to
support them against the drawbacks of in-
ferior numbers, divided ambitions, and divi-
ded commands. Only one of them —Greece
- —had anything dimly resembling a fleet, and
the reason why that dim resemblance was
able to keep the sea was that it in-
cluded a big ironclad, which a patri-
otic millionaire had bought for his country,
instead of endowing a Conservatorium of
Music or a Dogs’ Home. And money was
desperately short —in fact, everything was
short except courage and boundless energy.
The Bulgarians robbed the cradle and the
grave, calling out every able-bodied male
between 14 and 00, save such as were abso-
lutely required to keep the railways working,
and thus feed the army, or to run such a
skeleton of government as had to be main-
tained. The Greeks, who are liable to 36
years’ military service, partly in the army
and partly in the reserves, came out in the
same wholesale fashion. So did the Monte-
negrins, who do military service for 45 years,
from the age of 18 to that of 63—two years
as recruits, 33 years as citizen soldiers in
steady training, and 10 years as reservists.
So did the Servians, whose term of liability
for military service is 32 years, but because
the Servian reservist lays aside his gun at
the age of 50 he is rather looked down upon
by the grev-bearded veterans of some of the
neighboring kingdoms. It was no army of
carelessly drilled and semi-mutinous school-
kids that the Confederates turned out. When
the chance came to wipe the 500-year-old
blot of Asia off the face of Europe they
staked everything on the chance. Banks and
insurance offices, shops and factories and
warehouses were closed; gas and electric
light were cut off; tram-cars and omnibuses
and cabs stopped; so did trains, except such
as were absolutely necessary; farms were
abandoned, and the cattle and horses were
seized for military service and paid for in
Government P.N.’s. Many of the courts of
justice were closed, and offenders, tried and
untried, went into the ranks, where
they and the Judge and the jury fought toge-
ther. Most of the police went to the front,
and doddering old inhabitants, too feeble for
the march and the firing line, took their
places. Churches, schools and colleges left
off duty because priests and professors were
toiling at the guns. Women of the richer
class, accustomed to a life of luxury, went
out gathering wood to cook their own meals
because, in the general dislocation of things,
the fuel supplies often gave out. The birth-
rate was abandoned, and the dead were
buried by whoever could find time —and a
shovel that the army hadn’t carried away. In
the Confederate countries —and especially in
Bulgaria and Montenegro—almost the ■whole
machine of civilisation stopped except, in so
far as children, women, old men and cripples
could keep the wheels turning. And because
of the neglected fields there is at least a year
of starvation ahead when the war is over.
* * * *
Yet with a full knowledge of the odds;
with a full knowledge of the horrors ahead,
for each one of the four States had fought
for its life against the Asiatic over and over
again; and with every reason to believe that
failure might mean political suicide, the
Confederates went into the game with a
light heart. It is not meant that they went
to war as a jest, but their hearts were light
because they felt that their cause was good,
and so long as they did their uttermost it
seemed impossible that Providence would
wholly desert them. It was no case of a
strong military tyrant driving his helpless
subjects into (lie field to gratify his own
ambition, but : a general uprising of the
people. Of the four Confederate sovereigns
none save the old grandfather-king of Mon-
tenegro. can be called either a strong man or
a notable soldier. Bulgaria, Montenegro
and Greece have each a Parliament of one
Chamber, elected on the principle of one-
man-one-vote. Servia has a Parliament
elected by all male Servians aged over 21
who nay about 13s. a year in taxes. These
strongly democratic Legislatures cheerfully
subject themselves and their constituents to
as much as 45 years soldiering simply be-
cause they know the Asiatic as no other
white race knows him. And the four
nations took their lives in their hands when
the time came because any chance seemed
worth taking to get rid of the old Asiatic
tyrant. Over 1100 years ago Mahomet
went to the mountain because the mountain
wouldn’t come to him. When the Montene-
grin army led the way at the beginning of
hostilities the position was reversed —the
Black Mountain went at Mahomet. And
when the Confederates started they kept up
the reputation of Christendom remarkably
well. A few years ago the writer met a
pleasant old Bulgarian lady who, as a girl,
had been ravished, along with every other
presentable girl, in the stupendous Turkish
massacres of Batak and thereabouts. She
made one statement that it was not easy to
forget. “No Bulgarian woman,” she said,
“will be otherwise than glad to lose her
father, her husband, her brother or her son,
so long as he dies in a successful battle
against the Turk.” It was in that spirit
that the Confederates shouldered the gun
of ages and went out to look for Abdul the
Damned.
* * * *
And that person, as it presently appeared,
wasn’t quite the old Abdul though he was
as much Damned as ever. There had been
a revolution in Turkey. Sultan Abdul
Hamid had gone into prison after 30 years
of muddled tyranny, and his brother, Mo-
hammed V., after 30 years spent dreaming
and drinking in a gilded gaol, had come out
blinking into the sunlight of affairs to potter
round on the throne in his old age. There
was a Young Turk party, and a committee
of Union and Progress, and an alleged Par-
liament, which was controlled by the Com-
mittee. And there was much talk of free-
dom and the equality of races. There were,
of course, the usual massacres, dirt, swindles
and incompetence, but where there had been
one big tyrant there were now 500 small
ones. The Christian, who had formerly been
kept out of Jthe Turkish army, was now
shoved into it on the ground that, under the
reformed order of things, he was a man and
a brother, and he was copiously kicked by
his Mohammedan comrades on the ground
that he was the same old “infidel.” The
Ottoman army struggled to the front,
haunted by a suspicion that the ancient bigo-
try was getting out of repair, and bossed by
too many masters. The new and much-
kicked Christian soldiers (still described
as “cattle”) were put in front to be
killed first, and they naturally ran
away to the best of their ability. The besc
general that the Ottomans possessed was
murdered in a twopenny revolt, headed by
the leading “reformer,” for not being better
than he was. 'Then the leading reformer led
an army on to the Gallipoli peninsula, and
being defeated and bottled up he fled in an
open boat, leading his troops to hunt for
shellfish for a living. And amid all this
decay and failure and misery the Turkish
population of Thrace was steadily clearing
out in its wooden ox-waggon back to Asia.
It came from Asia proudly in a wooden ox-
waggon half. a thousand years ago, and
having no progressive instincts it is leaving
miserably in the same vehicle. But whereas
it came along the old solid Roman-Greek
roads it now leaves per medium of the Turk-
ish slush tracks. It is roughly 500 years
since the Asiatic Ottomah first got his hold
on Europe. He got 'if because the little
nations of the south-east devoted themselves
too much to lopal quarrels and State
Frights. He consolidated his powers because
Demetrius Brucesmithopoulus of Con-
stantinople failed to see that only universal
military service could save him when he
collided with a furious Asiatic nation in
which every man was a soldier. And now
the Ottoman is going out because the little
Christian States have learned, by centuries
of blood and tears and shame and rapine
and torture and misery, that there is no sacri-
fice, however 'great, that isn’t worth making,
to keep the hoof of Shem off the neck of
Japheth.
* * * *
When the-story of the Balkan war is
written in full, it will probably rank among
the great struggles of the world’s history.
So, far, howfever, it is only known in a
sketchy sort «of way. The war correspond-
ent is at a discount these days. When he
was content to send his news slowly and
laboriously by post he was a welcome addi-
tion to the forces, but the telegraph and the
motor-car and his own insensate haste have
damaged him. A time came when the cor-
respondent attached to General Smith’s
army would fly madly to the telegraph office
and advise hfs paper in some big European
capital that Smith was concfentrating at an
amazing rate,' and that it was plain that he
intended to abandon his defensive policy and
launch an overwhelming force at the widely
dispersed arm*y of General Brown. Then the
paper would get out a red-hot special edition,
and 10 rainttes after it was out, secret
agents would* be making the wires red-hot
with full information to General Brown of
Smith’s doings and purposes. And if Brown
was worth his salt as a fighting man he
promptly gathered together every soldier
within reach, and threw his army at the
throat of Smith before the latter had time
to develop his attack. In this way an excess
of efficiency has killed the war correspond-
ent. He was fairly dead in the Russo-Japan-
ese disturbance. In the great scrap between
the Confederates and the Unspeakable, he has
been quite dead, save for such news as he
could gather ’from the wounded, from the
men who had'run away, from the clatter of
alarmed citizens, and from civil and military
persons who fcpndescended to talk to him.
Even the list* of killed and •wounded is un-
procurable, though there is reason to sus-
pect that the slaughter has been something
incredible. The assurance that the Unspeak-
able is burst depends largely on the fact that
he has bowed jhis lofty fez in conference, and
offered to giye up nearly all his ill-gotten
gains in Europe save Constantinople. And
there is every reason to suppose that that
city will go in the next war, and then the
Asiatic rule in Europe will be finished.
MAORILAND'S WANDERING LABOR
PARTY.
Labor in Maoriland is still floundering in
the Wilderness at the heels of any old
blithering Moses that happens to be out of
a job, or fired with enthusiastic ignorance,
or premature fanaticism, or anything but
the qualifications far the business of pilot-
ing the party'to the Promised Land. Up to
a few days ago Labor had been for some
time split into two main camps—one under
the red flag of the UedeTation of Labor, and
the other under the banner of the United
Labor Party, which was practically the old
Trades and Labor Councils’ party. The
former had half a dozen Moseses to lead it,
and they danced before the Lord uttering
weird war-cries, such as :
Down with defence. We mierht as well be ex-
ploited by a Chow as a Maorilander.
To Hell with agreements;
World’s wealth for world’s workers.
Blank arbitration ! Let’s have the strike!
We want Revolutionary Industrial Unionism.
The other show was more sane and stood
for Conciliation and Arbitration, and de-
fence, and so on. These two factions loved
each other with that affection which Cain
bore for the late lamented Abel. The
leader of the Unity party, “Professor” W.
T. Mills (who blew along from Milwau-
kee, U.S.A.), was gently reprimanded as “a
dirty hireling of the capitalist class” , a
;> wastei ‘ ; whi ] e associated S
lnm, or who agreed with his scheme of ! ■
tical salvation, were ticketed as “fakirs oni
frauds.” dna
Brotherly love continued on these i;„
until the echoes of the Federation’s recern
industrial crash at Wailii had partly died
away. The Red Rag brotherhood then
knocked off remodelling the social world fn
make provision for its mere existence in the
sphere of things. So it called a conference
and invited the “fakirs and frauds,” inelud
ing the alleged “dirty hireling of a cani
talist press” (Professor Mills had been
conducting a Labor page in each issue of
the Maoriland Times, published at Welling-
ton, but it was blown out with great sud-
denness about this time). Mills with Ed
ward Tregear (formerly secretary of the
Labor Department) appeared to represent
the Trades and Labor Councils, and thev
were practically swallowed whole. They
pledged their followers to an alliance with
or, rather, an absorption into, the Federa-
tion, despite that section’s staggering re-
cord for ineptitude and blundering. The pre-
amble of the agreement sets out that
Between these two classes (employers and em-
ployed) a struggle must go on until the workers of
the world take possession of the earth and the
machinery of production, and abolish the wage
system....lt is the historic mission of the working
class to do away with capitalism.
And other idealistic flam from the text-
books of the Revolutionary Socialists, Wor-
kers of the World and similar excitable per-
sons who pursue the Millennium with a salt-
cellar in one hand and a halter in the other.
Then in order to prove to the Unity fly that
it was fairly inside the Federation spider's
parlor, as to the question of defending the
white blood of Maoriland from contamina-
tion, its women from rapine, and its sons
from slaughter, it was decided: —
That this conference is of opinion that militarism
has always been against the interests of the workers,
and therefore condemns it, and is prepared to recom-
mend to all unions the hardy and valiant resolution,
calling a general strike on a declaration of war by
any of the Great Powers. And we declare our deter-
mined hostility to the form of conscription placed
on the M.L. Statute Book.
As regards Conciliation and Arbitration, it
was decided by the representatives of those
unions that had formerly been in favor of
that way of settling disputes to let partly
slide the principle so laboriously gained and
so long stood by. The advantages of Maori-
land’s Conciliation and Arbitration Act have
been great, even if in later years many of
the awards of Judge Sim have given griev-
ous dissatisfaction. Anyhow, it has a
superb record with a brass band accompani-
ment and blue flare illumination when set
alongside the “general strike” scheme of the
tatterdemalion brigade that made such a
monumental mess of running a strike at
Waihi.
* * * *
Instead of dodging the strike foolery the
Unity representatives were cajoled into
grasping it with one hand while retaining
some sort of a hold on Arbitration with the
other. Each union is left to its own choice,
as to registration, although, in the event of
non-registration, none shall involve another
in a strike without a consultation which
may include the national executive. If the
crop of recriminations and ill-feeling and re-
pudiation calculated to arise from that
scheme gets a fair season the Employers’
Federation will be able to recall its appeal
(issued the other day) for funds, “to combat
Socialism, syndicalism and anarchy.” All
the combat necessary will be provided by
the parties in the Optional Omnibus. It is
true that neither the word of the itinerant
“professor from Milwaukee.” W. T. Mills,
nor that ageing sentimentalist, Edward Tre-
gear, binds the agreement arrived at. Their
action has yet to be ratified (or discredited)
and the decision forwarded to another gen-
eral conference in July. Meanwhile, in
case any man has any doubts about the
national value of the work already done, let
him be told that (1) Robert . Semple. the
Red Federation’s chief organiser, declares
that “it proclaims for the abolition of the
waster class” ; Professor Mills opines the
time for action has arrived”; and (o)
Harry Holland is satisfied the conference
meant “the deatli-knell of the Massey Gov-
ernment.” Body of a dog, camerados, we
are nearly there!
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Giving the Enemy a Leg-up.
The leaders and supporters of Labor in Aus-
tralia require one stock prayer which may,
with all justification, be breathed morning,
noon and night and between drinks: Gue
us patience to deal with some almighty fools.
The which remark is induced by last weeks
strike of the gas workers in Sydney. Galiea
by a decision of Justice Heydon in the Ar-
bitration Court on Thursday which pre-
vented the Union getting a shilling a day ove
and above their previous bargain the me.
without warning to anybody skied their tool
and left the city and suburbs in semi dark-
ness and with barely enough heating powe
to sterilise milk for children and invalids, r
say nothing of the old man’s morning cnop.
On the word of the secretary of the Gas E
ployees Union: — ~
When the assistant secretary convened a
meeting for two o’clock on Saturday (nearly two» .
after the strike had commenced) he had ,10 . 1(1 ® a
ever tiiat the men would be all out. His object .
have a discussion on the judgment delivered by J
Hkydon. But he could be no more surprised t
was. -11 1
It is a weird admission to make, and wil ®
seized and dished up to the public in
entire radiance by the Joe Cooks and
Bruce Smiths and Gregory W ades a
Denhams and all the rest of the Tory gak
that never ceases to prophecy that Ha
rule means Chaos and Irresponsibility
Repudiation of Authority. Occasionally
prophetic pace of the enemy dies now
want of fuel to keep up steam, and Lab
leaders toil overtime to make up
tug back unions which have drifted *
from public confidence. Just now, wit
party’s great double fight for its retur
power in the Australian Parliament am
acceptance of the Referendum coming •
few more effective political waddies
have been placed in the weaponry o
other side than this latest tactless disp .
disregard for the first principle of Unioniw -
If union rules and regulations are .
paper, and elected officers may be kej


vond the confidence of the people they are
representing before the public and the em-
ployers, what in Sheol is the use of union-
ism? N( t more than a few days ago the
representatives of unions at the P.L.L. Con-
ference, sitting at Sydney, lifted up their
voices and wept and even cursed because
the N.S.W. Labor Ministry had gone on
strike against the Labor platform. Pre-
sumably the tears and language of
Sydney Gas Employees Union flowed with
the best of them, yet when an extra bob a
day is not immediately forthcoming the whole
boiling of gasmen turn and strike against
(1) their own officials; (2) the State Labor
party’s wobbly chance at the next election ;
(3) the flank of the whole Australian Labor
array. The futile excuse was put forward
the morning after the exasperated citizens
were made to bear the burden of the gas-
men’s grievance that the strike was “a spon-
taneous outbreak of indignation” against the
decision (on a point of law) of Justice
Heydon. Doubtless Heydon was one of the
least affected persons in the community.
Probably while tens of thousands of those
most sympathetic with the workers were
taking off their boots with one hand while
they held a match with the other Justice
Heydon was calmly reading the literature
of the law or the sparkle of the decanter
beneath the refulgence of electricity. The
Bulletin is not now concerned with the
question of whether the gas employees of
Sydney should be paid the extra shilling a
day. It takes it for granted that they should.
Eight shillings per diem can’t be reckoned
as quite a living wage in the great Ma City
nowadays. But when Labor owns the unions,
the Legislature, the Government, the power
to make any necessary amendment in he
Arbitration system, and the whole box of
tricks, and yet can find no better remedy
for a shilling grievance than to go on strike
against itself and its principles and the whole
community, it suggests gross futility and
incapacity. .
The Beginning of the Battle.
0,. day last week Cook and ' Sen *“”*
Mir.r.EH and Gould and aomebodyo „ p
importance, opened the Federal L.W c«
paien at Paddington, Sydney. It was * g
night within limitations the tm
the atmosphere into toam wi : st
„ ei r ploauence washed up against
great waves of loose eioquc
shore Yet after reading all the
Il'oi vocabulary, there remains a feeling of
emptiness at the pit of the mental stomach. Cer-
t titily there is much to be said against the present
Australian Government, but very little of it was
said. There was, for instance, a heap of justifiable
denunciation of the recent appointments to the
High Court Bench, but they are made, and they
can’t be unmade, and there is no evidence
that the Cook party would have made any better
appointments. Then there was a wild and silly
clamor to the effect that if the Referenda pro-
posals are carried, and certain self-governing
powers are transferred by the people from the
people in their State capacity to the same people
in their Federal capacity the deed will “rob the
people of their right of self-government.” Even
a dead ass ought to know that when both Federal
Houses are elected by plain adult suffrage, and
the people hold all the power and run the entire
show, it is sheer lunacy to talk of them being de-
prived of the right of self-government. There was
talk about the Coal Vend, and the postal vote, and
about “Federal extravagance,” which, seeing that
the Australian Government is the one non-bor-
rowing political outfit which Australia possesses
didn’t amount to so much as it might have done.
And there were cheers for the King, and obser-
vations about the sinfulness of giving so many
appointments to trade-unionists. But when a
community is asked to transfer power and office
from the old crowd to the new crowd it wants to
hear something more than the new crowd’s
opinion, or alleged opinion, about the old crowd.
That is bound to be bad, for if it was otherwise
the new crowd would have no excuse for demand-
ing the old crowd’s job. What the community
would like is a clear statement as how the new
crowd proposes to improve on the old one. For
instance, the Fisher Government hasn’t floated
loans, and it would be interesting to know if a
Cook Government would float loans or not. The
Ministry in office has wobbled miserably over the
route of the Northern Territory railway, and has
hung up the most essential public work in Aus-
tralia for fear of losing votes. What route does
the prospective Ministry that wants to be in office
propose to take? The Northern Territory land
policy of Fisher and Co. has been a dead failure so
far; the public would like some definite news of
the policy of Cook and Co., so that it might know
whether any improvement is in sight. The Cook
party has bitterly denounced Fishers tariff (which
has nothing wrong with it save its inefficiency);
his Federal bank notes policy (which also has
nothing wrong with it save lack of courage to carry
it to its logical conclusion), and his Commonwealth
Bank. What exactly does the Cook party pro-
pose to do about these three matters? Will it
lower or abolish the tariff, and call in the note
issue, and shut up the bank? These and many
more are the big affairs of the future the affairs
that may go far to make or mar the prospects of
Australia. The fact that Fisher’s plausible evil
genius, Hughes, put Bowers and Piddington on
the High Court Bench is a comparatively small
concern of the past. It is bad enough, but
it is done and can’t be undone, and the
fact that Cook disapproves of it is in the
same category, for plain business purposes, as
the fact that he disapproves of the Inquisition
or the Deluge. It is always the device of the
barren, shifty politician to denounce his oppo-
nent’s past in order to disguise the fact that he
himself hasn’t any future. But there is no room
at the top of affairs for the man who has his head
turned the wrong way, like that of the ghost cf
Michael Scott of Balvvearie, in Slieol, so that
he weeps chionically down his back over the
alleged sins that are gone. That sort of individi al
walks blindly and is always falling over the living
present. The demand is for a man who has a
clear view ahead, and a full knowledge of what
he means to do next—not merely a view of what
somebody else did last.

The persistent Hop Poon Gooey case has
cropped up once more. Minister Thomas
has been notified that Mrs. Gooey has pre-
sented Poon Gooey with a baby girl, and
the decision now is that the lady must de-
part for China two months hence which
term seems splendidly liberal when one re-
collects that she has merely to be driven on
board a boat, and that an Australian mother
of 25 children lately walked six miles, five
days after her last child was born, m order
to register its birth. But Minister Tommy
need not flatter himself that Mrs. Gooey is
done with yet. She may find herself still in-
valid when the two months are gone, and
may desire, and get, a further extension of
three months, and before that period has
elapsed she may again be in the customary
interesting condition. Besides Mrs. Gooey
there is the problem of the new baby, which,
of course, has the right to dwell in Australia
as long as it lives. Mrs. Gooey may be
professedly eager to return to Chowland but
would perish if separated from her baby,
while Mr. Gooey, who does not want to
leave the Commonwealth, may be quite un-
able to contemplate a separation from his
new infant, who cannot be deported along
with her mother. Then will arise a new
agitation about the inhumanity of separat-
ing mother and baby or about the cruelty of
parting father and child, and the man who
couldn’t say “No!” at the first asking will
be made to feel that he is either an mhimian
monster or a person specially designed by
Providence for the role of fool in his own
comic rendering of the drama of White Aus-
tralia.

In all murder cases the prisoner has the (lamninff
allegations and suggestions of a circulating library of
policemen to contend with. Everything that could
be whispered against the prisoner is bound to have
been heard by some or all of the jurymen. In this

nolice to give disrespectable persons a darned bad
time.
“Laborite” writes:—
A few days ago the N.S.W. House of Fossils was
handed out a testimonial of character:—
“I have much reason to be grateful to the honor-
able members of the Legislative Council for their
courtesy, generosity and forbearance during the time
I have been the representative of the Government.
Ready assistance has always been forthcoming to me
in the conduct of the business of the House from my
strongest political opponents. There is a personal
touch of kindness here, which finds a response in the
right place. I shall ever remember with feelings c.f
gratitude the cordial relationship which existed be-
tween the House and myself during the passing of
highly contentious legislation.”
The testimonialiser was Flowers, M.L.C., Vice-Presi-
dent of the Executive Council, and Flowers, M.L.C...
was gabbling the sort of stuff that makes a real 1
democrat sick. Nobody ever accused the of
being a boorish mob who would slay a political!
opponent with brickbats or defunct cats. Nobody
ever said they were anything but very nice, polite,,
fatherly, elderly gentlemen—just the sort of gentle-
men, in fact, who would be likely to treat the raw’
Flowers in the manner described. Consequently, his;
eulogistic remarks were merely so much superfluity,,
which the Fossils may, or may not, relish. When,
without any apparent necessity, you tell a man he is;
a gentleman you seem to infer that there was pre-
viouslv a doubt about the subject, and that isn t.
flattering. What has been charged against the House’
of Fossils is that as a legislative Chamber it is:
effete, costly, useless, non-representative and undemo-
cratic. Laborites are asking what has become oC
that leading plank in the party’s platform which:
provides for the abolition of the Sepulchre, Has
Flowers forgotten it?
Before any military person, attorney, or
other outsider is brought in to till O Calla-
ghan’s place as chief Robert of Victoria,
the Government ought to thoroughly satisfy
itself (1) that Sainsbury, the inspecting
superintendent, hasn’t got the necessary
qualifications, and (2) that nobody else in
the force owns them. Prima facie, feAiNS-
BURY seems to have a good case. He started
as mounted constable, and his qualities got
him promotion through every grade ot the
service; and, on general principles, a man
who is reckoned good enough to work on the
first 19 steps of a ladder seems worth try-
ing on the 20th —especially if the choice lies
between him and a man who hasn’t proved his
fitness for any one of the 19. Certainly the
Vic. force is nothing to be proud of or even
satisfied with; but one question is whether
the service is likely to attract better men it
the Government gives the few plums to out-
siders. And, after all, that is what the ser-
vice wants —better men ; not men who will
be satisfied with the plain Bobby’s place and
pay, but fellows with brains enough to take
the highest places when they have had the
necessary experience and the chance comes
their way.
Recently the local council decided that
Stawell (Vic.) wanted a £lO,OOO loan, £3600
of it to build a new Town Hall. Had this
been a matter of national politics, with a
McGowen party in power, the proposal
would have been dashed through amid tre-
mendous cheers, and the McGowen crowd
would have got the honor and glory, and
posterity’s share would have been the bill.
But, strange to say, the principle of the
Initiative and Referendum obtains in Vic.
municipal affairs; if a certain number of
ratepayers put up £2O and demand a refer-
endum on any matter of local administration
referendum it has to be. Public opinion in
Stawell being largely that it was not neces-
sary to pawn the place to buy a new lown
Ha'll, the £2O was put up and the matter
was submitted to the ratepayers, who vio-
lently banged the proposed I O U on the head.
Precisely why the people should have the
power in parish-pump matters to say whether-
or not money should be borrowed in their-
name, and not have the right in State or-
national questions to stop a McGowen:
gang, which it elected on a reduction-ot-
borrowing ticket, from becoming the vilest
kind of political traitor and rushing every-
thing to general smash, will take a pile or
explaining. Apparently Party Government
is good enough for the whole country but
not good enough for a fragment ot the
country.
If Joe Cook is the magnificent general the
Tory press makes him out to be, he will call
his gang together and decide what yarn it is
to tell the electors. At the present time
every member of the push is fluting a totally
different tale; and some of the yarns flatly
contradict each other. At Paddington (Syd-
ney) Senator Gould alleged that the-Refer-
enda if carried, would rob the people of
their rights of self government. On the
same platform Millen told them the powers
were not necessary and therefore should not
be granted. At Carcoar (N.S.W.) he told
the audience that it was useless to pass the
Referenda; the powers asked for would do
no good as “every power necessary for legis-
lation was already in the hands of the peopl
and the acceptance of the Referenda could
not increase those powers.” Just previous
to this Joe Cook had explained that the
passing of the Referenda would be equal to
exploding a charge of dynamite under the
Constitution. At Carcoar, Millen aso
affirmed that both State and Commonwealth
were able to deal effectively with Trusts, but
did not want to. In Victoria Irvine stated
that neither State nor Commonwealth could’
deal with Trusts under existing circum-
stances. What sort of a fool party is it
that goes out to fool the public without
having carefully hatched one fool yarn with
which to do the fooling.
Two of the reasons why things grow dearer
in Australia were expounded by recent in-
vestigation. The population of England
having doubled in the last 50 years, and the
local stock of cattle having rather declined
than increased, British buyers offer higher
and higher prices for Australian hides. There-
fore Australian buyers have to offer higher
and higher prices to prevent all the hides going
to England, and that shoves up the cost of
local hoof-apparel. Another point is that

Your intentions are all right,
And your general appearance
Is intelligent and bright;
But the question you’re discussing
Rather flicks me on the raw,
And it really doesn’t matter;
So we’ll close the subject. Haw!
Since the very first reformer
Made suggestions in the trees
All the old earth’s agitators
Meet with phrases such as these.
And it acts as brake and hobble
On the progress of mankind,
This superior aloofness
Of the static type of mind.
“Haw!” It rings throughout the ages
Since dim neolithic years,
Striving to discount the credit
Of philosophers and seers ;
And the richest, fattest mollusc
Spat it out in savage hate
When he marked his fellow’s yearning
Towards a structure vertebrate.

With an apathetic glance.
Then that simple interjection
Clothes a mass of ignorance.
“Haw ! The fellow is a boundah !
Do not heed his fuss and fret,
And the subject he alludes to
Isn’t mentioned in our set.
Friend, if you have privileges

And the claims of poorer brothers
Cause you most acute distress
When all argument has failed you

And the haughty Tory Haw !

When with calm, unswerving reason,

That abuses need redress.
Do not weakly yield to measures
That your prejudices bate, „

And that closes the debate.
Still, my friend, despite your coldness,
’Spite reactionary “Haws.

When they have a worthy cause,
And the fat and foolish mollusc
Who the vertebrates ignored,
Did not block all evolution,
So the scientists record.

Deaving molluscs far behind,
Progress ever has to reckon _

\nd the fighters in the vanguard

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At a largely-attended meeting of the General
Laborers’ Union the following motion was unani-
mously agreed to:—“That this union views with
disgust the prosecuting of boys two or three times
for one offence, the taking away of their civil
rights, and the garnisheeing of their wages, and
urges upon the citizens to do all in their power
to lessen the birth-rate until the Defence Act is
repealed.”— Maoriland news item.
Yes, they voted him a fortune and the
nation’s grateful thanks,
For he saved them like a hero and a genius
from the ranks;
Well he knew his men and led them, so his
land can still afford
To pay honest white men’s wages at the bid-
ding of a board.
Men with picks and shovels used him in the
days before the war,
Just to illustrate their arguments and stop
a flow of jaw
Re the old eternal problem: Why should
all the good things be
Kept throughout the toiling ages for the
aristocracy?”
But since lie brought home the standards
and the parks of captured guns,
Why the friend of birth and breeding from
the subject simply runs;
For Bowyangs, F.M., is Labor from his hel-
met to his heels, ,
And he plies his trade of wheelwright midst
his field-guns’ shell-scarred wheels.
He’s the idol of the workers, and they tell
you with a laugh
That a lord is proud to serve him. (He has
two upon his staff;
And they gallantly attend him when he rides
abroad in state —
Yet, off duty, he’s been overheard to call a
private “Mate”!)
He’s an asset to the workers, for he’s popu-
lar with all, ~ , ,
And before his reputation olden hatreds melt
and fall; , , , , ,
He’s the product, born and branded, ot a
once-suspected caste
Who has proved he and his people can be
faithful to the last.
So the old class hatreds vanish, and, with
Bowyangs in the van,
Goes a young, war-hardened nation welded
by a single Man !... .
What a splendid dream to usher in a most
unsober morn ! .
Why the General D —n Fools’ Union won t
allow him to he horn!

WHERE
EVERYBODY
GOES
Bull, having to a great extent killed his
own agriculture under Freetrade, had to im-
port, in 1900, 68,000,000 cwt. of wheat of
which countries outside the empire supplied
58,000,000 cwt. In 1912, things having be-
come still worse, he had to import
109,000,000 cwt. of which countries outside
the empire supplied only 50,000,000 cwt.,
because their population has so greatly in-
creased that they want more and more of
their food for their own sustenance. With
Bull in the market, offering steadily in-
creasing prices for his indispensable victual,
it is difficult to keep wheat from becoming
more costly in Australia. In another 20
years Bull will probably be buying wheat
and beef and sundries so feverishly that the
prices will shoot up to a figure not even
dreamed of to-day.
.
“Father of Fifteen” speaks a piece:—
After considering the case of a dead infant, who
had been overlain by his mother, Melbourne’s coroner
the other day delivered some hostile remarks anent
the practice of two generations sleeping in one bed.
The official’s animadversions were probably read by
a number of mothers who are not guilty of this
offence, and possibly by an odd one or two who are.
But a large number of ignorant females who did not
peruse the observations will still continue to en-
danger the life of Australia’s valuable infant. This
country annually loses about 9000 infants under the
age of one year, and 12,000 under five years. And,
having regard to the generally unsatisfactory condi-
tions under which the profession of motherhood is
practised, the loss is not surprising. We assume that
a woman is fully equipped to be a wise and efficient
mother solely by reason of her sex, just as it is
assumed that, because of the mere accident of sex,
women make economical housekeepers and more or
less bosker cooks. No such fatuous ideas are held in
regard to males. If Freddie wants to become a law-
yer, bookmaker, archbishop or tailor, he has to set to
work to learn as much as possible about the business.
But women enter the matrimonial profession with-
out any preliminary study. A female is not specially
endowed by Providence with an up-to-date knowledge
of obstetrics, hygiene, domestic economy and so
forth, any more than a male is ushered into the
sunlight with a profound acquaintance with law, medi-
cine, drapery, engineering, etc. All that Providence
does is to make one sex physically capable of mater-
nity, and the other of paternity. If we are to pre-
vent the wiping out of embryonic posterity, we must
take up the job where Providence has left it off by
teaching the mother her business. It is only reason-
able to suppose that were the profession of mother-
hood run on common-sense lines, the infant death-
rate would be considerably smaller. At present, the
Commonwealth is paying awav something like
£45.000 a year in bonuses for children that do not
live beyond 12 months, and are, consequently, of no
economic value to the State. And this apnalling
waste is very largely due to ignorance. John Bull's
Board of Education recently reported that in London
alone there were 100,000 defective children attending
■school, most of whom had been rendered either nhvsi-
■cally or mentally unfit through malnutrition. Things
■may bp a bit better here, but they are quite bad
'enough. The relative value of foodstuffs is one of the
■subjects of which woman is assumed to have a wide
■and accurate knowledge merely because of her sex.
Yet the appalling ignorance of such matters possessed
by a large number of females must be painfully obvi-
ous to any intelligent citizen. Little Muriel, aged
two. is often fed on anything, from green apples to
Irish stew. In fact, there appears to be a pretty
general impression that Mttrtel will positively thrive
on badlv cooked meat and fancy cakes. Undpr the
circumstances, it is scarcely astonishing that Muriel
frequently stops thriving before she is five years old.
The State wants kids who will live long enough to
become defenders and taxpayers, and there is some-
thing rotten about the State education systems which
make little or no provision for teaching the future
mothers of the race how to ensure this by seeing to
the health and general fitness of H'-uV oO’spring.
“Leo Lear,” from Victoria’s oldest settle-
ment : The Hentys, arriving at Portland in
1834, established the first permanent settle-
ment in the Southern State. They took on,
among numerous other industries, that of
whaling, and erected boiling-down works on
the beach. These remain there to this day ;
the old cauldrons and stone-work lie under
the shelter of Dutton’s Point, romped over
by kiddies and leaned against by lovers.
This Dutton’s Point is named after an older
pioneer than even Edward Henty, who when
he first saw Portland in 1833 found the
place occupied by a whaler and general ex-
plorer. William Dutton had arrived in the
locality in 1828; but he didn’t reside there
permanently, simply building some huts for
Ibis men, and returning to these domiciles at
.odd intervals. Hard by, on Whaler’s Bluff,
.overlooking Portland Bay, there are a series
.of humps and hollows, grazed over by the
itown cows. These scars on the landscape
;are all that remain of the first wounds made
■on Victoria by a plough. Further in is the
IRichmond Hotel, and I don’t know of any
(older pub standing in Victoria. It was,
however, built for a private residence —this
was in 1836 —and did not house a beer-
pump until long afterwards. Richmond
Henty, probably the first white child born
in Victoria, arrived in this old Richmond
Hotel on August 3, 1837. The place when
built was surrounded by a blue stone wall
Bft. high, capped with broken glass set in
mortar. This barricade was intended to
check the attentions of any Black Brother
who might arrive without an invitation. The
wall has withstood the winds of coastal Vic-
toria for close on 80 years, and is solid still.
Another ancient residence is Burswood.
built by the Hentys in the first days of
Portland. It is a massive stone affair, and
owes its chief interest to the fact that the
materials were all imported from the Cold
Land. Good building stone is as common
rohnd Portland as dust in Melbourne, and it
doesn’t appear a whit inferior to the rock
from overseas; but, then, this importing
craze always was a queer fish.
