Walter Hamlet Bentley, or Walter Bentley
Hamlet, or Walter B. Hamlet, as the case
may be, at the Theatre Royal, is not a per-
son of deep subtlety or grave mystery, but
an energetic sort of prince who makes things
move along with considerable vigor. The
veteran actor’s voice is not the sort of article
he used to carry 20 years a?o, and there are
touches of bellowdrama about his interpreta-
tion which are probably introduced to make
up for the lack of vocal power, or crack-up
of vocal chords, or something. The change
is an improvement, but W.B.’s elocution
has lots to recommend it, and he does get in
fine points of expression in his lines which
rouse the friends of the late W. Sliakspeare
to much enthusiasm. Everybody who saw
his first night agreed that the play scene was
the best of the show, and that the King got
things rubbed in properly. Miss Stephanie
Dale, as Ophelia, was just Miss Stephanie
Dale, and Hamlet’s best girl didn’t appear to
any skyscraping extent through the diseuvse.
For the rest, John Cosgrove was a fierce and
snappy King in extensive orange-purple
whiskers ; S. A. FitzsrerMd. a venerable but
blithersome Polonius ; and the large and var-
iegated amateur support ranged from mid-
dling to awful in the usual thoroughgoing
amateur way.
All Sydney wives who desire to poison
their husbands—the yearning usually oc-
curs every Monday morning at breakfast
during the summer—are strongly advised
to see how the thing is done at the Cri-
terion before crudely experimenting with
lysol or bullets. Sugar of lead looks like
white Australian sugar; and by keeping
two sugar-basins, one containing sugar of
lead and the other the domestic article,
any experienced wife can manage the
troublesome business easily while her hus-
band is buried behind the newspaper’s ac-
count of the gas strike. Care should be
taken to keep the sugar of lead sugar-basin
out of the children’s way, as otherwise acci-
dents might happen. And it is as well to
warn sugar of lead novices not on any ac-
count to ring up Dr. Julius Knight to pre-
scribe for the sunstroke that hubby got

The thrilling drama of the last two acts,

The congregation all stood up and reck-
oned it had had a fair return for its contri-
bution to the collection plate at the Palace
tabernacle (Sydney) on Saturday night.
Deacon Willanderson’s sermon was “The
Prince and the Beggar Maid,” the point
thereof being that it is easier to covet your
neighbor’s best girl than to get her. The
sermon (which has been given before by
Bishop Billanderson in Sydney) runs on
the stirring lines exploited by Rabbi Stanley
Weyman and deserves well of all denomina-
tions, including the Holy Children of the
Water Waggon. Pastor Anderson, with the
assistance of Sister Eugenie Duggan, Bro-
ther-in-the-bones-and-blood Cyril Mackay,
Brother - pray -on - and - weary-not Beckett,
Brother-in-the-Smoke-of-Gomorrah Max Clin-
ton and others will conduct service nightly
at the old stand, and will be glad
to see friends and other parishioners. Pews
may be booked in advance, but none are free
owing to the high cost of living. Those who
faint by the wayside may gather at the
river which flows over the counter of Bro.
Adams next door.

“The Kings of the Forest,” a J. D. Wil-
liams picture wherein lions, leopards, etc.,
wander to within a few feet of the trombone
and then dash out into the plentiful dark-
ness, has been the highest-powered attrac-
tion at the Crystal Palace. The picture is
founded on a love-yarn in the jungle, possi-
bly of New York—and the lions are intro-
duced to prove conclusively that it is jungle
and not cow-paddock. Still, it is one of the
best wild-animal-at-close-quarters pictures
ever shown at the Palace. At the Empress the
moving-picture Indian in “The Redemption
of White Hawk.” has been touching the heart
of the Methodist Conference by turning
Christian, and refusing to mutilate any more
pale-faces—till the next picture demands it.

way that her fairy chiffons seem as naught,
and her pink form divine is a Midsummer

At the Opera House a noteworthy re-
arrival this week is Claire Romaine, the
buxom boy-impersonatress, and the gallery
is catching on to Claire’s choruses as before.
George Bryant and Co., Mozetto, May Quin-
trell and Alexander Prince remain in evi-
dence, but the Arabs are leaving to-night
(Thursday), having remained long enough
to emphasise the old-established fact that
the tinted races have no inventive skill in
acrobatics. The Patty-Frank Troupe puts
in some fine work that makes the Arabs’
turn look rough and ready. Moreover, the
white men preserve a dignified silence which
shines by comparison with the foolish hulla-
baloo from “the East.”
** * *
The elements are dealing so gently with
the open-air shows at St. Ivilda that Brans-
combe’s Dandies rarely bring their sliding
canvas roof into action, or catch cold on
the chest. Their entertainment is wearing
well. Songs frivolous and otherwise, inter-
spersed with patter and concerted fooling,
comprise Part I. and constitute Part 11.
Ten Dandies, including the pianist, keep the
ball rolling for an hour or two always in the
same old way, which is evidently the way
it should go. If all the year were summer
time The Bulletin would see no reason
why the ball shouldn’t go on for ever.

“It’s Never Too Late to Mend,” adapted
by an Amurkan tilm-fakery, had an enthusi-
astic innings at West’s (Melb.)last week. The
picture was elaborately done, with just suffi-
cient accent on the villainy of John Meadows
to make the effusion bellowdramatic. An-
other instalment of the Panama Canal series
divulged further details of a mammoth en-
terprise, and the concluding chapter of
“What Happened to Mary” showed that
Mary was a little lamb. As for “Bloomer
and Lea,” its roaring farce dispersed the
gloomy feelings aroused by the sight of the
town of Rhodes trying to look impressive
in its slummy decay.

Adelaide Royal has got to work again
with Albert Edmunds’s “Native Born,”
which offers a fine fat part for Bert Bailey
and a welcome novelty in the inclusion of
Australia’s one snow-and-ice patch. The
Exhibition is getting ready for the March
Show, so Dampier and his popular “Dan-
dies,” well suited by these warm evenings,
have to get out of the gardens for a week ;
and the Wonder Circus has to move on to
Broken Hill with Mikko, the mind-reading
dog, and Tomato, the educated ass, and the
rest of the quaint attractions. Cannon, the
56-stone man, made the journey in a special
van. At the King’s, the Delinos are still
walking the wire, and the Delroys are jug-
gling. and Leonard Nelson, whose singing
of “Mr. Booze” is artistic, commits the
criminal offence of inciting lots of amateurs

Many strange things can happen to a man
in Sydney, but a visiting Maoriland press-
man seems to have fallen upon the limit. A
man of reputation (he has been chairman of
the Parliamentary Press Gallery and Presi-
dent of the Institute of Journalists, which
are the two highest positions the M.L. craft
can hand to a scribe as a compliment), he
walked from the Daily Telegraph the
other night, down King-street to George-
street. He was there told to get a move
on by a policeman (who, it transpired, had
been four months in the service) in such a
manner that, according to the subsequent evi-
dence before the Magistrate’s Court, he
threatened to report the officer. He pro-
ceeded on his way towards his hotel in
Wynvard Square, and was suddenly arrested
by the John in Barrack-street, taken to a
station, and lobbed into the cells. There
he was charged, not with loitering, but with
vile language. The only direct evidence be-
fore the magistrate was that of the police-
man and the pressman. The policeman
swore the language was used and that he
arrested his man in George-street. The
pressman swore the facts as set out above,
and called proof of character. The magis-
trate opined that there was perjury on the
premises, and, having said so, discharged the
newspaper man. Presumably now _ some re-
sponsible person will make inquiries as to
the perjury side of the matter.
AT POVERTY POINT.
From Jo Smith: I have read the critic’s notice
and one or two pars concerning my play, “The Bush
Girl,” which finishes its run to-night (28/2/’l3) at
Sydney Palace. For anything complimentary that
he may have said, I thank him. I have no doubt
the piece deserved both his censure and his praise.
I think he was niggardly over the latter, but per-
haps he doesn’t; and, anyway, he’s got the floor, and
good theatrical ethics teach me that I mustn’t squeal.
But when he expresses a doubt as to whether the
situation—the “damphoolish situation” —-at the end
of Act 11. was in the original script, I feel I may
butt in. I was puzzled to know why that “curtain”
did not “make good” in Sydney; whenever it was
done in Melbourne and in the western States it was
easily the most popular situation in the play. I
have since learned the reason. In my script it is
set forth that the boys who are instructed by the
Major to fire off the rifle match shall consist of about
24 cadets, such as used to be attached to our
State schools before the advent of compulsory train-
ing. They looked a serviceable lot —could march
well, were of good physique, and when they rushed
the bridge and fired their final volleys at the imagin-
ary targets, heedless in the noise and excitement of
the troopers’ commands to cease, it looked anything
but a “damphoolish situation,” and it earned the
praises of The Bulletin’s Melbourne critic, who is
not the softest snap in the world either. Of course
if the bridge was “held” by eight scrae-gy infants
in the rig of boy scouts, then I forgive the Sydney
critic, and I suppose I richly deserved his ridicule.
“Van Eck” : Huge Deal Mclntosh is still hustling.
He blew into Adelaide on Thursday last accompanied
by Mrs. Harry Rickards, and ran on to Broken Hill.
He returned to Adelaide on Saturday, and within
two hours had agreements signed for taking over the
lease of the new Princess Theatre from Sayers and
Lennon, Ltd. ; also, he will take a hand in building
a new theatre at Broken Hill, and intends to drop
in occasionally at Port Pirie and Port Adelaide.
“Haresfoot”: Many of us are grieved by the sad
news about Olive Berkeley’s distress in America (B.
20/2/’l3). She came to us in this way: J. C. Wil-
liamson was an old friend of Harry Edwards, of the
San Francisco stage, and struck the idea of bringing
Edwards to Australia with “Little Lord Fauntleroy.”
Edwards had left Melbourne for America about 27
years before.' He and George Fawcett Rowe had
managed the Melbourne Theatre Royal for Brooke. It
is said that Edwards was the only man who ever
acted “Timon of Athens” in Australia. This would
be on the Sydney side. At San Francisco he pur-
sued a hobby, and became the greatest butterfly
collector in America. Returning under contract with
J.C.W., he brought two Little Fauntleroys—Olive
Berkeley and Gracie Hopkins. I think he wanted
to open with Gracie, but Williamson preferred Olive.
She was by far the more handsome, but chubby and
smaller Gracie had more talent. Olive’s mother
acted in the play as mother of a scrubby boy im-
postor set up in rivalry to the real Fauntleroy. Her
trenchant vulgarity may be remembered —such a
striking contrast to the sweet and delicate Fauntleroy
of her daughter. Leopold acted Hobbs, the genial
grocer. George Selth, son of the Hon. George Selth
Coppin, was a footman.
“Harrison O.”: After viewing Kean’s production
of “A Midsummer Night’s Dream” at Drury Lane,
Hazlitt complained that a delightful fiction had
been converted into a dull pantomime. Much the
same can be said of Asche’s production, beautiful as
it is. Titania is merely a well-brought-up but rather
tired young woman, in fancy undress. Puck is a street
arab pure and simple. The fairies sing indifferently,
and dance on a lake on which the feet of some of
them fall with heavy thuds. Hippolyta’s amazons are
about the size of AVade’s mind or “Liberalism s
policy; and the alleged ass’s head worn by Bottom
would be rejected with scorn by anv self-respecting
pantomimic animal impersonator. The show is m
many ways theatrically impressive, but as a picture
of fairyland it is not much more convincing than
Snout’s impersonation of Wall, or Snug’s lanthorn
which doth the horned moon misrepresent.
George Dixon, whose scenic work at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney) some years ago was the feature of manv a
show, returned from London a few days ago. ror
the past seven years he has been painting in the
studio of Joseph Barker, in the Big Smoke, where
the beautiful work of the Oscar Asche productions
was turned out. Dixon has joined brushes with
that other well-known scenic artist, Harry Whaite,
at Sydney.
“Harrison O.”: The success of the Repertory The-
atre’s “Man and Superman” matinfee at Melbourne
Princess’s should induce Gregan McMahon to endeavor
to arrange to put on his plays in the afternoon at one
of the theatres —the King’s, Princess’s and Bijou would
practically always be available —instead of at night
at the inadequate Athenaeum. Since the inception ot
the Repertory Theatre three halls have been tried—
the Turn Verein, St. Patrick’s and Athenaeum—and
each has proved unsuitable. The performance of
Shaw’s play at the Princess’s, where Tanner s motor-
car did noff take up the whole stage and part of the
wings, was much more convincing than it was at tne
Collins-street lecture hall; and, what is more, the
big playhouse was filled. It is possible to lure the
Best People to a theatre, even when literary drama
is offering, but it is quite another proposition to get
them 6 to a place that is mostly devoted to flower
shows, lectures on the human inside, and other
strictly unfashionable matters.
“Chasuble”: Hannay’s new comedy of “General
John Regan,” just acted in London, recalls the stage
works of some other reverends. There is Australia’s
Rev. G. Walters, with “Joseph of Canaan,” and Hill-
house Taylor’s “Parsifal.” The Rev. Home scandal-
ised the Scotch Kurrk with his tragedy of “Douglas”:
“My name is Norval,” is the remembered titbit. The
Rev. Maturin wrote “Bertram,” the most horrific
tragedy ever conceived, except perhaps “The Duchess
of Malfi,” wherein Duke Ferdinand places her lover’s
dead hand in the claw of his wife. Edmund Kean
and Brooke used to act Bertram. Dean Milman
wrote “Fazio,” a play in which Miss Cushman and
others were admired. Rev. Dr. Young, author of
“Night Thoughts,” wrote “The Revenge,” often acted
by Kean and others. Byron, in his paradoxical way,
said it was better than “Othello.” It reverses that
play, with a white Othello and a black lago named
Zanga. “Oh, vengeance! I have followed thee too
far, and to receive me Hell blows all her fires.”
Rev. John Bate was a London dramatist in the 18th
century, and Rev. J. Genest wrote the longest history
of the English stage. Talking of Byron and
“Othello,” Byron said: “I won’t read Shakspeare.
He cows me. If I read him, I would not attempt any-
thing myself.” Goethe said a better thing: “We
steal the silver dishes of his apples, but only give
potatoes.”
“Propstick”: Brough said: “I have found out a
complete solution for all this difficulty about the first
names of people on the playbills.” The company won-
dered what this cutting of the Gordian knot by
Brough and Boucicault could be. Then the bills
struck everyone aghast. It was simply “Mr. Brough,”
“Mrs. Brough,” “Mr. Titheradge,” “Miss Browne,”
and so forth. There could not have been more com-
motion if boiling water had been poured on an
antheap. Even a strike was threatened. However.
B. and B. were implacable, and this plain fashion
went on for several years. Brough said: “What on
earth do you want to be billed with a first name
for? If it is absolutely needful to distinguish you
from anybody else, you shall have it. The old, sensi-
ble manner in London was simply “Mr. Kemble. Mr.
Kean, and the like.” One time, Musgrove had the
same trouble. “All right,” says Muzzy. The bills
came out with Miss Stewart and Beatty, and nobody
else mentioned at all 1 But that could never be
kept up, because the public says, “Ah, it’s a rotten
company, and the management is ashamed to give the
names.”
Maudie Thornton, the Ballarat girl who cut a few
bright dashes in comic opera and panto, on the Aus-
tralian stage, was “principal boy” in “Sinbad the
Sailor” at the Theatre Royal, Edinburgh, this year,
and the city of Auld Reekie seems to have taken to
her kindly. Her photo appeared in the Sketch re-
cently as evidence in favor of the continuance of the
she-boy system in panto.

“T.O.I”: A curiosity, in the shape of an old pro-
gramme of Rowe’s Circus, Melbourne, in February,
1854, comes my way. The circus stood on the
corner of Exhibition-street (then known as Stephen-
street) and Lonsdale-street, where George Coppin, in
1855, built the Olympic Theatre or “Iron Pot.”
Now for the bill: “Grand entree, the Venetian
Nobles ; the mighty deeds of Atlas, in a performance
with the revolving globe, by Mr. T. Nunn; graceful
equestrianism by Mrs. Yeamans; trick horse,
Othello, introduced by Mr. J. A. Rowe; principal
equestrian act, by Mr. J. L. Smith ; drawing-room
performance, by Mr. G. Lewis and Lilliputian Tom ;
pyramids, by the company; young Raphael in his
great act, ‘The British tar’ ; trick pony, Beeswing,
introduced by J. A. Rowe; J. L. Smith and Mr.
Yeamans, on two horses, as the Swiss pair; ground
and lofty tumbling, by the company. The whole to
conclude with the laughable farce, ‘Love in a Tub.’
Clown to the arena, Ned Yeamans.” Mr. T. Nunn
was Tom Nunn ; sometime ringmaster, clown, etc. ;
he was always engaged to “double” Clarence Holt in
“The Corsican Brothers.” He was subsequently
landlord of the Ilummums Hotel, on the corner of
Russell and Bourke streets. Tom erected a very fine
stone water-trough in front, on which was inscribed,
“Drink, and let thy cattle drink also.” Mrs. Yea-
mans was a Miss Griffiths, and began her profes-
sional career with a circus in Sydney. Her first
dramatic part was that of Little Julia in “A
Farmer’s Daughter.” She was born in the Isle of
Man in 1835, and at 18 years of age married Ned
Yeamans. In 1865 Mrs. Yeamans went to America
and plaved in stock companies in San Francisco.
On her '7sth birthday her companions of the Echo
Company, with which she was then playing, pre-
sented the old ladv with a bouquet and 75 crisp
dollar bills. She died the next year. Her husband
died in the ’6o’s. Mr. G. Lewis was, of course, Mr.
G. B. W. Lewis, husband in after years of Rose
Edouin. Lewis induced Tom Mooney, of the National
Hotel, to build him the Amphitheatre in Spring-
street, which subsequently was named the Princess
Theatre, having George Fawcett Rowe as lessee.
Tom Mooney hailed from ’Frisco, and returned there
in 1856. While in Victoria, during the agitation
by Wilson Gray and others to unlock the lands he
urged the policy of “A farmer, a vote Mid a rifle.
I think he got a hint to “get,’ and got. The
performers are all gone excepting Lilliputian lorn,
who is a resident of a Sydney suburb.
A competition in Pearson’s to decide the 12 people
who would be most easily recognised in the street had
the odd result of leaving all the theatrical people
out of it. Ellen Terry was top of them with 16b
votes, hut was nowhere in the general list, which in-
cluded three royalties (King, Queen and Queen Alex-
andra). five politicians (Llovd George, Churchill,
Balfour, Asquith and Chamberlain), three soldiers
(Roberts, Kitchener and B. Powell), and one author
—Hall Caine.
Hamer” : A letter from the Actors’ Union reached
the Barrier Labor Federation just as that body was
considering the question of shooing the non-unionist
off the premises, and the B.L.F. took no time to.
decide that non-union theatrical companies shouldi
not be welcomed at Broken Hill. As that somewhat
unattractive rising, hump, or inequality of the
ground is always considered a good port of call, the
decision will probably hit some theatrical shows
pretty hard. Labor organisations in several other
large industrial centres have already come to a
similar decision. The Actors’ Union, which has
already complained of bitter hostility and unfair
tactics on the part of its opponents, is apparently
going to work its industrialism for all it can, anil
gather in advantages in the places where to be a
union is strength. It now threatens to send a list
of non-union companies to the influential bodies in
all the larger centres, and the boycott may begin any
old time.
“Cockaigne”: Actor-manager George Alexander,
having reaped a small crop of glory as a County
Councillor, declares that he will stand for Parlia-
ment at the next election. Offhand, I recall only a
brace of Thespic legislators, both of Melbourne—
George Coppin and Morton King. Coppin, at only
39 years of age got into the Upper House amidst a
howl from the Wowsers. Afterwards he was in the
Assembly. When in the Council he introduced a
really grand measure, the Torrens Land Act,
borrowed from South Australia, simplifying titles,
Morton King was tragedian, auctioneer, M.L.A. and
magistrate. Between them these two rattled up a
nerformance of “The Merchant of Venice,” at Melb.
Royal, by members of Parliament. King was Shy-
lock and Coppin Launcelot Gobbo; and the Melb.
Hospital was £lOOO the richer.
Harry Roberts, who will pool his abilities with
the newly-formed Hamilton-Plimmer comedy co. to
tour Australasia, tells a good story of how he got
his chance with David Belasco. He (Roberts) was
engaged to play a small part in New York in “The
Price of Peace.” One day, at rehearsal, he was
asked to double up and play a bishop as well. The
bishop’s job was merely to read the marriage ser-
vice, and Roberts, being very young, reckoned it
was a slice below his dignity. However, he agreed
to do it provided he might put any name but his
own opposite the bishop on the cast. This was
agreed to, and “John Hinton” was used. When the
piece was produced, the critics did not give Roberts
a line, but they had a hatful of hallelujahs for Hin-
ton. They advised church dignitaries to go and
learn the true solemnity and beauty of the marriage
service. Hinton became a boom." Then one day,
when Roberts was feeling mad, an ascetic-looking
man appeared, and said to the stage manager,
“Hullo, old man ; you’ve got a chap named Hinton
here” (looking round). Up stepped H.R. “No,”
he snapped, “there is not.” “Oh, sorry for that,”
says the other. “I had a good part to offer him.”
“Look here,” explained Roberts, “I’m all there is
of Hinton, but my name is Roberts.” “It’s all
the same,” replies the austere gentleman; “my
name’s Belasco, and if Mr. Litt” (Roberts’s boss)
“can spare him next season, I can offer Hinton a
capital part in Mrs. Leslie Carter’s new play, ‘La
Du Barry.’ Do you think he would sign with me?”
“I’ll forge his name and chance it,” says Roberts.
Hinton never appeared again.
“Gordon G.”: For its size, Ballarat is the worst
show town in Victoria, or the world. On a popula-
tion of about 48,000, it runs three picture shows, and
nothing else. Any other form of theatrical entertain-
ment is to the natives almost unknown. During 1912
it sported, besides the pictures, a very occasional and
very heartrending vaudeville show, and about seven
more or less busted bellowdramas. J.C.W. and
Mclntosh shun it; and so far this year even Marlow
and Anderson have given it the go-by. I don’t
know what is the matter, unless it is that the old
Golden City is dead, and can’t drag even a decent
semblance of a dividend out of the bowels of its
inner consciousness.
The Pollards—no longer the Lilliputians, but the
“Australian Juvenile Opera Co.” nowadays—spent
their Christmas in Halifax, Canada. There are 39
members of the co. now, running from 14 to 20, and
thev are usually led bv “Teddie” McNamara and
Willie Pollard on the boys’ side and Queenie Wil-
liams, Eva Pollard and Pattie Hill on the girls.’
“The Mikado” and “Sergeant Brue” are the strong
suits in the present play.
Fred Elton drops a line or more:—
Cordially agree with “Aldridge Evelyn’s” (B.
6/2/’l3) arguments in favor of a vegetable and fruit
diet and the abolition of corpse-tearing. Edison,
Marconi, Eustace Miles, Haeckel and thousands of
other non-flesh eaters all testify that much more work
can be done and much more endurance exhibited on
nuts and other vegetables. On them, General H.
Walker, aged 81, walked from Land’s End to John o’
Groats. A band of Philadelphia grass athletes beat
a cannibal team by four hours at the hardest work
known (rifle extended up and down on haunches, be-
side which shearing or shovel work is a positive
pastime and most other kinds of labor mere child’s
play). Eustace Miles won every event on a bicycle
card (Aston, Eng.), one mile, five ditto, 10-mile, and
so on —the blamed old dashed lot! Flesh is not
necessary to our health and strength. I know, and
can prove, that uric acid fills our hospitals, our
gaols, poor-houses and lunatic asylums. It may even
fill our pulpits and museums for all I know to the
contrary. We are what we eat, of that there can
be no doubt; and as long as we are carnivorous we
shall love wars, strikes, bloodshed, prize-fights and
all such horrors. Until we give up meat eating we
shall be virile only, and our falling longevity will
gradually lower, until we shall be old men at 20.
The Bulletin begs to mention that there
is no falling longevity on the premises. It
is a creature or jim-jam of the imagination.
The span of life, especially among meat-
eaters, is constantly increasing.
A BONUS TO ART.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He is an actor dark and grim ;
With him the tragic role’s the thing.
No pro. can hold a light to him
As Hamlet or the Silver King;
But ’tis his grief that he must do
Whate’er his masters tell him to.
Said he to-day: “Me little wife
Will in a month or so bequeath
To this old world another life.
No longer shall I writhe beneath
The managerial tyranny.
’Tis then the higher art for me.”
“But how?” asked I. “How can this chick
Make such a difference?” He shed
A smile of pity, paused to flick
A fiy from off his beer, and said:
“With five pound one may go alone—
I’ll start a company of me own!”
VICTORIA.
S. S.
GAS v. GOSPEL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
However, one preacher remarked: “The light of the
gospel can penetrate through anv darkness and light
op the whole world.” —S.M.H. (3/3/’X3), referring tn
Sydney’s gas strike.
Maybe it can ; I will not say
That parson he doth lie to me;
But I will bet its fiercest ray
Is dull to electricity.
And, even if the Gospel’s light
Can penetrate the gloom opaque,
Where howls the heathen in the night,
I’m dashed if it will grill a steak!
The whole world may be lighted up
With beams of brilliance from on High—
-1 only know at home we sup
By candle light on cold tinned pie.
There is no boiling water now
To brew the whisky punch we love —
In fact, the gas strike is a cow,
Despite the Gospel light above!
N.S.W.

WINES
According to a story told to Sydney’s City
Coroner last week there’s a whole boat-load
of murderers raging round Sydney Harbor.
A thoughtless fellow took his wife and in-
fant out in a sailing craft on Sunday week,
and they were capsized. He got his wife
and child on to the keel of the overturned
boat, and, of course, it nromptly ducked
under and threw them off again. While
they were struggling in the water another
sailing boat blew along, circled round them,
and then streaked off without offering aid.
The woman and the baby were drowned,
and the man rescued with difficulty. It s a
ghastly business altogether, but one cannot
help wondering what sort of a fool a man
must be to take a woman and a baby out in
a 16ft. boat without a centre-board, in a
stiff nor’-easter, and without having life-
belts attached to his wife and himself so
that in the event of a capsize they would
have some chance of keeping the baby afloat
between them till assistance arrived. It’s
nothing but madness to take women and
children out in anything but a motor-launch
or yacht when a nor’-easter is sweeping Port
Jackson. So far the police have been unable
to trace the water-pigs who so gaily sailed
away.
“Ogalimite”: Fort Queenscliffe (Vic.) is
at present occupied by 28 of the festive
youths who refuse to take up arms in de-
fence of their country —or, anyway, dodged
it. They are, on the whole, of very good
stamp and behavior, and have fallen into
tlie hands of the new military police mostly
by reason of the fact that they were too
busy leaning up against the fence. They
are under canvas, and their spirits are high;
also their religious convictions are being
considered, so the Roman will eat fish twice
weekly and the Hebrew (if any) will be
considered likewise. What time he is not
undergoing the pangs of physical culture,
the delinquent has military discipline para-
ded before his eyes in the shape of a guard
of five men to see that he does not escape
over the low mud wall that prevents the
passer-by from stealing secrets of alarming
nature concerning our coastal defence. About
the only thing that he ought to get, but
doesn’t, is a daily lesson on the need of
national defence, with a history of the un-
pleasant things that have happened to
Korea and Poland and Provence and other
countries that have shirked it-
“Petifi” recalls an ancient tale —with a
modern counterpart:—-
For 38 weary years the mystery of the ship
Marie Celeste has baffled the theorists. Writers
have built stories on the puzzle; it has been talked
over in the fo’c’s’le, in smoke rooms, palaces, hovels,
parsonages, pubs, and other places, and the more
alleged light that has been thrown upon it the
murkier it has become. In December, 1874, the
vessel was spoken to in mid-Atlantic by the High-
lander, and reported “All well.” Two days later it
was again spoken by another vessel, but there was
no answering signal. Puzzled, the captain of the
other vessel ran close in and sent a boarding party
to investigate. The party found everything ship-
shape. Boats (there was not one missing) were in
their places on deck, and were seaworthy ; cargo
was well-stowed and in good condition; brass-work
was well-polished, and personal effects of crew and
passengers were O.K. A cold luncheon for the
passengers was set in the saloon, and that the ves-
sel had not been rolled by bad weather was proved
by a medicine phial standing upright on the cap-
tain’s table. Some sail was set. Yet there was
not one human being on board. And not one of the
hundred-odd aboard has been heard of since! AV’hat
became of them ? AVhy did they leave the ship ?
And how did they leave ? Now, just when one was in-
clined to class the story among impossible legends,
alongside Vanderdecken and the Flying Dutchman,
comes the weird yarn of Captain Claridge, of the
steamer Roumanian, which made New York Harbor
recently. He declares that he found, in mid-
Atlantic, the three-masted Norwegian barque Re-
mittent, 300 tons. It was undamaged, all sails
were set, but there was not a soul aboard. Evi-
dence that the vessel had been abandoned only a
few hours previously was abundant. The Rou-
manian took the derelict in tow, but had to cast
off during a gale. Is it another Marie Celeste
case? If it is, what is the uncanny thing that
lurks in mid-Atlantic, and so mysteriously empties
healthy ships of their human cargoes?

Apparently there isn’t going to be any-
thing distinctive about Myolemargarine, the
Federal capital city, except the idea of put-
ting it on a plain littered with the corpses
of drought-stricken rabbits. One of The
Kink’s brilliant thoughts is to start business
with an “emblematical column,” of granite,
six-faced; and for the material he is going
to sponge on the granite-workers of various
parts. This, of course, is merely tearing a
leaf out of Oom Paul Kruger’s book. Want-
ing to commemorate events of 1881, and
having an empty Treasury to do it with,
Oom Paul called on each district to fetch
along a stone; and piled together they made
the monument of Majuba. But that was the
record of work done. The other is merely
the preliminary whoop of a jest begun.

From “The Old Soldier” :
It looks as if the AVar Correspondent would
shortly find his occupation gone—if, indeed, the
tragedy hasn’t already arrived. For some years
now he has been kept carefully out of the firing
line, and only allowed to see what the army he was
attached to thought fit. Still, hitherto, he has at
the worst been allowed to see something of he
trouble. The correspondents who went to the Bal-
kans, full of hope and ambition and high resolve
and a determination to staggbr humanity with their
thrillful yarns, saw absolutely nothing. With one
exception they didn’t even get material for a rea-
sonably convincing set of lies. They were bundled
en masse into Sophia and left there till the armi-
stice began. The only man who made good was the
famous Austrian soldier-journalist who made such
a scoop for the Vienna paper that had the luck to
retain him. He had the good fortune to be allowed
to see the official Bulgarian despatches, etc., as a
result of which he was enabled to describe, in detail,
every battle almost as though he had witnessed it.
Slightly biassed in an anti-Turk direction, of course,
but that didn’t injure the story to any extent.
The war-correspondent has largely killed himself.
There are too many of him, and he is too irrespon-
sible and dishonest. Half a dozen reliable, sober
practitioners of, say, the Bennet Burleigh stamp
would be endured with equanimity by any set of
warring nations. But four or five score of
yellow journalists are perilous propositions, and,
consequently, the tendency of Governments, com-
manding officers and privates alike is to treat the
correspondent with contumely. Take the painful
experience of Angus Hamilton. He was attached to
the Turkish army. He was caught hy a Bulgarian
cavalry patrol, threatened with revolvers and swords,
called a liar, tied to a cart-tail, and left there all
night, and generally treated like a dog. And that
about represents the opinion of the average soldier
in regard to war-correspondents within a few min-
utes of the gun’s first remarks.
The curse of a critic: —
Having decided to acquire some examples of the
work of portrait-painter Sargent, Melbourne Art
Gallery has secured a couple of “bargains” in the
form of utterly unimportant water-color sketches —
a Venetian scene and a landscape. The latter,
“Autumn on the Simplon,” is a confused' mass of
colors, vague in composition and entirely lacking
in the qualities which have won so much praise for
the artist’s best water-colors. “The Grand Canal,
Venice,” though more pleasing, is a painfully con-
ventional piece of work, almost completely spoilt by
an ugly mass in the foreground representing the bow
of a gondola. The picture contains some nice archi-
tectural work, but is in no way representative of
the artist. A feeling of hopeless protest surges up
when one considers recent purchases made by the
Gallery: (a) A doubtful Raeburn, (b) a ditto Rey-
nolds, (c) a Hoppner which is almost certainly a
fraud, and for which £2OOO was paid, although
four years ago the thing was sold for £165; (d)
Brangwyn’s “The Card Players,” which cost £630
and is inferior to “The Scoffers,” acquired by Syd-
ney for £400; (e and f) two undistinguished
water-colors by minor artists of the Glasgow school
forices £315 and £175 respectively). And now
these two sketchy things of Sargent’s, the price of
which has not been disclosed.
“T. Ofttt" on the training 0 f Australia
jun.
Let mo appear for the defence in the matter of
separate school cadet companies. There are all sorts
of good reasons why secondary school boys should
be allowed to drill together. 'The evening is the time
when they do half their real work. If they have to
go night-parading, it means a whole evening’s read
ing lost. Under the present system, drill fits into
the ordinary run of school work. There is the hour
or two of parade on the school ground, and then the
boys go straight back to class-room. Pearce’s
business is certainly to administer the Act so as to
promote efficiency. But the real fact is that the
school companies are the most efficient of the whole
lot. Down here at Geelong, for instance, the College
and Grammar School companies took first and second
place in the “area” marching competition. And in
shooting averages the school corps are always on top
The whole trouble seems to come from a sort of in-
verted snobbery in the P.L.C.—an uncomfortable
feeling that makes them anxious to assert at all
times that they are as good as anyone else. I don't
see why the question of manliness should be dragged
in. Vic. Public schoolboys had enough “manliness”
to keep up decent volunteer corps under the old
system ; sometimes as many as 80 per cent, of them
were enrolled. They’d stand comparison, age for
age, with the rest of Victoria in the games that
are supposed to mark out “manliness” ; and, all
round, they’ve no 6ort of “manliness” to learn from
Billy Jones, except the brand whose sign is blue
language and a tendency to spit on the carpet. And
that sort won’t help us to deal with an enemy.
“U.S.”: Claims for over £1,200,000 ster-
ling have been put in to the White Star
people by relatives of those lost in the
Titanic. And yet no claims have been made
in regard to Astor, Thayer. Hays, Stead or
Widener. Mrs. Harris wants £200,000 sterl-
ing for her husband, a theatrical manager.
The widow of Futrelle, a novelist, claims
£60,000 sterling; more modestly she esti-
mates at £6OO her own MS. of two novels.
A Mrs. Cardeza values her lost gowns and
jewels at £35,000 sterling; and another
woman wants £ll,OOO for 110,000 feet of
picture films. Of course the claims will be
decapitated; but, even so. their size is
enough to make shareholders in Australian
ferry and steamship cos. turn uneasily in
their beds when they wake at 2 a.m. and be-
gin to think of the usual unpleasant possi-
bilities.

Bernard O’Dowd, besides being guardian
of Melbourne’s Supreme Court Library and
chairman of the Fibrous Plaster Wages
Board, has been appointed Poet Laureate to
the State. Certainly there hasn’t been any
Gazette notice; but that is a mere detail.
His first job was to write a “Welcome to
the Members of the Dominions Royal Com-
mission” ; the fact that Itider Haggard is a
member of the Commission suggested that
something literary was called for. The job
done, the Government bade the Commission
to a theatre party (“Midsummer Night’s
Dream” was the fare), and there, on a
souvenir programme, the work of the Laur-
eate was put into the members’ hands. And
it was good work, too. There are things of
O’Dowd’s which constitute Empire problems
that even a Commission might well hesitate
to solve ; but this one bore intelligibility on

THE THRICE ACCURSED SKIRT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is therefore patent that some radical change in
the form of woman’s dress is imperative and urgent,
and as a first step toward that much needed reform,
let that ever-ready, insatiable death-trap, the skirt,
be at once abolished whether as an outside or under
garment. I do not presume to suggest what should
be its substitute, but in my mind’s eye I see attire of
a very pretty, neat, comfortable, seemly and hygienic
character, adaptable to all classes and to all weathers.
—Letter to Melbourne Age.
Abolish the Skirt !
It trails in the dirt
AVhen worn with a train in majestical way,
Or is flaunted around,
Hanging close to the ground,
From the waist of a woman old-fashionedly gowned,
Or “hobbles” the charmer in mode-ish array.
Loose fitting, or tight,
An indelicate sight
On a feminine figure that’s podgy or slight ;
Being meant to provoke
Giddy thoughts in a bloke,
AA T ho tickles his pal with a blue-tinted joke
Concerning the limbs thus concealingly girt.
Oh, abolish the skirt!
Abolish the Skirt!
And thereby avert
The dangers that threaten all spinsters and dames,
Or plain or good-looking
AVho, washing or cooking,
Stand close to a furnace, unheeding the flames,
Oh, think of the kiddies,
Young virgins and widdies,
AVives, daughters and Biddys,
Not wisely alert.
Poor creatures ill-fated,
Thus incinerated
Or terribly hurt!
’Tis true that dead bodies were better cremated
Than Ghristianly buried (as often is stated),
But any live body would rather have waited
Than taste in the kitchen the torments of blazes:
Aye, better decay with your toes to the daisies
Than burn to a cinder
AVhere no one can hinder
Tlie flames with a squirt.
Oh, abolish the skirt!
Abolish the skirt!
It enables a flirt,
AVith twisting and swishing,
To keep the boys wishing,
AVhilst grey-headed parents are tushing and
pishing
At all those devices
AA r hereby she entices
The amorous Percy to covet possession
Of shapes which are cramped in their means of pro-
gression
The pants of the harem
(Why can’t women wear ’em?)
AA’ould show the boys facts, but the hobbles ensnare
’em
By broadly suggesting
Legs more interesting
Than mostly are hid in inflammable gowns—
Quite bandy, perhaps, or knock-kneed, like a clown’s.
Though transparent trousers
Might worry the AVowsers,
And parsons would view them with scandalised
frowns,
They’d hold no illusions for Percy and Bert,
A\ T ho, backing their fancy, would bet on a cert,
Oh, abolish the skirt!

New Catalogue posted on request
Cabled last week that, according _ to
“Ccockford’s Clerical Directory”—a publica-
tion without which the Religious Editor
never goes aeroplaning—no less than 30
Church of England parsons have disappeared
within the past 12 months. Now and again,
in the life of every holy orderer, there must
come a time when the long wash of after-
noon tea wears the gilt off the religious
gingerbread. When that drear hour arrives,
everything that goes to make up his tiny
round must seem futile. He can find no
comfort in the 35 pairs of worked slippers
he will never live to wear out ; the bedridden
oldest inhabitant seems a foolish old tyrant;
the winter flannels presented by the alleged
“gentry” to the poor look a poor sort of sop
for many years’ systematic extortion; the
empty pews suggest a yawning waste; the
scorned collection-plate seems a brazen mock-
ery ; and his own professional flat hat has a
low comedy look about it. The complete in-
difference of rich and poor, realised suddenly
in a dark hour, oppresses him horribly. So lie
decides to get out. But, because an ex-
clergyman is often looked upon by the stupid
with "superstitious uneasiness, he does a more
or less pantomimic exit, like the ex-reverend
now on his way hither with his lady com-
panion. Most of him, though, contrive to
r 0 cleanly through the trap, without the
’imelight and umbrella frills that eventually
spoilt the Knightly get away. In the leap
cowards a new life, an umbrella and a blazing
blue rocket are the worst sort of encum-
brances.
HOLIDAY MEMORIES.
Long, lazy days spent lolling in the surf.
The tragedy of the divorcing of Dorothy
Grimston Meyer by her husband is in the
fact that she has become afflicted with a
deafness which is said to be incurable. This
will prevent her from earning a living as an
actress. The co-respondent medical man is
married. The Court granted to Meyer the
custody of the children and costs. Dorothy
is left with the reflection that her parents,
the Kendalls, who stand for all that is most
“respectable” in the theatrical profesh in
England, were strongly opposed to her mar-
riage. Apparently it was an unfortunate
affair from the start. Meyer had the money-
getting disposition of liis race, whereas his
wife’s particular weakness was for getting
into debt. He proposed to cure her extrava-
gance by placing her with her children “on
the land” at lea (Vic.), where he bought a
property. There she remained for 16 months
on friendly terms with the local doctor, whilst
for nine months of the time her husband was
abroad on theatrical business. He had not
long been back on the land when he was
called away again to England, and then
Mrs. Meyer insisted on going to Melbourne
to live. The long absences of her husband
were certainly not calculated to reconcile
her to existence on the land. The doctor
was her sympathetic friend, and when grass-
widowhood goes motoring with sympathetic
friendship they are apt to toot towards the
divorce court. Justice John Madden once
preached a long sermon from the Bench to
the effect that a young wife with a mostly
absent husband was practically certain to
find a co-respondent sooner or later, but the
question of making legal allowances for in-
evitable indiscretions was not raised.

“Hamer”: I am moderately willing to
share in the public glorification of Scott s
heroism, but the greatest hero I ever met
was Willie Smith. Willie was a law clerk
on a microscopic salary, with an oppor-
tunity of making overtime, which he used
to the fullest extent; and he was popularly
known as Weary Willie because of his
energy and cheerfulness. Willie owned a
family and a wife. The latter was an un-
healthy person requiring any number of
trips to the mountains, and Willie contrived
to pay the price every time without a mur-
mur. On these occasions the little man got
the children dressed and off to school in the
morning, raced away at lunch time to teed
them, and rushed home at night with a
bundle of overtime work in his bag to see
that they had more food. It was haro
going, but by dint of working every moment
that he wasn’t washing the children s faces
or stuffing them with bread and jam. at
something which brought in cash, \\ilhe
contrived to keep the wolf and the bailnt
from the garden gate. He hadn’t much to
be Cheerful about, but he grinned all the
time When overwork made him ill he
laughed casually. It had become a habit
with him, and he couldn’t leave it off even
when at last his disordered inner mechanism
broke down and required a serious opera-
tion. When things were at their worst he
had to leave the office, but continued to
work at home until his last insurance pre-
mium was paid, the last instalment on lie
time-payment furniture made good and the
surgeon was ready with the knife. Fve
then he took things in lighter vein and when
the operation failed and Willie lay dying
in the hospital the frivolous expression was
still on his countenance. It may aoble
to finish as Scott did, but Weary Willies
end makes a stronger appeal to me.
It takes a woman to insist on the legal
pound of flesh. One of her in Adelaide had
difficulties with her husband. Then these
things happened. (1) They separated. (2)
She obtained an older against him for main-
tenance. (3) She made it up and took him
back. (4) She sued him for the arrears.
The Court said she was certainly entitled to
them, but hinted that if he paid-up to the
date of the reconciliation the matter might
reasonably be allowed to drop at that.

RATS!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The idiosyncrasies of councillors are permitted to
retard the public weal, and for one city father who
has a policy a score have nothing but a plentiful
aggregation of fads. —Melbourne comment.
In Hamelin, the story says,
A piper pied beguiled with airs
Through all that ancient’s city’s ways
A stream of rats, that down the stairs,
And up the cellar drains in pairs,
Came tumbling from their nasty lairs.
I would this gifted sprite might bring
His pipes along to Melbourne town,
And set the city streets a-ring
With witching tunes, while up and down
He lured the rodents big and brown
To Yarra’s turbid flood to drown.
’Tis not the greedy rats that lurk,
In musty dens beneath the leads,
Nor those that in the gutters work,
Nor rats that haunt the tumbled sheds —
Not these would choke the river beds,
But rats from alderr.ianic heads!
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.
LIBERTY ANGELS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Close packed in the cutters a merry ere
The launches tow them across Farm Cove,
Then Man-o’-War steps are covered with blue—
Brave lads with pockets of treasure trove.
Oh! the roving life is the life they love >
By farms and cities they set no store;
And Liz gives Polly a joyous shove
When liberty men are coming ashore.
As the white clouds march with the shouting wind,
I see another and whiter qj-ew
Going down the heavens some joy to tina
On another sea intensely blue.
Oh the brave white sailors of Paradise.
Like schoolboys out of the Gate they fly
And I know that Admiral God is wise
When liberty angels go down the sky.
N.S.W.
E. O'F.
THE DOUBLE LIFE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was often away from home. Sometimes in
those lonely hours dark doubts would assail her.
—Shiver from a slush novel.
McPherson leads a double life;
I found him out but yesterday.
And he was walking with his wife.
(McPherson leads a double life!)
When barmaids grin and fun is rife,
He tries to “kid” us he is gay.
The hypocrite! The beery strife
He shirks to lead the double life!
I hate all liars. Mac is one.
No more lie’W praise our barmaid’s dimple !
His gay pretence with us is done;
My friendly eye I saw him shun.
Mac is a Wowser, pure and simple !
N. S. W.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
Uneasy lies the head that wears a crown.
John Topi, “King of Ruapuke Island,” is the latest
royalty to feel trouble. His woe is poured out into
the Bluff (M.L.) Press, the newspaper handiest to
his kingdom. Here goes:—
Sir, —I, John Topi. Oblige me. I, John Topi,
am losing 100 sheep every year for 10 years, and
last year from July to 27th October my sheep were
rounded up at night ; I only saw the tracks of my
sheep in the morning. Looks more like they were
boated. All I am sorry for is the wool-buyers. Re-
port it for these people’s sake —the wool-buyers,
etc. Lost the pick of my lambs last week. Good
thieving! Ten years, lots long enough for John
Topi. Sheep-stealing—it’s getting common on Rua-
puke. It’s time it’s going to be stopped. All thieves
to leave Ruapuke Island. Honesty and virtue is
going to be the flag of Ruapuke. So please put this
in for me. The dam thief will be found. Fancy me
losing the pick of my lambs. I am sorry to do it,
but thieving must be stopped under no consideration.
I have been losing from £5O to £6O for 10 years in
sheep. So this year the man’s that shorn my sheep,
his wool-buyer pays me £2OOO. Honesty and virtue
Ruapuke’s flag. All thieves to leave Ruapuke Island.
This is to much of a good thing to live cheap on
other people’s work.
THE NERVE TEST.
Granting an aviator’s license, 1950.
“Sarto”: Passed through Albury(N.S.W.)
the other day, the following weird procession: Dad,
age somewhere near the Old-Age-Pension mark, in the
shafts of a tilted cart (two-wheeler) ; Mum, pushing
behind. Carrying a billy of water in one hand ; in
the lead, and helping per rope attached to shafts, a
youth, very lame. And, riding in the cart, amongst
the requirements of travel, Baby Australia, evidently
enjoying the trip. This family had travelled thus-
wise from Tarcutta, a distance of some 90 miles. I
don’t know where it was going to, but it vanished
over the sky-line, evidently bound for somewhere on
the old Oblivion Road.
“11. G.” : The persistency with which it is
held that white men are not permitted to witness
black brother’s circumcision rite (practised by Bin-
ghi practically all over Australia) calls for evi-
dence to the contrary. I have seen the operation
performed twice, once when I visited the Daly River
smelter with the then Government Resident (Mr.
Justice Herbert), and once elsewhere. On both occa-
sions, other writers notwithstanding, the business
was conducted in the morning. At the Daly River
the lad operated on was about 17 years of age, and
several lusty fellows held him down. They also did
an amount of howling, to save him the trouble, so
they explained, but evidently to drown his efforts.
The old man of the tribe was the chief surgeon.
The time during which the boy must abstain from
various foods varies. I offered a boy on the West
Arm, who had been a “man” for two years, a shil-
ling if he would eat an egg. He refused for fear he
would “close up die,” and this with a Chinese store
near by, where square gin was procurable. The
probability is that some old nigger in bygone days,
when there was a scarcity of food, established the
custom. Under it the able-bodied young men have
to hunt the food which is hard to procure, while to
the piccaninnies, lubras and old men is left the eggs,
gohannas, etc. Sub-incision is practised only in the
districts where food is scarce, and in consequence a
high birth-rate undesirable.
Kimbolton ’: Was prospecting on the
Leopold ranges, Kimberley, and the horses were dog
poor when I struck Sloppy Bill. “You oughter get
mewils, advised Bill. “I see a bloke camped at
Bungarlgoodysoak with three beauties he wants to
sell. I had to go in for tucker, anyway, so I de-
cided to inspect the “mewils.” They were beau-
ties, mud fat, and the owner assured me, “as quiet
as sheep.” I bought the mules, and was up betimes
next day. I had just finished tightening the girth
on the first when something happened. About din-
ner time I came to, and found a stranger pouring
water on mv face out of a quart pot. “How d’ye
feel now?” he asked. “What happened?” I care-
fully felt myself all over, and said, “I dunno; I
a bit queer; must ha’ fainted.” “Better have a
day’s camp,” he advised. I assured him that it
was impossible, I must get away, and he kindly
offered to assist me pack the mules. The good
Samaritan was just stepping back after tightening
the girth on the second mule, when the accursed
hybrid sprang four feet in the air and la n ned out
with all four feet at once. His, or its, near fore
and hind feet collided with the stranger’s chest, and
he threw several back somersaults with surprising
haste. I knew then how those lumps like teacups
came to be on my bosom, and what caused the cocoa-
nut-l;ke growth on the back of my head. The stranger
came to a bit before sundown, and, after carefully
feeling himself all over, said, looking sourly at the
mules, “Turn ’em out, I’ll fix ’em in the morning.”
He was no effete scion of civilisation, that man ; he
was a bushman, and, of course, resourceful. Next
morning, with a prospecting dish full of sand under
his arm, he approached the acrobatic brute that had
tried to stave his chest in, and stepping quietly to its
head, deftly inserted a handful of sand in each eye.
Before the astonished mule could decide to do any-
thing definite, the packs were on, surcingle tightened,
halter tied up and the hobbles buckled to its neck, all
ready for the road. Sounds severe, perhaps, to those
who do not know mules, but firmness is an essential
in mule-conducting. Since then, should these mules
appear preoccupied while I am preparing to pack up,
the mere pretence of scooping up a dish of sand re-
duces them to a condition of abject servility.
“Red and Blue” : Re that Parthian quiver
from “Aldridge Evelyn” (B. 6/2/T3), anent vege-
tarianism, may it be permitted me to reply:—
(1) Man has teeth closely resembling the car-
nivora. Refer to any museum for study of the den-
titions of any of the following carnivora (number of
teeth in parentheses) : Common seal (36), sea lions
(32), walrus (28), tiger (30), hyaena (34), civet
cat (40), domestic dog (a variable 42). The bear,
which is mostly herbivorous, has 44 teeth, while man
normally has 32 in the permanent set.
(2) Truly, “all flesh is grass.”
(3) By cooking, the poisons in an animal body are
either eliminated or neutralised. And if by “poisons”
“Aldridge Evelyn” means bacteria, then these micro-
scopic unicellular organisms are destroyed practically
in their entirety, including spores (bacterial means of
propagation), since heat is the best germicide known
to surgery.
(4) As against the foregoing, would “Aldridge
Evelyn” care to stagger his eyesight by making cul-
tures and microscopical observation of such unpre-
pared articles of diet as celery, watercress, lettuce,
radishes and other such vegetarian palate-ticklers, and
thereby note bacillus typhosus, bacillus tetanus, taenia
echinococcus (hydatids), ankylostomum duodenale,
distomum haematobium (generally known as worms),
et alia rnulta ?
(5) A diet consisting solely of vegetables is not
conducive to the best health. Results of observation
and physiological research on true vegetarians show
their muscles are flabby, they are less vigorous than
those who live on a mixed diet, and their resistance to
disease germs and the effects of haemorrhage is low.
There are also early evidences of senility.
(6) The comparison of the horse and the dog is a
weak example of the triumph of the vegetarian over
the carnivorous. The dog, in running, does not hold
the head erect, and since the tongue is partly involun-
tary muscle, it projects from the mouth not of the
animal’s volition, but of its own accord and by its
own weight. Also, the tongue thus forms a bridge
for the discharge of the excessive amount of saliva
that is formed in the action of running, and allows the
dog to breathe more freely. Almost always the
“glassy eyes” are due to dust. In retaliation of the
horse-and-dog comparison, note the success of Amund-
sen and his dogs in getting to the South Pole, as
against the ignominous failure of horses in two
unsuccessful expeditions. And cor. ider the eagle, vul-
ture, seagull and hawk, and their staying powers in
the air, as against parrot, sparrow and eo.
(7) That “flesh-eating more than anything else is
responsible for cancer,” is a wild throw-in. Would
that the cause of the dread scourge were established
beyond doubt, which it is not by any means.

“Mooki” : If the contributor “from a
strangled town” (B. 30/1/T3) knows as little of
the Gunnedah (N.S.W.) district as he does of the
local bridges, his remarks have not any special value.
His “little wooden bridge over the Namoi” is really
a steel structure, which cost £60,000; and as for its
being “perched above the general level,” if he had
been in the town during the flood of January, 1910,
he might have perceived that there is a reason even
for that. The slack times to which he refers are due
much more to three consecutive bad wheat seasons
which the Northern farmer has suffered from, than
to the strangling influence of the stations. For
some time past, land in these parts has shown a
much better proportionate return from wool than
from wheat, with much less worrv and risk ; and
plenty of cookies contemplate giving up the wheat
gamble and taking to the more profitable jumbuck.

“Gouger”: A very effective method of
catching fish, employed by natives of the Danetree
River (N.Q.), is to throw into a deep waterhole about
51b. of the roasted and cracked yellowish-red fruits of
one of the many species of palm-tree to be found in the
scrubs. In about a quarter of an hour the surface of
the water will be alive with apparently demented
fish. As far as my examinations have gone the only
effect of the fruit seems to be blindness, but black
brother says poison. Anyhow, it doesn’t stop him
scooping the tucker in and stowing it in the place
where it is calculated to do most good.
“Sa Wen” : “Blackwood, now so scarce, is
almost worth its weight in gold,” etc., says
“Johanna” (B. 13/2/’l3). Would it be too rude to
whisper that “Jo.” is orating through her millinery?
As a matter of fact, blackwood is slowly superseding
kauri as the cheapest medium for furniture faking.
Kauri is easier to work, but its price has been stead-
ily increasing, and manufacturers invariably use
blackwood if they can crowd it in. If the lady had
stipulated fiddleback, now I should have left her in
peace. But fiddleback is a very special breed of the
vegetable.
“Platman”: The finger talk or sign lan-
guage used by the Pascoe River (N.Q.) natives, men-
tioned by “Gouger” (B. 16/1/T3), is common
amongst the aborigines of the east
coast and tableland of Cape York
Peninsula. I have noticed it outside
Cooktown, at Port Stewart, the Coen
and Lloyd’s Bay, but, so far as I
have observed, its use is not confined
to relatives. At Port Stewart, the
niggers on different sides of the
Stewart River belong to different
tribes, and they converse, appar-
ently at great length, by signs. A
Batavia River boy I had with me used
this whirling or semaphore speech
with Coen, Lloyd Bay and Night
Island blacks. My own opinion is
that the sign language has been
■evolved by the necessity for silence
when hunting for game. The Far
Northern nigger is a very silent per-
son in the day-time, either in camp
or on the track, but at night, in
camp, gins and bucks let their
tongues out on the loose. Judging by
the laughter that frequently rings
out, some of the jokes are highly
appreciated, though from the inter-
pretation given me only a pickled
commercial traveller could stand them
raw.
“Catherine M.”: Re moon-blindness (B.
30/l/’l3). Once knew an able-bodied ox-conductor
who one moonlit night requested the head of our
house to see that his (the ox-conductor’s) team of
(scarlet) steers was safely camped. His apparent
laziness was due to moon-blindness, which he had
acquired long previously as a result of sleeping in
the open, face up to a full moon. By daylight his
eyesight was quite normal, but he could see nothing
on the brightest of nights. I was led to understand
years ago that anyone afflicted with moon-blindness
sometimes suffered from a permanent twist of the
head in the direction from which the moon shone at
the time of the affliction. The only way to undo the
kink was to cross the Equator; but I wouldn’t like
any moon-twisted citizen to make the trip until he
verifies the information. It may not be correct.
THE SEASIDE CHAPERONE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The boarding house I’m living at
Is nestled down beside the sea ;
The living there is none too fat.
The “comforts” do not comfort me.
I stay because one girl divine
Who oft I fancy may be mine
Goes dipping in adjacent brine.
Her name is Maud. Her sister Jane
Goes bathing, too. My one regret
Is that when dabbling in the main
They have with them their Auntie Bet.
Aunt Bettie’s over fifty-five,
She has a figure like a hive—
On Gospel tracts it seems to thrive.
By moonlight in the silver wave
We splash about, a sportive set.
I'm given here no space to rave,
But Maudie’s figure when it's wet
Would stir a saint. Not much is seen ;
I get one glimpse of beauty’s queen,
Then Auntie wallows in between.
{.ast night I cried, all patience spent:
“For pity’s sake abandon Aunt!”
Her blue eyes were so innocent.
“Oh, no,” she said, “I really can’t.
She guards our dear aquatic larks.
Wherever Auntie Bet embarks
There's not the smallest fear of sharks!”
VICTORIA

“Settled” : Some little time ago I was
travelling by rail from Atherton to Mareeba (N.Q.),
and noticed a particularly noxious-looking China-
man drinking from the pannikin provided for the
use of passengers at the station tank. I drew the
station-master’s attention to the pollution. “What
can I do?” said he. “He’s a passenger the same as
you are.” I didn’t see my noxious friend again till
the other day, after his arrest for butchering an-
other Ah Sin. Two days later I found that he had
been discovered to be a mere shell —Chinaman outside
and leprosy within. He was shooed into the morgue
to await trial. Isn’t Bruce Smith due for a pre-
sentation mug or something? If so, how about that
station pannikin for a loving cup ?
“P.A.Q.” : When John Maori gets into the
habit of doing things in a certain way, it takes
some argument to make him change. Was keeping
store at Taneatua (M.L.) when the postage rate
was reduced from 2d. to Id. Timi Tapu, a casual
customer of mine, always would, and did, up till the
time they held a tangi over him, affix 2d. to his
missives. My first argument with him on the sub-
ject was a failure; it was like this: “Homai te
tamp,” says Timi, placing a threepenny-piece on the
counter. I gave him a penny one and 2d. change,
at which he remarked: “E hoa, no good this one,
kapai te tuppny.” “That’s all right, Timi,” I
said; “penny sufficient for letters now.” “How te
way that, he always te tuppny, ain’t he?” “Not
now, Timi; Government reduce the rate; all let-
ters penny now.” “Py gorry that no good to me,
what te ’ell te Gumment he know about my retter,
I write it, don’t I? You pull my reg, I tink. You
gimme te tuppny tamp quick, else I write te Cho
Ward, you get te pruddy sack.” At that threat I
gave him one which he affixed with much nressing
with the handkerchief, remarking the while, “No
good te tarn penny, kapai te tuppny that te besh
way, he go rike ’ell now, I pet.”

“A. Borig”: Re long-distance country
Bungs. The 39-year record of Rush, of the Iluka
pub, was easily beaten by the late Kimberley, of
Collector (N.S.W.). He told me he had been be-
hind the pump for 52 years, and he didn’t die for
awhile after that. When he went aloft to assist
in the nectar department, his widow kept the family’s
name over the door for a few years longer. The
total would thus run to about 60 years.

“Leichhardt” : For casualness Black Sister
beats all other performers. Take the case of Scraggy.
Scraggy was the house gin on an outstation below
Cloncurry. Incidentally, she weighed 731 b. As she
was about to become a mother, the boss decided to
send her to the head station, where she would have
the benefit of the station gins’ attention. A dray
was going in for rations, and with Scraggy aboard
started off. I was the driver. Crossing the Clon-
curry River, Scraggy asked me to pull up while she
visited a well-known water-melon patch. I am pre-
pared to swear that she wasn’t absent more than 45
minutes. In view of subsequent revelations, the time
should be borne in mind. Next day Scraggy was
busy at the washtub. Noticing her condition, I
naturally inquired after the piccaninny. “Oh, I bin
bury that fella longa sand yesterday,” she said with
a laugh. I had my own suspicions, but asked no
questions—then. Later on, Scraggy admitted to me,
with equal frankness, that she had been a mother, a
baby-slayer and a gravedigger all in those short 45
minutes.
* * * *
“L. 8.”: Re “Coola’s” remarks on the
pest of “bluebottles” (Portuguese men-of-war), now
infesting the N.S.W surfing resorts. He mentioned as
the best remedy he had encoun-
tered the leaves of a shrub used
by a couple of Binghis. As
“Coola” did not know the name
of the shrub, I will say for gen-
eral information that it is com-
monly known as the “pigface.”
Almost instantly it allays the
agony of the virus of the “blue-
bottle’s” streamers, and for scor-
pion or poisonous spider stings
or bites it is equally efficacious.
Its leaves are triangular and
multi-colored, thick and fleshy,
and it may be found growing in
plenty at Watson’s Bay and along
the cliffs where the Shocked have
not squashed it out of existence
watching the female surf-bather.
In the hot summer months it
produces a sub-acid fruit, purple
in color and not unlike the fruit
of the prickly-pear, but with a
corona of beautiful scarlet leaves
or petals. It is also a splendid
febrifuge, and cattle love it.
Solander, the botanist, considered
it “one of the most valuable
plants in the universe, with a world-wide habit, yet
almost unknown except to the botanist.”

“Yannatherra”: It was a slack hour in
the traffic, and the day was hot and dry. Constable
Murrphy, meditating a beer epic, languidly altered
his vision centre from the corner pub to the passing
corporation water sprinkler. Suddenly a large, brown
Billjim rushed wildly across the blocks, arms ges-
ticulating, whiskers waving adieux to his wind-dis-
placed hat, and his dust-stained voice vainly endeavor-
ing to attract the attention of the sprinkler-driver.
With a curse Murrphy was after him, and at 135
yards had locked Billjim in the official embrace.
“Gorstruth!” panted the indignant visitor from Dry-
berra, pointing to the sprinkler, “he don’t seem to
know its leakin’!”
“Wolfram”: Visits of men of religion to
the Papuan goldfields are few and far between, but,
when they do eventuate, the diggers celebrate the
occasion in a befitting manner. It was a well-known
cleric (familiarly referred to and sometimes ad-
dressed by the miners as the “Bish”) who sent word
to the Yodda Valley field that he would arrive there
on a certain date. The news was passed around, and
most of the men from Finnegan’s, Hudson’s,
McLaughlin’s and other outlying parts assembled at
one of the two stores on the main field to meet him.
All hands were attired in their best Yodda finery—
pyjama pants, flannel and handkerchief. Red Bill,
Klondike, Black Bill and a few others, who formed
the reception committee, formally welcomed the Bish,
after which he informed the crowd that the main
object of his visit was to hold service and read
prayers over the graves of those miners who had
died at and been buried on the Yodda. All then
adjourned to a place where most of the deceased
Yodda miners had been interred. The Bish held ser-
vice and read prayers, during which the congregation
observed proper decorum. When the ceremony was
completed, Red Bill stood up on a wooden headpiece,
and, after a brilliant oration, in which he exhorted
his listeners to give up strong drink, etc., he pro-
posed a hearty vote of thanks to the Bish for his
offices. This was carried unanimously, and with
three cheers. The bewildered cleric did not respond
but walked silently away from the cemetery. So far
as I know, he did not visit the Yodda after that,
which was a great disappointment to the boys, who
had decided to entertain him at a smoke concert on
his next visit.
* * *
Nnel 1 : Saw a wild and woolly fight in
the scrub just beyond St. Hilda Bluff to-day. The
parties had come from one of the northern suburbs
to settle a little difference over “a bit of skirt ”
Denny was a hefty bloke sft. lOin. in his bluchers ;
Artie was not more than sft. 7in., and slimly built.
Artie copped out from “go,” when Denny swiped
him a right swing that took two complete twists out
of him, and left him perched on his shoulder-blades,
looking for his enemy among the clouds. After that
Artie was receiver-general through seven long, ex-
hausting rounds, and nothing could be said for the
victim but that he was tough. He had contracted
two black eyes, by the way, and his left ear, where
Denny’s patent, circular swing connected, was like a
ripe purple plum. A long cut over the right eye, a
puffed nose, a split chin, sundry bruises, and two
missing teeth completed the tout ensemble. When
Artie came up for round nine Denny’s disgust was
terrible. “Yah!” he snorted, “kindness is jist thrown
away on you,” and he whirled in a fist that might
have shifted the rock of ages. It hoisted Artie into
an adjoining state, that of unconsciousness. While
the combatants were receiving the attention of their
seconds, a plump damsel came puffing from a furni-
ture van in the middle distance. Several friends
warded her off from the gruesome spectacle. She
was the bone of contention. “Who’s won?” gasped
the bone. “Artie,” replied a facetious youth. “He’s
got poor Denny all broke up.” The lady squealed,
“Oh, the wretch!” she said. “The beast! Poor
Denny—my poor Denny ! ” She broke through into the
field of action. There lay Artie in his gore; there
stood Denny open-armed, the pride of the victor in
his only good eye. The lady shot one look at the
vanquisher and another at the vanquished ; then her
pent feelings surged into a yell, and, descending
upon five and a half pounds of metal, she put it in
with such deftness that Denny keeled over, dead to
the world. After which Madge prostrated herself
upon the hero, who had fallen in fair fight.
“Artie! Artie!” she said; “oh, my poor, poor
Artie !”
“S.T.N.”: There has been a strange mor-
tality among the perch in Lake Bonnev (S.A ) hun
dreds of fish drifting ashore dead, without any a n
parent trouble except that the eyes are gone. “Tal'*. 1
out by birds,” was the local verdict; but Mclntosh
Chief Inspector of Fisheries, puts it down to shallow
flats with decaying vegetable matter. When a fish
gets into such shallows, and a heat-wave sends tem-
peratures up, he has ophthalmia, and presently the
eye bursts. Mclntosh says he has seen the exact
process in an aquarium in Italy.
“A.L.B. : I claim to have discovered the
most civilised bird. It inhabits North Queensland
scrubs. Its nest has one entrance only, which is fitted
with a hinged door. The nest is suspended from a
bush like an inflated paper bag. When the bird turns
in, it locks the door and slumbers in peace. It is
a little brown bird, the size of a tom-tit, with con-
fiding eyes and a long beak. It lives on honey. It
was a lady bird I saw ; and as there was nobody to
introduce us, and I’m a Wowser, I don't know her
name.
“Eddyson”: George Edward C'riff, 0 f
Wylow, was a smart settler, with 300 acres of
pretty fair land and a terrific supply of resolution.
His greatest misfortune was that he had no capital
worth mentioning, beside which he was not brought
up to the land, having been a type-snatcher in the
chy till the machine-setter gave him a set-back. But
he was young, and strong, and bent on getting
there. Among his other requirements, after three
years battling with the great Australian gum, was
a wife. For his own part he considered Nellie
David admirably adapted, and told her so. M ; ss
David said she liked his cheek ; but George Edward,
being a persistent bloke, hoped to make her liking
more general. The man who had overthrown and dis-
posed of 600 giant gums was not easily discon-
certed. He kept at Nellie, although this necessi-
tated his fighting one or the other of her five big
brothers two or three times over, and placed him in
some jeopardy from old David’s gun. It will be
gathered that there was some objection to George
Edward Griff. The Davids had extensive lands, and
innumerable cows. They had crops and pigs, and
money abided with them. Criff, as a penniless
settler, was distinctly no class. Some people may
excuse the young man for not seeing it in that
light. He missed no opportunity. Old David
started a law suit against him about a boundary in
the hope of breaking his spirit, and when one of the
David boys could not lick him two did not blush
to make the effort. Mrs. David scalded him once
when he had the impudence to invade the front
verandah after Nellie, and Mr. David shot him once
when he was lingering in the moonlit orchard, “with
a view to the above.” The Davids bought a large
and vicious mastiff for George’s discouragement,
and twice the gallant youth was injured in tooth-
and-nail conflicts with the abnormal tyke, while the
David family stood round and heartened on their
horrid dog. Then Criff, with inconceivable impu-
dence, publicly asked David for his daughter’s hand.
It happened in Proctor’s bar in Wylow township.
David in his just wrath clubbed George Edward
with a crutch like a hockey stick, and threw a kero-
sene lamp at him as he withdrew from the stirring
scene. It was just ten days later that Criff blun-
dered on the quartz reef on his block. He quietly
secured all he could hold, put his friends on to
other leases, and then made disclosures. The out-
crop was just putrid with gold. It blazed in the
sunlight like the Federal note reserve. Old David
went with, the crowd to gape at it. He gaped for
an hour, and his mouth dripped all the time. Then
he limped to George Edward Criff. He took his
hand. “Eh, man, man,” he said, “we’re jist as
keen as ever about your marryin’ our Nell, mind
veh.”
THE ART OF THE WOWSER.
Mixing the color.
“Bulloak” : I crowd on to my daily crust
by farming in the Mallee. Had a chap and a bloke
doing a bit of fencing lately. The township is five
miles away, and every Saturday the two went there
to buy a pair of boots. They had only one horse be-
tween them. The chap first mounted the moke ami
tore like a bush fire for about half a mile, where he
hitched the horse to a fence and walked. W hen the
bloke who was padding the hoof reached the nag
he mounted, and laid on the flail till he got half a
mile in front of the chap, when he in turn tethered
Nugget to the fence and walked. This business was
repeated till the pub yard was reached. The only
thing the chap and the bloke have ever disagreed
over was when Bill had the horse for the last mile
two “Saturdees” running. You see, Bill was thus
able to swallow five beers before Jim could clutch
a glass. Coming back, Bill rode the horse and Jim
had the mount on one of the snakes they brought
home with them.
CALL OF THE SAP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A wind is blowing from the downs,
With fragrance of the flowering tree;
A call to come from pa veil towns
And hear the songs of land and sea ;
To smell the wild boronia buds,
To see the glory of the heights
And hear the tumult of the floods,
Or whispering peace of summer nights.
So leave the desk and close the door,
And sling your rod and rifle on ;
The wild duck chatter by the shore,
And southward string migrating swan ;
So leave the counter and the till,
And put the saddle on the mare ;
The new pink growth is on the hill;
The new crisp sweetness in the air.
This desert-wind brings in the tale
Of how the sun has turned the yea?,
And many buds are bursting pale,
And watercourses rippling clear ;
Of grasses clothing slope and strand.
And birds re-plumaged all anew;
So take your hunting-gear in hand,
And I will out and off with you.
There comes a time to each and all,
Who live in webs men-spiders spin,
When wild and wayward voices call
And wake the shuttered soul within ;
When open space and windy tree
Rouse up the dormant imp of change—-
So bolt the doors, and come with me
To chase the wind across the range !
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.

ITEMS
Three books, mostly about Victorian
courts, Judges, barristers and attorneys, are
under way. J. L. Forde, a well-known
journalist, has a history of the Supreme
Court nearly through the press. C. P. Wil-
liams, formerly Clerk of the Melbourne In-
solvency Court, has the material for another
the prospectus was issued a while ago
And ex-Justice Holroyd, who was 25 years
on the Bench and 26 at the Bar, is writing
his recollections. Madden, C.J., lately looked
through Forde’s proofs, and gave the infant
his blessing in advance.

wood and sister to squatter Everard
Browne, has gone to Rhodesia to collect
tour buckets of local color for her novel

an Englishman, could not have collected
more than a pint of local color.

in S. Aus. Her husband brought river
steamers from Scotland, in pieces, in 1653

them m through the dangerous Murray
Mfluth. ms wife followed him out in 1857.

up and down the river, from Ecliuca to Gool-
wa. James Ritchie died in 1883, but at
least tour sons are owners and captains on
