From the Oldest Inhabitant
Apropos Felix Clewett (B. 20/2/’l3). In the old
sandhills cemetery, replaced by Sydney Railway Sta-
tion, were many notable monuments sculped by
George Clewett, who had his monumental mason’s
yard in Bathurst-street west. In the late ’3o’s he
went into Lower Pitt-street. Some of the very fine
monuments with his sign manual bore nothing else,
by the way. One of them was supposed to be over
the remains of Dr. Wardell, shot by runaway convicts
on his estate at Petershamj another was supposed to
keep down the bones of Captain Logan, murdered by
blacks at Moreton Bay. The late Queensland M.L C.
was born in Sydney in 1836, while the sculptor was
at the top of his fame.
T _ Joha Roberts, who died at Branston-road,
Kew Gardens, London, the other day, aged
84, was the last surviving partner of the old
bydne.y firm of goldsmiths, etc., Flavelle and
Roberts. In the ’4o’s the firm was Flavelle
and Brush ; the E., S. and A. Bank now covers
the site of their shop. In 1850 the firm was
Flavelle Brothers, opticians and watch-
makers, 478 George-street North. These
premises were opposite the Military Bar-
racks. In the mid ’so’s John Roberts,
importer, of Jamieson-street, joined the firm
which became first Flavelle Brothers and
Co., and then, as it still is. Flavelle and
Roberts.
“Nichiquin”: One more instance of the
terrors of the North. Inspector Foelsche,
after spending more years at Darwin than
the oldest Northern Territory drunk can re-
member, retired a while ago, and moved
south to Adelaide. But he decides that no
reasonable white man can put up with such
climate, and so he goes back to his
old-age sanatorium. They all do it sooner
or later. A man may leave the N.T. and
llve ,n e l sewhere for y ear s, but eventually
you 11 find him back there. Like the “East”
it calls.” And when it calls —well, we go
that’s all.
More Revelations from “Jeames”:—
R° rd Ronald Gower, who went bankrupt last year
(the whole of his patrimony of £40,000 having
been lost for him by F. R. Shacldeton, brother of
the explorer), has had a busy life. He is an art
cntm of distinction, a sculptor, a fine writer, and
the author of several biographies. But he will
chiefly go down to fame as the man who slew the
top hat. For 20 "ears he has waged merciless war
against this senseless garment. At a period when
no self-respecting Englishman dared to appear dur-
lng the Season in the Row in the morning, or in
Bond-street in the afternoon, beneath anything but a
hat of stove-pipe shape and weight, Lord Ronald
brazenly, frequented these places clad in a mere
slouch. ’ At first his action drove right-thinking
people to a state of frenzy. By the end of the ’9o’s
one found Londoners, here and there, who were dis-
posed to argue that there might be something in
his notion. Lord Rothschild compromised by having
a top hat made for himself of white straw. He
claimed that it supplied the essential air of dignity
and responsibility, without bearing unduly on the
fevered brow of the wearer. However, the straw
topper failed to boom. To-day the silk hat is a
rarity m London, even during the height of the
season Its vogue is moribund. Presently, without
a doubt, it will join the Georgian stock and the tie-
wig, both of which adornments were too infernally
uncomfortable to last.
Lord Ronald’s examination in bankruptcy dis-
used the bland casualness in regard to business
affairs _ which characterises so many artists. He
unquestioningly handed over all his money to Shackle-
ton, and told him to do with it what he thought
best. It went into the International Financial De-
velopment Corporation, which paid about two divi-
dends (carpers say out of capital), and then burst
with a report that reverberated over half England.
Shacldeton is alleged to have defrauded a number of
others, of various impressive sums. Also, he is
credited with having, while employed about the
castle in Dublin, purloined the Crown jewels. The
latter accusation, however, has no significance.
Almost everyone who was in Ireland within six
months of the disappearance of these famous orna-
ments, has been more or less roundly charged with
stealing them. Not to have been in some wav asso-
ciated with this strange affair is, in Dublin Society
circles, equivalent to the charge of being an obscure
outsider.
DIANA AND THE CHASE.
VIOLENT WOMEN. —THE LATEST SCHEME TO
KIDNAP MINISTERS.—GROWING INDIGNATION.
London, Feb. 22.
The Standard states that the women’s suffragists are
plotting to kidnap several Cabinet Ministers.
An Australian's conception of the English
Suffragette.
John Redmond, leader of the Irish party,
claims (or somebody claims it for him) to
be a descendant of Raymond the Fat, the
large and warlike Norman person who came
over to Ireland with men-at-arms and archers
in advance of Strongbow. Raymond
did things in a thoroughgoing fashion, and
when he made a haul of prisoners he hurled
them over high cliffs into the sea. It is pro-
bably part of the old scoundrel’s return for
his misdeeds in life that he looks out from
his seat on a red-hot pinnacle of Tophet to
see his Irish descendant in the direct line
nghting the battles of his country against
the more or less Norman persons who sit
impotent in the faded House of Peers.

CRAWFORD VAUGHAN , Treasurer in
Veran's ex-Ministry (S.A.)
Bishop Williams, of Maoriland, is pro-
bably one of the very few people living who
saw Samuel Marsden. In 1837 the old man
who had started the Maori mission at the
Bay of Islands, and who was incidentally
the cause of Maoriland becoming a British
possession and Hongi getting firearms with
which to decimate the Maori race, paid a
last visit to his brown friends in the Iloki-
anga district. Whatever else may or may
not be true about Marsden, he gained the
confidence of the Maori to a remarkable ex-
tent. At the Anglican General Synod at
Nelson, when the Marsden centenary com-
memoration was • being discussed, it was
stated that the veteran was in the habit of
making the journey from the Bay of Islands
to the Thames on foot at a time when the
bad habits of the native race were very bad
indeed and long pig was regarded rather as
a necessary than a luxury.
Archbishop O’Reil.y (Adelaide) hardly
knows himself how he came to miss being a
soldier. His _ father and grandfather were
both officers in Wellington’s army, and he
was born and bred in barracks. Three of
his uncles fought in Canada, a brother went
through the Indian Mutiny and a nephew
was in the United States service during the
fighting in Cuba. For his own part, all he
can do now is to soak himself in military
history.
An interesting paper-covered booklet, pub-
lished in Melbourne in 1866, is “Flowers of
Rhetoric, comprising a Series of Recitations
delivered at the Fitzroy Penny Readings.”
The “Flowers” are Macaulay’s “Horatius,”
Aytoun’s “The Execution of Montrose” from
“The Lays of Scottish Cavaliers,” and Tenny-
son’s “May Queen.” Thus was the imported
population of Melbourne entertained on win-
ter evenings half a century ago. Times have
changed!
THE DEVOTED LOVER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
,, Ij 0 ND .0. N > Tuesday.—The suffragette meeting in
the Pavilion Music Hall was a stormy succession of
tights with male interrupters, who successfully re-
sisted ejectment. Mrs. Drummond, appealing for
contributions to the fighting fund, said it did not
require much to buy gunpowder. One gift was pro-
mised—a quantity of paraffin.— Cable.
Chocolates and roses—trinkets dear and bright
Only vex My Dearest. She wants dynamite!
Dynamite and petrol! Paraffin and tow !
Laden with my love gifts, cautiously I go!
Pockets stuffed with powder—kit bag full of death !
Thus I lightly step it, daring to draw breath !
She will run to meet me, when my load is seen,
Calling me her “sweetest powder magazine.”
Should a small boy bump me, I will surely die!
So, with dreadful caution, I go shuffling by.
Until I’m unloaded, she about my neck
Will not hang in rapture, lest I come to wreck.
Chocolates and roses—trinkets bright and dear—
(Heaven, these fine evenings, seems absurdly near!)
Only fret My Dearest! But her eyes are bright
When I shuffle towards her with some dynamite!
N.S.W.

The WILD CAT

The Insurance Office of Australia,
Ltd (Sydney), which was founded in 1910, and began
with a deficit of £5250 representing preliminary
exes.:—
Towards
Nov., Profits. Dividends. Reserves. Deficit.
-• ~ £5250
1911 .. *4115 Nil £4115 1135
Dec., Loss.
1912 .. 436 Nil _ 1571
Twelve months ago it was pointed out here that the
alleged profits for the co.’s first year were probably
fictitious. “Obviously when a policy has been only
three months in force, three-fourths of the premium,
less the office loading to cover expenses, has not been
earned, and ought to be reserved to cover the unex-
pired part of the risk.” In those first annual
accounts it was assumed that the entire premium had
been earned. Hence a net profit of £4115 was
shown. It made things look all right then; but it
did it at the expense of the second year. And the
second year is doing some of the same sort of thing
at the expense of the third or current year. For this
reason: the business was nearly doubled last year,
and thus, though the whole of the first-year pre-
miums on that new business was credited to last
year, the losses to be set against those premiums
will largely occur during this current year. The
proper thing to do, of course, is to put aside a
reserve for unearned premiums. The usual thing for
established offices is to maintain a reserve equal to
40 p.c. of the premium income. Suppose, however,
that 20 p.c. was provided in this case, though that
isn’t enough. Then the situation would be as follows:
First. year, profit £4115, less £5190 of unearned
premiums, leaving a net loss of £1075; second year,
loss £436 plus £4258 (being 20 p.c. of the addi-
tional premium income), making a total loss for
that year of £4694. Those figures are certainly not
very cheerful. But there is no cause to feel down-
hearted about it. The excellent feature is that the
co. in two years has got a net premium income of no
less than £47,241; and it has naturally cost a lot
of money to get a business of that size. ' A fire busi-
ness certainly isn’t quite the same as a life business,
because a fire policy has to be renewed every year;
nevertheless, once got, much of the fire business
sticks almost as tightly as life business does, and in
life business it is reckoned pretty good if the first-
year’s premiums cover the cost of getting the policy.
If this office can hold £30,000 of its present business,
then with average loss and exoense rates there should
be a profit of at least £5OOO in it, equal to 6 p.c.
on the capital.
The next little table shows some other move-
ments :
Net Loss. Expense
Premiums. Losses. Expenses. Rate. Rate.
1911 £25,951 £6,748 £17,537 26.0 p.c. 67.5 p.o.
1912 47,241 22,443 27,845 47.5 „ 58.9 „
The first year’s rates are fictitious. The loss rate
for that period was less than normal, because few
if any of the policies expired at the end of the
year to which the premiums are credited. So also
the loss rate for the second year is under-stated
because a large proportion of the policies were
new, and the risks covered by them didn’t expire
with that year. Yet, even a 47.5 p.c. loss rate is
unsatisfactorily high. The expense rate is high,
too, but that is explained by previous remarks.
So much for the position from the shareholders’
point of view. The policy-holder can be much
surer about things. The liquid assets run to
£57,356 in excess of visible liabilities, and in
addition, there is George-street (Sydney) property
valued in the books at £15,081, and worth no
doubt more. Policy-holders are therefore .very well
protected: assuming that the risks are reasonably
well distributed, present policy-holders and a whole
lot of new policy-holders need have no doubts
about the co.’s financial position. The first few
years of a fire office are invariably a time of
struggle. But this co. starts off its third year
with an unusually big business, and business is the
foundation on which profits are built. Given capable
management the future is therefore bright.

The special reserve, it will be noted, is the accumu-
lated profit on sales completed. But there are many
time-payment sales; also fractions of many proper-
ties have been sold. The policy with regard to the
former has been not to include the profit until the
purchase has been completed; and where a part of a
property has been sold at a profit, part or all of that
profit has generally been used to write down the
unsold fraction to a nominal figure. No doubt this is
why the report mentions that “the realisation of
the remaining properties at reasonable prices will
very materially increase the Reserve Fund.” Mean-

In previous tables the rate of dividend has been cal-
culated on the amount of capital actually paid. This
method has now become awkward, because, while the
original £75,000 of capital participated in a £
tor £ bonus, the subsequent £54,000 of capital has
had no bonus; and, in fact, some of it paid a pre-
mium. On the average, the dividend is now equal to
about 10$ p.c. on the actually paid capital. But while
some shareholders (the originals) get for the half-year
a dividend at the rate of 14 p.c. p.a., the last lot get
a shade under 6 p.c. p.a.
There is another way of pitting the last half-year’s
results: you might scratch out the line, “£5OOO
written off,” and then give the profits as £3715.
This is the position. In the previous accounts, £5OOO
was credited to a Special Renewals Fund. Now this
fund has been transferred bodily to Profit and Loss ;
but as “eight vessels of the fleet out of 11 have
undergone surveys and overhauls during the six
months at a very considerable outlay,” and as that
outlay has apparently been charged to Profit and Loss,
it seems fairer to write off the £5OOO rather than
charge the whole lot against the half-year’s revenue.
Even then a big shrinkage has occurred in the pro-
fit- The directors explain it as due to the afore-
said “considerable outlay’* plus “higher rates of
wages .increased cost of labor and material detention
m loading at Newcastle, and shrinkage of general
cargo trade, with no compensatory increase of
freights, . etc.”—and the remark about no increase in
freights is put in black letters, by way of a hint or
something. Still, almost everything depends upon
the amount the co. has spent on overhauls ; and there
is no way of getting at that. But if a co. overhauls
e *£ht-el e venths of its fleet in one half-vear, and in-
cludes the outlay with current exes., it can’t expect
to show dazzling profits. It will be safer to wait for
another term or two before shedding any tears or in-
dulging in any curses.
One thing is certain: the co. has had a wonderfully
fine 17 years of life. Formed in 1895 by the amal-
gamation of three concerns, it was given a capital of
£75,000, based on the valuation of its assets. About
three years ago another £20,000 was got in. Last
half-year £34,000 was got in (in £lO shares), with
£6BOO of premiums. Total capital actually paid
(including- the original £75,000) £135,800. Now
consider this:—
Dec.,’o4. June.’l2. Dec., T 2.
Steamships and other
non-liquid assets.. £127.118 £256,144 £330,090
Liquid assets .. 49,052 70,237 53 189
Liabilities .. .. 12,796 28,395 53,’436
Liquid assets practically balance the liabilities; so
the co. has steamships and other non-liquid assets
standing at £330,090 to represent its £135,800 of
actually paid capital. Even if a good slice should
come off that £330,090 on account of depreciation
not covered by renewals and appreciation, this isn’t
a result to cry over, especially in view of the sub-
stantial dividend that has been paid throughout.
That things are not quite as good now as they have
been is highly probable; and it is equally probable
that they are not going to be as good. It is likely
to be with this co. as it is with a heap of industrial
cos. They fell into a patch of clover and had it to
themselves for a while. Then the work-people and
others noticed that the cos. were getting fat, and
came up for a cut. In most cases they got it, in
the shape of increased wages, prices of materials, etc.
But, just as the cos. had the rich things to themselves
for a while, so it is probable that wages and other
costs will continue on the higher scale for some time
after the rich clover-patch has gone. So it is possible
or more than possible—that when the tide of pros-
perity turns, the profits of such industrial cos. will
for a time be as abnormally depressed as for a while
they were abnormally inflated.

The amounts shown as written off are added to some-
thing called variously a Depreciation and a Depre-
ciation Insurance Fund. Maintenance is charged to
Profit and Loss; but, apart from that fund, appar-
ently nothing is written off against wear and tear.
Hence in the foregoing this fund is not included
with reserves. The paid capital is £60,000, and the
liabilities amount to £5856. This money and the
reserves are chiefly represented by property, plant
rolling stock, goodwill and water right, all thrown
into one heap and valued at £77,228. Stock, ad-
vances and other assets total £19,726. There isn’t
much useful information to be got out of that; the
paper assets may be £2OOO or they may be £25,000
Two definite points, nevertheless, rise above the ’haze
—the small amount of debt and the wide margin of
profit in excess of the requirements of a 10 p c
dividend.

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

Sydney Water and Sewerage Board for elevated re-

tions. Apply, Wm. Adams and Co., Ltd., Sydney,
Melbourne and Brisbane.*

In all smithing work high-class iron can be more
safely manipulated at varying temperatures than
steel or ordinary iron. It is for this reason that
Best Yorkshire Farnley Iron is preferred by engi-
neers, where toughness, great strength and close
grain are needed. Whatever the specification, Iron
of the Farnley type does not vary, but is simply the
best that can be produced from what is really
wrought iron. “Farnley” Bars of all sections sup-
plied from local stock bv Sole Agents of the Farnley
Iron Comp., Ltd. Bell's Asbestos, Ltd., 315 Kent
Street, Sydney.*
From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yds. of
road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yd., and the cost of the spalls per cub. yd. averaged
2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub. yd.,
broken and spread. Their last contract under the
hand-breaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per cub.
yd., stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yd. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stoneerusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [3

For some years past the co. has had a debenture
debt of £ 50,000 ; but this has been got rid of dur-
ing the year by the issue of £72,401 of pref. capital,
of which, however, £79 was unpaid at the end of the
year. The change from debentures to capital ex-
plains some of the spring in profits, because hitherto
the interest on the borrowed money has, of course,
been deducted from the profits, whereas now the in-
terest on the corresponding amount is included with
the dividends. Of ordinary capital there is £94,886
in £lO shares paid to £9 10s„ and £76,950 in
£lO shares fully paid, and £52,741 in £lO shares
paid to £9—with a few pounds representing unpaid
calls in most cases. This, also, gives the accounts
an untidy appearance. As for the true inwardness of
the business, it is hard to arrive at anything very
definite. The reserves certainly have been growing
fast; but the question is how much (if any) of those
reserves are a set-off against accumulating rust and
decay. Most gas cos. provide for that depreciation
by paying for extensions out of surplus revenue, in
addition, of course, to paying for current renewals.
Sometimes if they want to hide profits, they stuff
away into extensions far more than is necessary to
counterbalance wear and tear. In that case, the ten-
dency is for the book value of the assets —that is to
say, the assets which have been charged to capital
account —to show an increasing percentage of earn-
ings. In 1907 this co.’s fixed assets were valued at
£241,120, and the revenue was £80,678 = 33i p.c.
Now the fixed assets are £421,653, and the revenue
£97,124 =23 p.c. That slump certainly doesn’t look
as though any revenue had been stuffed away in ex-
tensions, etc. On the contrary, it is plain that the
capital isn’t earning anything like what it used to.
The business may be, and no doubt is, solid and very
profitable; but. all the same, some of the reserves
are under suspicion. And that being so, some of the
alleged profits are under suspicion also.

There has been another issue of shares on terms that
are not sufficiently explained. There were already
70,000 ordinaries fully paid to 10s., and 50,000 pref.
10s. shares paid to 9s. Now another 80,000 pref.
10s. shares have been issued, 72,883 of them paid to
ss. and 7117 fully paid. Instead of capitalising re-
serves or issuing bonus shares, the plan was adopted
of issuing the bulk of these at a discount (unmen-
tioned), and the balance were sold at a premium (also
unmentioned). The aggregate discount was £14,662
more than the aggregate premium, and this amount
of paper capital is represented by tacking a corres-
ponding figure on to the book value of the fixed
assets. It is a clumsy, roundabout method, adopted,
no doubt, with the object of making things as
obscure as possible. The frank way of putting it
has been shown in the table, after an amount of
figuring that ought to have been unnecessary. The
plain fact seems to be that 72,883 bonus shares have
been issued paid to 55., while 7117 fully-paid 10s.
shares have been issued —probably to the public—
at 10s. premium. The ultimate effect of it all is
that South Brisbane is now paying 10 p.c. dividend
on £18.221 of capital which never came out of the
shareholders’ pockets. The consumers supplied certain
profits, which the co. didn’t disclose, and they have
now also the joy of paying dividends on the amount.
The co. has had a rattling good time of late. Take
a 10 years’ run: —-
Dec., 1902. June, 1912. Dec. 1912.
Liabilities .. £20,352 £17,876 £18,098
Liquid assets . . 6,088 11,304 19,030
Deficiency . . £14,264 £6,572
Surplus . . £932
The only capital got in during the 10 years is that
lately raised —-£5000 on first pref. shares, £3558 on
second pref. shares, and £3558 of premiums; total
£12,116. Meantime, as shown just now, a net lia-
bility of £14,264 has been changed into a net liquid
surplus of £932. Also, all extensions and additions
to plant have been paid for; and what they have
amounted to can be judged from the fact that as at
December, 1902. the half-year’s revenue (gas sales,
etc.) was £7262, while for the past half-year it
was £15.469. The future depends a good deal on
what Parliament likes to make it. In N.S.W. Parlia-
ment has mst cut down divs. on gas cos.’ canital now
existing, or to be created inside six months, to 10
p.c., and on future capital to 6 p.c., with slight in-
creases in case of reduction of gas rates; and there
can be no further issue of bonus shares. This fol-
lows pretty closely on English precedent—-and Eng-
lish precedent is supposed to be a sacred thing, some-
how related to the blessed word Mesopotamia.

But as £3793 of the reserves stands to the credit
of a Plant and Gasometer Depreciation Fund, the
true reserves are probably a good deal less; also the

Thus, in 10 years, after paying the dividend and pro-
viding for current repairs, renewals and extensions,
£2998 has been put by—not a bad showing on a
capital of £7500. There may be a general deprecia-
tion which is not covered by current repairs and re-
newals ; but that excess ought to be more than cov-
ered by the extensions. Anyway, the revenue from
the works last half-year was £2233, as against £1603
10 years ago, which suggests that the extensions have
been considerable. On these figures it seems safe to
say that the dividend has been earned, with two or
three thousand to the good, in 10 years.
* * * *
For the man who isn’t in the swim B. H.
Junction North is dangerous goods to touch. There
are pronounced bull and bear sections; and just at
present the bears are squealing because the bulls,
who are in command, didn’t say there wasn’t going
to be a call this month. Of course if a man wants
a parcel of shares for an investment the tricks of
the market don’t matter. But the Junction North
isn’t a mine for the investor. A few months ago it
was estimated that the reserves were 263,000 tons,
and that is only about four years’ supply. The
lode in this mine, however, plays contortion tricks
that would be worth a place in a circus, and it is
quite possible that close up to the neighboring
North’s boundary there is half as much again below
the present levels. But as the body gets shorter and
shorter, it will not pay to go after it to the last
ton. The eo., besides its own tailings and slimes,
has nearly 300,000 tons of slimes bought from other
mines—Block 14, British and North. Some of the
prices paid or to be paid for this stuff were so pre-
cipitous that mill men who hate slimes chuckled at
manager Palmer for a greenhorn. But lots of things
have been found out lately about the treatment of
these zincy materials, and if the markets stand to
the co. it will do far better out of them than this
paper once expected that it would.
The public is coming into Carpathia tin
(N.S.W.) when the men with inside knowledge are
getting out. Not that the insiders know anything
about the bottom. But they do know that (1) there
are 120,000 shares, (2) there is no surface equipment,
including water supply, (3) 80 p.c. of surface tin
deposits don’t go down, and (4) though the surface
stuff is rich it will take a big slice of the profit from
it to provide surface equipment, while another big
slice may go in the search for the lode which may
or may not be there.
Occidental (N.S.W.) reports a further
loss of £2823 for its half-year, but the directors
still continue to pour honey into the badly-soured
milk. Work has again been resumed underground,
after a brief spell, due to the dry weather. The
trouble, however, is not the mine itself, but the
failure of the main treatment plant upon which a
pile of expectations was built. A new metallurgist
was recently called in to patch up the job, and his
remarks aren’t complimentary. The sand-leaching
plant has had to be slung out, and 30 head of
stamps hung up, so as not to swamp the inadequate
slime plant. The Pachuca tanks come in for a
special word of condemnation. When the new man
arrived they were choked up and damaged, so that
the old agitating tanks that hadn’t yet been re-
moved had to go on with the job. The Pachuca tanks
are now declared to be workable, but only at a
devilish cost for compressed air. Chief among the
promised improvements are the concentration arrange-
ments, the roasting of the concentrates produced (in-
stead of shipping them to Cockle Creek), and higher
recoveries and lower costs at the slime plant. Occi-
dental’s past performances, however, don’t encourage
too much faith, though evidently some of the share-
holders still retain a bit, as they have absorbed
the whole of the recent issue of 28,000 contributing
shares of 10s. each.
* * * *
The C.S.A., Cobar Tinto and Cobar Glad-
stone have been put under option in London, and an
expert is to come out and see for himself.
* * * *
Mount Boppy Block 51 (N.S.W.) is to
be registered with a capital of £20,000 in 2s.
shares. Of late there have been some promising
finds, and it is now said that the two south lenses
of ore have been traced to their junction point. The
shaft is to be slapped down another 100 or 200 feet
without delay.
* * * *
Melbourne Cope’s Creek (N.S.W.) went
£559 to the bad last half-year, plus a trifle for de-
preciation of plant; but this still left the co. on
December 31 with about £l2OO of liquid assets
over liabilities. The co. has been picking up odd
bits of tin land, but they are none too plentiful;
and the future is precarious. For a few terms, how-
ever, a comfortable profit should be shown.
The Elsmore, which at one time looked
among the best of the Tingha (N.S.W.) tin-sluicing
propositions, has the want-of-water trouble badly.
At the best of times it costs a lot of money to get
enough water on to the hard ground ; and lately the
water hasn’t been there to get. Nevertheless by
picking its ground, the co. made a profit of £ll4B
for the half-year; but £l7B comes off that for de-
preciation. Costs will continue high; but there are
still some rich runs to sluice.
Instructed, no doubt, by Labor Minister
Collier, the mine inspectors at Kalgoorlie and be-
yond are stirring up the Dago in regard to the lan-
guage test. A number have already been weeded out
of the ranks of underground men, but, according to
local reports, the examination is still perfunctory.
In some mines it is alleged that nearly half of the
workmen are aliens, many of whom are unable to
communicate with British workmen in case of dan-
ger arising from mis-fires, etc. In some cases it is
asserted that Britishers are being dismissed to make
room for the non-English speaking foreigner. A
suggestion has been made that the best way to end
the present state of affairs would be to impose a
heavy fine on the company every time an under-
ground worker is found who is unable to pass the
language test.
Hannan’s Reward (W.A.) decided at its
last meeting to carry out certain prospecting at the
south end of the 400 ft., and preparations are now
being made. This work, like a hundred and one
other bits of development that have been put in
hand during the last year or two at the north or
steerage end of the Kalgoorlie field, is reckoned to
be a matter of great interest by the local papers;
but the results begin to heap scorn on the stock
phrase.
- Another attempt is being made to make
money out of the deep leads at Kanowna, from which
a million sterling is alleged to have already been ex-
tracted. Absence of pumping facilities has hitherto
prevented mining at depth, but these have now been
provided, and things, according to local experts, are
going to hum. There is a doubt, however, about the
gold contents of the remaining wash.
Including 14,223 tons raised by tributors,
Associated Northern (W.A.) treated 20,476 tons from
its Kalgoorlie property during the year ended Sept.
30, and it also milled 7292 tons of purchased ore.
After deducting £4150 for working expenses at Ora
Banda and £845 expenditure on plant at Kalgoorlie,
the accounts show a credit balance for the year of
£16,900. London and Adelaide office expenses reduce
this to £11,518. On Sept. 30 cash and securities
amounted to £54,000, as against an indebtedness of
£12,500; but profit derived from ore treatment at
the Ora Banda show has increased the co.’s credit
balance by about £20,000 since then, and will con-
tinue to do so at the rate of between £6OOO and
£7OOO a month. Operations have again had to be
susDended in Mexico, but negotiations are in progress
to let the Board’s mysterious venture there on tribute.
The co.'s future now mainly depends on Ora Banda,
from which divs. of 4s. to ss. a year are expected.
Bones of managing director’s report in-
corporated in official circular issued to Bullfinch share-
holders: “Am very pleased with the look of the lodes
at the 210 ft. and 310 ft levels. We are, of course,
unable at present to spare much money for the devel-
opment of these bodies, as our finances have all been
required for the purchase and erection of plant. Had
we been able to spend more on development during the
last six months, I am confident that we would have
placed the mine in a brilliant position for the start-
ing of crushing operations. However, the present
position is entirely satisfactory. .. .The general man-
ager is quite confident that he can maintain a 60s.
grade, instead of having, as previously advised, to
limit_ ourselves to a grade between 40s. and 50s. As
working costs are sure to be below £1 per ton,
we are justified in expecting a profit of from £lO,OOO
to £12,000 per month when the plant is in full
operation. This will soon clear us of our liabili-
ties and leave us in a good dividend-paying posi-
tion. .. .Tests made with the ore from the new find
at 310 ft. show that we can obtain a high extraction
with our present plant. This stone will also be of
incalculable value to us in providing a hard rock
to mix with the soft, oxidised material from the
upper 1eve1.... A large tonnage of oxidised ore is
dumped on the surface, which will relieve our min-
ing costs for the first few months.” It would seem
from the above that, even if the best happens, some
time must elapse before the co. gets square, builds
up a cash reserve, and pays its first dividend on its
500,000 or so issued shares.
Central and West Boulder (W.A.) has
acquired 19,000 out of 45,000 issued shares in a
Nigerian tin venture for £6OOO. It previously did
well out of a speculation in the same country.
Hopes are still entertained of making money out of
the co.’s principal lease at Kalgoorlie. Of the other
two one has been thrown up, and the second is more
of a handicap than a help.
When it comes to records, it would be
hard to find a worse alluvial smoodger than the
Great Southern Consols, Rutherglen (Vic.). Some-
how after a disappearance for a year or so, it always
bobs up serenely. Of so little account as a stayer is
the co. that all it shows in Mines Department re-
ports is—calls, 38s. 3d. on 40,000 shares; divs.,
£19,000. The reason of this poor showing is that
the co. has had to mark time till it could get the
plant going at the shaft. Then it had to find out if
the levels had settled down like Rutherglen drives
have the knack of doing. Manager Ramsay now
screams with delight because he could crawl to the
end of the level without pinching his equator. That
is only his first little bit of training. His time will
come when he is in the gutter slopping up expenses
for a weary board to finance.
North Nuggetty Ajax, Daylesford. got p
flat make a day or two ago, and shares were hit up
again. The old Ballarat crowd were quick off to
“cover up” the scrip they had piled into Queen-
street when the latter decided to hold the reins. The
hope has been that the No. 6 level on the north drive
would give good stuff, but the Ajax stuff is not real
stingo there, so Queen-street can’t quit its load of
shares. Yields are now down to under the Mwt.
grade, and it looks as if Bill Rain’s words as to the
show being a 15s. crop, are correct. Nuggetty Ajax,
too, is very feeble. It hooks out only 140 oz. for the
month from 900 tons. This looks as if this show at
last has to admit that successful scratching is over
and that hard calls have to be faced.
* * * *
From the days of W. R. Wilson and
Frank G. Duff onward, something has always hap-
pened to Lyell Blocks (Tas.). All that has been
coughed up so far has been calls, directors’ salaries
and office perks. Even Colin Templeton at length
forsook the board table of the Blocks to rub knees
with Bowes Kelly and Wm. Jamieson in the rich
pastures of the Lyell Co. Yet there are some who
remain faithful to the Blocks, and of these the chief
is manager Ferguson, brought from a gold district
to unravel the Lyell Blocks tangles. Once his
trouble was with copper-bearing clays. When those
were worked out the truth dawned on him that the
North Lyell shoots of ore were underlying into the
Blocks. Shaft sinking then became his programme.
Unfortunately, as The Bulletin was able to point
out, the underlay changed. The Blocks since then
has kept in the vale of calls; and Comstock develop-
ments and the blankness of the crosscut to the North
Lyell boundary at 1000 ft. have made the market as
shy of the mine as a rat of a terrier. Ferguson,
nevertheless, remains hopeful; his conclusions are
that the underlay will change again, and that the
depth of 1200 ft. will bring him the ore. Perad-
venture that is the reason why Neale Wigg has left
the Lyell board, and why Sticht comes to Melbourne
for a serious talk. George Swinburne, by the way,
takes up Wigg’s £lOOO job among the Lyell direc-
tors.

It took all little “Jimmy” Lewis’s
M.C.E.’s and M.l.C.E.’s to snuggle round the awk-
ward corners on the road to Merton’s tin show at
Rosebery (Tas.). Lewis as an acting ’Varsity prof,
always swims deep, but he stuck at the sedimentary
stuff. He commits himself to 200,000 cub. yards of
drift, which he considers bears a greater similarity
to Gladstone, long dead, than to the Bnseis lead.
“The tin is richer nearer the surface than the Bri-
seis,” he adds, but says that from “its fineness the
tin is harder to save and the bulk is much less.
On the lowest assay, allowing for a 50 p.c. loss ot
tin, and with costs kept below Is. per cub. yard,
Lewis gauges that the 200,000 cub. yards he regards
as proved are likely to yield £30,000, of which
£lO,OOO should be profit. As there is plenty of
drift under foot Lewis wants more prospecting and
the verification of his surmises. This is honest if it
isn’t convincing. Still, Merton can console himsel
that he has got enough out of Lewis to entitle him
to get more cash into his show.
Croydon (Q.) still has a kick left It
produced 12,7690 z. of bullion last year, valued at
£37 176. The average yield was 2.280 z. of Croydon
s-old per ton. A recent crushing from the Golden
Gate United returned 6980 z. (value £2 12s. 10d.
per oz.) from 170 tons. A sixpenny dividend has
been paid, and more will probably drop in ; for this
old show, after its long abandonment, is said to
be looking well. #
Charters Towers is in the doldrums, its
three most promising propositions—Brilliant Deeps,
Block and New Queen Cross— having slumped badly
in the local market. The Cross crushed 338 tons for
7290 z ( £2408), with sands and concentrates to
come’ Over £7 per ton is satisfactory; but there
are intrusions of granite and the proved stopmg area
is very limited especially for a co. with 200,000
shares.” Deeps are out of favor because the western
No 5 drive has run into a big reef of low grade
stone. It should, however, be remembered that
blanks are characteristic of this mine.
The Halley Oil Co. has been registered in
Melbourne to bore for oil at Trinity Reach, on the
Victoria River-,in the Northern Territory. The capi-
tal is £lo,o(join 40 shares of £250 each, 20 being
prefs. and 20 ordinary. The directors are Drysdale
Brown (Attorney-General of Vic.), Hagelthorn (Hon.
Minister in the Vic. Government), and Allen Tye, fur-
niture merchant. The other signatories include
William Halley, the Yankee oilman.
“Looker-on” : In the matter of top hamper,
I’ll back the Eleanora (N.S.W.) to beat that
northern copper mine. According to my latest
count, it was taking 10 officers, most of them highly
paid, to look after 26 miners. If the methods of
working were superb, I wouldn’t have anything to
say; but in this multitude of counsellors there is
merely confusion.

Sydney, March 3, 1913.
My Dear Arini. —
There has been a great commotion in
Australia’s biggest home-grown bank —the
“AVales.” A little of it was due to the fact
that general manager John Russell French
was having a 50th banking birthday. But
that didn’t mean changes—only congratula-
tions. The changes came through the loss
of two men at the top—lvey, the deputy
general manager, because he thought he was
too old to stay, and Denison Miller to take
charge of the Commonwealth Bank. Mr.
Ivey’s ytmrk has been cut up between Messrs.
Alford and Edwards, two N.S.W. inspectors;
and Harry Salmon has been brought over
from Dunedin to take Alford’s place in
southern N. S. AA r ales„. where the cows come
from. AA T ith him recently arrived in the
Warrimoo a wife and a brace of daughters
—all with a reputation as Bridge-players in
wet and sodden Dunedin. They leave be-
hind them a young male member of the clan
in the generally ornamental and occasionally
useful office of Judge’s Associate.
Our new Lady Mayoress, Mrs. Cocks, is a
woman with tons of energy. Just now in
the slack season when everybody else is
bending up all energies in the task of keep-
ing cool, Mrs. Cocks is composing a set of
variations on the word “only.” She says
she lias done nothing in February, only “get
up bazaars” (for which she did most of the
needlework). She is giving “only” three
parties before Easter, one a public reception,
one to say good-bye to Lady Chelmsford, and
one to welcome Lady Edeline Strickland.
The party for Lady Chelmsford —which Mr.
Layton frantically begged nobody to say a
word about beforehand, but it’s all over the
town by now—is fixed for Friday. It is
quite a private affair, only 300 guests having
been invited.
Mrs. Scott will arrive in Sydney to-
morrow by the Aorangi. The lost explorer’s
widow will not be altogether without rela-
tives here, and some people expect that she
will stay a while. A sister of Mrs. Scott
is Mrs. Zoe Newham, of Brisbane, who has
a local name as a writer. And the Gover-
nor-designate of Tasmania is married to
her sister-in-law.
Mr. and Mrs. Fitzwilliam AVentworth, of
Vaucluse, leave for England on the 12th inst.
Mr. AVentworth is the eldest son of AY. C.
AVentworth, father of our State Constitution,
and the grandson of D’Arcy AVentworth,
whose name crops up every few furlongs in
the early-day history of Sydney. Mr. Fitz-
william AVentworth is in his 80th year; he
married, in 1868, Mary Jane Hill, a niece of
the wife of the first Conner baronet. The
present Cooper lady, who is returning to
Sydney, is a sister of Mrs. Fitzwilliam AVent-
worth.
An interesting event of last week was the
centenary of the Benevolent Society of
N.S.W. At the annual meeting the Gov. and
Lady Chelmsford presided, and a festive ap-
pearance was given to the building in
Thomas-street by flags and greenery. The
premises of the society, part of which is now
used as the Renwick Hospital for Infants,
are a well set up group of buildings, but as a
quiet, peaceful spot for a hospital they leave
much to be desired. Pigs, vegetables, fowls
and other products are sold in the adjacent
markets. Trains rattle by, and smells of an
unholy blend are wafted upwards on the
dusty breeze. But the authorities want a
central place for the babies, so that the
mothers will have less excuse for not coming
to see them. The doctor and nurses in charge
of these little derelicts strive their utmost to
get the mothers to keep the baby and nurse it
herself. In most cases it is the only chance
the infant has of life. Many of the mothers
are at service, and must let the child go.
They tell a story of one of these babies whose
mother was in service in a godly household with
a reverend at its head. The hospital people
sent her word that she could have the babv
with her, as they had cured it of its little
ailment. But “Master” objected. He couldn’t
have the wailings of an infant disturb him
when he was meditating or writing his ser-
mons. Lady Chelmsford went the round of
the cots on AVednesday, and had a word of
praise for the extreme cleanliness and com-
fort that prevails.
The death is reported from Michelago of
the widow of Alexander Ryrie, who in his
day was a pure merino and M.L.C. She was
Charlotte, eldest daughter of Captain Alured
Tasker Faunce, of the 4th King’s Own Regi-
ment. which was stationed in N. S. AVales in
the ’3o’s. The first of the Ryries came to
Sydney in 1825 as Deputy Commissary Gen-
eral. He had been with Picton at AVaterloo.
His first wife was a sister of Major-General
Stewart, who was Acting-Governor of N. S.
AVales in 1825.
Among the faces of the new peeresses
which more or less adorn the illustrated
papers to hand since the last batch of handles
was tacked on to the commonplace names of
various notables, is the visage of Lady
Sydenham, whose husband was, until
last month, Sir George Sydenham
Clarke, formerly State Gov. of Victoria. The
new peeress used to live in Sydney. She was
a Miss Morant. Her first husband was Cap-
tain Arthur Reynolds.
The Sam Horderns, since their arrival in
Sydney after their trip to Europe with their
small family, are busy getting ready to flit
into their splendid new house at Darling
Point, for the decoration of which —consist-
ing of nearly 100 rooms—they have brought
a whole shipload of magnificence with them.
Mr. and Mrs. Sam Hordern gave a dinner
party on Friday in honor of their new sister-
in-law, Mrs. Lebby, formerly Olga Monie. It
was a family affair, and the acquaintance of
the bride was made by the host and hostess.
The Lebby Horderns were intending to go
to Europe soon, but Mr. and Mrs. Tony are
on the wing, so the newest wedded pair can’t
go, because, under the terms of old Sam Hor-
derns’ will, two brothers must be in Aus-
tralia at the same time. Mrs. Hordern,
senior, is on her way to Australia. The head
of the Hordern family is a good Australian.
He declared recently to a friend that nothing
on earth would induce him to live out of his
own country.
Talking of the high-and-dry Society of
Great Britain, the same authority remarked
that there is nothing underhand or back-
stairs about the selling of position and in-
fluence that is one of the features of this
century. It is purely a business proposition.
A peer, or such, rather down on his luck,
will state his terms for taking a wealthy
person, from furrin parts (Australia is “fur-
rin”) into the holy of holies in the world of
titles. They may compete against each
other. The highest or any tender not neces-
sarily accepted. Most Australians, if they
have not acquired the habit of giving at the
knees in the presence of the mighty, turn
the whole thing down and prefer to travel
strictly on their merits. The other kind of
tlimg is so dirt cheap nowadays.
The Foy family is on the wing. “Sonny,”
his wife aDd other belongings will be off this
month to the land where the sun sits on the
fence all day long and the crows jabber in
Irish. A daughter, now in her preliminary
stages of Convent life, will be taking the
black veil, and to that rather shuddersome
ceremony her family will wend its way.
Mrs. H. H. Osborne, a very pretty bride
of last Easter, has qualified for the baby
bonus, so her bright-faced mother, the youth-
ful-looking Signora de Beaupuis, is preparing
to hear her friends saying “Fancy vou a
grandmother!” without looking too bored
at the oft-repeated compliment. But, hon-
estly, she doesn’t look the part. Mrs. de
Beaupuis has been spending her time between
two hospitals, the one in which are Mrs.
Osborne and infant son, and the other in
which is de Beaupuis himself. The signor,
who is now reported better, has been suffering
from heart trouble. He had been holidaying
in Maoriland, and kept on working at musi-
cal composition instead of merely enjoving
the scenery as a well-conducted tourist
should.
The Cooper estate is getting it in the neck
over the Federal absentee tax. The family
decided to sell Bellevue Hill. Very choice
bits went off by auction a short time ago,
and some of these didn’t fetch such astound-
ing prices either. In order to escape some
of the payments demanded by the tax, the
Coopers are coming to coop here as long as
they can stand it. They have taken Fiona,
Double Bay, renting it from the Misses
Knox, who are going to travel about for
quite a little while.
Mrs. Ifould, our new public librarian,
lends out books to all and sundry, as no doubt
you know. AVhere then, asks the carper, does
the usefulness of the Bush Book Club com"
in? Well, it is like this: Nobody can have
too many books, and the more ways there are
of sending books and magazines into the
backblocks. the better for the inhabitants
thereof. The Bush Book Club had a meeting
at the Town Hall on Friday morning, with
Mrs. Cocks in the chair, and it was
resolved that the best way of holding
the good work together was to have a bail,
which will be pretty soon.
Two well-known Sydney matrons, Mrs.
Finlayson (who used to be Miss Dorothy
Russell French), and Mrs. Lightolles (Miss
MacCallum), are now eligible to claim £5
each from a grateful Treasury. Mrs. Light-
olles found her little daughter on the summit
of Mt. AVilson. Mrs. Finlayson’s baby is also
a girl.

Club on Thursday, to welcome Mr. and Mrs.
Joe Cook. Mrs. Waugh, acting president,
received the guests, also Mrs. Charles
Walker, vice-president, and Mrs. Bogue Luff-

ing dame, is a leading light of her league at
Parramatta. She is a cousin of the late
C. C. Kingston, of South Australia. Mrs.
Charles Walker is sister-in-law of Arthur
Adams.

Mav 28 and the three days thereabouts
have been chosen for the dates of the cele-
bration of the Blue Mountains Crossins:
Centenary. Bonfires are to be lighted at in-
tervals from Emu Plains to Bathurst. The
places named after the three pioneers, Blax-
land, Wentworth and Lawson, will be speci-
ally decorated. Katoomba contributes a
rifle match and illuminations; Blackheath a
concert with illuminations. At the Hydro
at Medlow there will be a big ball- The cere-
mony itself will take place at Mount York.
Girls are taking to all sorts of things to
make a living nowadays. It was reported
recently that Miss G. Leigh, of Supphiretown
(Queensland), has been appointed gem-buyer
on the sapphire fields for Messrs. Hoffman, of
Melbourne. There are two other women
buyers of gems on the fields already. The
secretary of the N.S.AV. Sawmillers’ Associa-
tion is Miss Florrie Ellis, who has held the
post for seven years. Miss Ellis is going off
on a holiday for some time with Mr. and
Mrs. Walter Way.
Just now our dancing teachers and our
musicians are puzzling over the tough problem
that is facing them in the coming by-and-by.
The old dances and the old tunes are quite
“out.” The true inwardness of that “out”
should be expressed by rights in large capi-
tals. It is a sad fact that waltzes and polka-*
no longer tempt the feet of society dancers.
The “Boston” is absolutely “It” in all well-
conducted b ol lrooms. Then there are the
one-step, the “Turkey Trot” and the “Bunny
Hug.” Some ill-informed people declare these
weird steps are not the thing. But they are
the literal caper, and nothing else. In the
winter balls in London, don’t you know,
nobody danced anything but the above. Even
the Gaby Glide is introduced as a ballroom
dance. The music is rag-time, and hard to
whistle. Jessie Lonnen does the Gaby glid n
in “The Sunshine Girl,” but isn’t the tune
just the ugliest ever?
Artist Hoyte, whose death was recorded
last week, had two daughters, one of whom
was the wife of the late Frank Myers, one of
the most brilliant of Australian journalists.
Dr. Taylor Young, an enthusiastic first-
nighter and a Macquarie-street physician, is
going off to England this month, taking Mrs.
Taylor Young along with him.

From Bath (England) comes an account
of the celebrations of our anniversary day,
when the Mayor and Corporation of that
ancient city added their tribute to the
memory of Admiral Phillip, the first Gov-
ernor of N.S.W., by placing on his mural
tablet a chaplet of wattle blossom. Governor
Phillip lived for the last years of his life in
the house at Bath, which is now distin-
guished by a bronze tablet. He is buried
in the picturesque little suburb of Bathamp-
ton, just outside the city. The chaplet of
wattle was tied with a blue ribbon showing
the Southern Cross (minus one star).
The Bath florist might easily be excused
for not knowing how many stars are in the
Southern Cross, or in the Commonwealth
flag, our national standard; but mistakes
about our own country are so exasperatmgly
common that it is a kind of relief to be
nasty about them when they come under our
ken. In a recent Lady’s Realm, which, sets
out to be a well-informed magazine publish-
ing stories by Wells and other big wigs—
there is a tale called “The He-Suffragette,”
the plot of which is laid in Sydney at the
time when womanhood suffr"- a was under
discussion. The hero, who lives in a house
overlooking “Lady MacQuarrie’s Chair and
the incomparable Bay,” is in favor of “Votes
for AVomen,” because of his best girl, wb«
lives in a house with a beautiful garden full
of “glorious geraniums,” situated in “Bays-
water.” But she refuses his hand and large
fortune because, although a “Suffragette”
herself, she despises Percy (the lover afore-
said) on account of his nickname, “the he-
suffragette,” given him by his companions of
the Sydney Rowing Club. In the liberal
daubing of local color which the writer ap-
plies, this paper receives a gratuitous ad.,
as the hero’s brother at a critical moment
was “reading The Sydney Bulletin,” but
even then, she (the writer is Mrs. G. C.
Miln), failed to get the name right. After
the sad parting of the lovers, the quiet hush
of night sinks down on “Sydney Bay.’ Mrs.
Miln doesn’t seem to remember much about
Sydney.

“Coon Can” is the latest card game It-
bound to reach us very shortly if
already a craze by the time this meets von.
attentive eye. Much more lively than eithe
whist or bridge, Coon Can has caught on

A big budget of music just sent bv the
publishers includes “The Australian Cadets’’
a song and march in honor of the boys in
brown. It is nicely rhythmical, and the
sentiment is full of patriotism and “duty’s
high command” and “fight to keep Australia
white,” which is all right. The composer i s
Robt. P. Thomson. From the same firm
(Paling’s) come several songs all of the
requisite shortness. The fashionable draw-
ing-room ballad must not be long. It need
not be coherent or melodious, but it must be
quick, the drawing-room victim now prefer-
ring sudden death to lingering agony,
Song of Triumph,” by F. Anderson", and
“Uncertainty,” and “Look to the Rose,” by
Kathleen Boydell, may be recommended as
smart, effective ballads for present day use.
De Beaupuis has used his editorial blue
pencil on a waltz by Emil Liebling. “Flor-
ence,” a “grande valse brillante de concert,”
does not discredit its parentage. It looks
rather difficult. I should prefer, myself, to
play it from a music roll, with my feet.
“Norwegian Reverie.” bv Marcelle Christian,
and Part I. of Karl Jensen’s violin system,
which seeks to lessen the worries of the
beginner at the violin, are also to hand.
Chappell and Co., Ltd., send two song
albums, vol. 11. of both Guy d’Hardelot’s and
Herman Lohr’s. They may be of great use
to the soprano or baritone seeking titbits for
encores. “In 1822” is a fetching effort in
the former collection, while “The Little Gal-
way Cloak” and “A Song of Surrey” are two
fine man’s songs by Lohr. “I Love the
Moon,” by Paid Rubens; “White Laures-
tine,” by Emmeline Brook; “God Make thee
Mine,” by Faydn Wood ; “Morning,” by Liza
Lehmann: “Love’s Words.” by d’Hardelot;
and “Hwang-Ho,” one of Margaret Cooper’s
songs, by Phillip Braham. also in a slow time
valse; “L’Amour Defender,” by H. J. Van
Prang, make up a fascinating bundle of
melodiousness.
A very jolly juvenile party would have
been wrecked by the gas strike on Saturday,
only for the kindness of the next-door lady,
who cave the use of her kitchen stove to
boil the kettle and any other things required
for the children’s feast. The guests played
about on the lawn till it grew too dark to see
each other’s sash bows, and then they all
went home to candles and early bed. Church
bells rang in the late afternoon, and congre-
gations filed in, two hours in advance of
usual time. At night black darkness settled
down amid a stillness that was quite weird.
This, of course, was in the outside suburbs
that are lit by gas. The stars were grate-
fully observed by hundreds of folk who never
see them at ordinary times. Stargazing as a
pastime was about the most popular pr-
of whiling away the evening. Men were so
hard put to it, that they took tramjourneys
in order to read, and, as only the eagie-
eved can see by tram-light, you mayJ#
of the desperate plight to which the Ma
State metropolis was reduced.
This letter is going to press in the thick
of industrial trouble. When the gas is flaring
merrily again (if ever it does), perhaps we
shall be able to find some fun in talking over
the makeshifts of “Starvation Sunday, but
it’s too near for us to see anything but
dreadful discomfort in it, at present. What
sort of a stove have you?” is absolutely m
burning question of the day, and if the st
does burn, then its possessor is looked up to
with respect by all the neighbors, home
butchers nobly came to the rescue by deli
ing the meat already cooked to those unior
tunate householders who only kee PjM
stove, but in many suburbs backyard cooKe
are improvised out of three bricks and .
pair of tongs. What rubs in the difficulty
the fact that this is summer, when firejjoa
is a negligible quantity in our homes.;
less folk are burning up their fa™*?®
desperation. Old chairs and P ac j n
dressing tables come in very ha ? d ?' ates
some houses, so great is the nead ’. m an d
are tearing down their back premises,
combusting them regardless of the p
present, and the future. A friend ofj^

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, March 3, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
All things come to her who waits, and even
summer gowns have had their chance at last.
It was hard that Newmarket Day should
really have been cool enough for autumn
raiment; nevertheless, the start of the V.R.C.
Autumn Meeting, on Saturday, brought out
some smart striped velvet gowns, a few ex-
cellent things in poplins, scores of triumphs
in combined velvet and satin in fuchsia
colors, and advanced models of the already
familiar Richelieu coats. The Fuller lady
sported a ninon gown in the shade of a blue
hydrangea, and a wide-brimmed hat of the
diaphanous order.
Federal Government House has acquired
two new lodgers in Mr. and Mrs. Arden
Franldyn, who are here on a six-weeks’ visit.
The lady, who possesses nice looks and smart
attire, is a sister of Secretary Wilberforce,
of the Fuller staff, and an old friend of the
Denman lady. The visiting pair will jour-
ney home to their native Hampshire via
China and Japan, arriving in England just
in time to follow next season’s fox. Hunting
that smellful little animal is the favorite
pastime.
Melbourne is positively seething with At
Homes and teas organised by the social
climbers, with the object of gathering funds
for the coming charity bazaars. Th
Mayoress’s stall is being built up by bridge
parties, linen teas and musical outbreaks,
where everybody is a little bit more im-
portant than the last duchess you ran into.
Mayoress Hennessy sits back, and occasion-
ally indulges in a weary presiding smirk; but
she seems to get little joy out of the scramble
of the almost-arrived dames for precedence
and other inedible substances. Anyhow, the
Homoeopathic and Women’s Hospitals will
reap the benefit of it all at Easter, and it
only means that a few more suburban peer-
esses will hate one another. Where so many
already hate, a few more don’t matter.
On Friday last, the Denman lady, attrac-
tively upholstered in blue, slid back the doors
of the new Lost Doggery. On the instant, a
large white stray, which had been carefully
secured beforehand, dragged a very small boy
over the threshold. The youth was to have
led the animal; but the four-legged pauper
was too eager to get in. The complimentary
presentation to her Ex. consisted of a silver
dog paperweight. Mrs. Chads, the original
mover in the scheme, was motored over from
Camberwell by order of the committee, and
occupied a seat of honor. There was an
immense attendant crowd of the Very Best
dogdovers. Some of them brought dogs and
children; others just brought dogs.
An afternoon-tea and bunning in sultry
weather mostly struggles along in a dull,
mechanical fashion; but the first social ven-
ture of Eunice Russell Chia was not that
sort of function at all. Of course, it had a
special social interest. The large reception-
room at Sandringham House, tricked out in
pink and blue, matched the blue-garbed hos-
tess nicely. She wore her “so delighted” ex-
pression full on, and was eagerly seconded
by mamma Mrs. Russell in the background.
The Warrawee Club, an institution which
is struggling bravely to fill the void left by
the defunct Swastika, threw open its doors
last week, and entertained little Lizzie New-
bold. A swarm of members listened to song
and story, whilst juggling with refreshments.
President Mrs. Staughton hurried down from
Gisborne, and shook an expensively plumed
head over the occasion, while Mrs. Secretary
Phillips stood by reverently. A solid splash
of blue indicated Mrs. Id. J. Rickerton,
while a granny bonnet located Mrs. Noall,
whose head covering made a startling con-
trast to the wide-spreading lids of the other
women. Girls abounded. This club bids for
young folk who yearn for culture, and sub-
scriptions are pouring in so fast that already
its eyes are fixed on more imposing Collins-
street premises.
Mrs. Robert Simson, who has just col-
lected a son and daughter from England,
where culture-polish is supplied by the gal-
lon, celebrated the event by “bringing them
out” at a dance in St. Kilda Town Hall,
which was expensively decorated with pink
and white roses. Despite sultry conditions,
all Toorak heroically motored along to the
revel. The hostess, a magnificent spectacle
in melon pink and gold, stood handy to
daughter Margot, who was coolly frocked in
white. The Federal Government House con-
tingent, shepherded by Chamberlain Nevill,
was made up of the Barttelot pair, Mrs.
Arden Franklyn, and Help Burnham. Secre-
tary Wilberforce represented the State
House. Norman Brookes and his wife (the
latter furnished in white) were right in the
thick of the two-stepping. Mrs. Norman
Armytage chaperoned daughter Edna and
Aileen Bell, who has quite recovered from
her appendicitis, and is booked back to the
Toowoomba station in May. Mrs. Nash
came down from Oranbourne with a pink
daughter, and the Werribee Chirnsides (like
the refreshments) helped to swell the crowd.
In the younger section, Greta Laidlaw, from
the Hamilton district, was easily first.
Mrs. Norman Brookes gave a farewell tea
to old-friend Jessie Landale Murray, who is
taking off husband Ronald and their small
son for a European wander. The Brookes
couple these days dwell at Waverdon, the
cosy South Yarra home of Charles Emmer-
ton. The place, which has been loaned
to them during uncle’s absence in
foreign parts, possesses a fine library
filled with classic tomes. This apart-
ment was used for the tea, and pink
bella-donna lilies mingled with the brown-
backed volumes for the frivolous occasion.
Mrs. Norman, in trails of black and white
chiffon, cleverly shaded with Brussels lace,
had little daughter Cynthia on view in her
best bib and tucker. Mamma Emmerton was
in close attendance. Alex. Landale’s widow
attended in black robes, with the respective
wives of sons Hunter and George. The Bart-
telot lady smiled upon the gay librarians,
likewise the Henry Wrixon dame, Mrs. Sam
Staughton, Mrs. Albert Miller, and the rest
of the local peerage.
Douglas Mawson, leader of the Australian
scientific expedition in Antarctica, is having
a long, cold courtship. The “wireless” which
told of the death of two members of his
party, but went into no details about it,
mentioned also that Mawson and perhaps
half a dozen others had been unable to take
the relief ship northwards, and would have
to spend another winter ir> the. ice. It must
have been cold news for the girl he left be-
hind him—one of the daughters of G. D.
Delprat, the man who engineered the iron
and steel business at Newcastle on behalf of
the big Broken Hill Co.
Lily Morris and Charles Lazarus recently
succeeded in getting themselves made one;
but the wedding suffered so many interrup-
tions that an elderly relative wept bitterly
over the feast as she counted the ill omens.
A puncture to the bride’s motor-car was
the first trouble. The second disaster was
caused through the waiting bridegroom
secreting the ring in some unlikely pocket,
and having to conduct a frenzied search
while the party looked on. After smashing
the required crockery, the newly-wedded
pair hurried away without signing the bond,
and the distracted rabbi had to send his
assistant after them. The ill-luck seemed to
peter out after that; but the elderly rela-
tive refuses to take a cheerful view of the
matter. Omens go for a lot in Jewish wed-
dings. . .
A March bride who is promised to a Col-
lins-street fangsmith will not go poorly
dressed to her new surroundings. Pa has
risen to the occasion like a well-inflated bal-
loon, and his girl is hard at work spending
a cheque for £BOO on trousseau chiffons, in
these times, when Fashion changes with the
rapidity of an electric sign, spending that
amount of money on clothes looks rather
like buying a vanload of bread for one per-
son.
Among this month’s marryings is that of
Dr. J. Ira Morris, of San Francisco, with
Stella, the plump daughter of “Joe” Perl,
of St. Kilda. The bridegroom is understood
to be considerably dollared, and papa Perl
is alleged to have enough of the needful to
bless the union with a ponderous leaf from
his cheque book. Anyhow, he has com-
mended a full ceremony at the Bourke-street
Synagogue, and has hired the Masonic Hall
for the subsequent feeding, with champagne
trimmings. Also, he is said to have shed so
much cash for the bridal finery that all
female Melbourne speaks of the bill with
reverence.
Professor Josephine Cilder, from Minne-
sota University, U.S.A., is busily grubbing
about this State after native vegetable mat-
ter. Josephine has mommer along to take
care of her, and is in such a feverish hurry
to get through with her quest that tea riots
have no attractions whatever. Still, under
her learned exterior, there lurks, the womanly
hankering for simple domestic pleasures.
The spirit of professional enthusiasm evi-
dently burns brightly in her spare frame.
A wise Mitchell baby recently arrived on
David Mitchell’s 75th birthday. It is
the child of Frank Mitchell, who became
enamored of father David’s trained nurse
and married her. The hardy old gentleman
mourned the loss of his favorite attendant,
but all has long been forgiven. The baby
has been christened David, and spends half
its waking time on his delighted grandad’s
knee. Aunt Melba sent little Dave a trous-
seau worthy of a prince, and the outlook
for the youngster is of a golden hue.
Betty Paterson, the youngest daughter of
artist Hugh Paterson, has won a singing
scholarship at the Marshall Hall Conserva-
torium. The young woman also plays the
violin with more than ordinary skill, but
her fine voice puts the second talent in the
shade.
Colonel Harold Grimwade trips to Eng-
land aboard the Otranto this month. Whilst
in the Cold Country he may go into camp
for a military course, being somewhat of a
glutton for soldiering and a fervent admirer
of the Commonwealth’s compulsory service
system.
The artistic world which counts Mr.
Laurie Abrahams, of South Yarfa, among
its best friends and brothers will be glad to
hear that he has just returned from his
health trip, looking ever so much the better
for it. Some months ago Hattenbach, the
’cellist, persuaded friend Abrahams to go to
Germany with him; and Hattenbach’s Teu-
tonic frivolity, the long voyage, and the Ger-
man Spas have done wonders for the
health-seeker.
When Oscar Asche stepped into the box
to give evidence in the Meyer-Dorothy Grim-
ston case he seemed to suffer from stage
fright or something equally uncanny. The
muttered remarks of the owner of the voice
that boomed through Cyprus were scarcely
a udible, and he had to be requested to “speak
up.”
Dr. Arthur- Brebner, who wrote “Patches
and Pomander” and is now inspecting Vic-
toria at close quarter, drops a line from
Ballarat to say that I did him too much
honor when the other week I put in for him
a claim to acquaintanceship with “numer-
ous Royal foreigners, including Kara-
georgevitch,” of Servia. “My knowledge
of Royalties,” he says nicely, “is
limited to seeing them on civic occasions
from the top of a ’bus ; and,” he adds nastily,
“my knowledge of Australians is, I am
happy to say, even less.”
Mrs. Fred Knight, daughter of the late
wealthy Riverina squatter Faulkner, is leav-
ing shortly to show her daughters how to
foreign-travel. The lady has been on the
sick list for years, but has managed to get
off it at last.
Curly-haired Margaret Lewis James, who
was the soloist of the Women’s Choir two
years ago, but left these shores on the advice
of Melba for further study, writes of foreign
successes. At time of reporting, the clever
little lady was singing leading parts in
opera in Venice. With a fine voice, the
command of several languages, and the work-
ing capacity of a colony of beavers, the Aus-
tralian girl (who, by the way, is half
French) really deserves her limelight. For
a time she was on the teaching staff of the
Presbyterian Ladies’ College, which was also
Melba’s old academy. Away from the foot-
lights, her name is Margharita Valdi.
Mrs. Love, news of whose death in London
was cabled last week, intended returning
with her two girls by the Osterley. The
family resides for the most part in Towns-
ville, where Love, sen., does good business
in shipping horses to India. Daughter Olive’s
wedding with squatter Robinson, of Banana-
land, had been fixed to take place in Syd-
ney immediately after the arrival of the
voyagers.
Annie Sadlier, after quietly dawdling
through parts of the Commonwealth, lias
crossed to Maoriland prior to returning to
her Tipperary home via Canada. A titled
gent who has a weakness for assisting de-
crepit religious institutions in Ireland, is a
near relative, as is also our imported naval
instructor Ryan, husband of the lady who is
understood to be dimly related to the Den-
man family.
From “Rosna,” in London, where the fogs
are:—
So many Australians flew to their country’s birth-
day party on January 27 that the Colonial Institute
was crowded out of house and home. They filled
rooms, corridors, stairs and lower hall, and won-
dered what was happening aloft. Everybody wore a
feathery spray of wattle, or, as the Briton persists
in calling it, mimosa. The rooms and tea tables were
also wattle-decked, and High Commissioner Reid,
who made the one speech of the revel, was framed
in tall jars of it. The speech was brief but full of
thrilling pieces of information about Australia Felix.
Outside, too, wattle was on that day the city’s most
conspicuous vegetable. The Australian offices and
banks were decked with it; “Australia’s Message”
hoarding in the Strand was wreathed with it; and
one big universal provider (Selfridge’s), running a
wattle day on its own account, swathed its florist
department in the Australian blossoms, and showed
Australian products in its windows. As most of the
dailies devoted at least a column and big head-lines
to the occasion, the Commonwealth scored an able-
bodied advertisement out of the anniversary revel.
The Society of Shakspeare holds its first
meeting of the year at the Austral Salon on
Friday. The faithful are exhorted to attend.
Office-bearers for 1913 will be elected, the
annual report and balance-sheet will be re-
cited in blank verse, and brothers Edward
Stevens and M. M. Phillips. M.A., are to
read papers on the “trend of Shakspearian
criticism” in two different centuries. A
violently exciting subject, to be sure.
Bernard Hall’s display at the Athenaeum
Hall will contain a lot of new work as well
as some of the pictures that figured in his
ill-timed show a vear or two back. The
private view is to-day (Thursday), and the
season will last until Wednesday, the 19th.
The committee of the Misses Hyams’ hops
have already arranged a new series of Cin-
derellas for the coming season. The kick-
off is to happen on May 8 at All Saints’
Hall, Windsor, where the ball was kept
rolling last winter.
An old doctor of bats, racquets and clubs
was caught in a confidential mood the other
day. This is the essence of his wisdom,
poured out while he attended to a bad frac-
ture in a golf stick : “The worst payers of all
are the golfers. I don’t know why it should
be so. Perhaps it is because golfers are
gentlemen—that is to say, men with nothing
to do. When a man idles most of his time,
there’s nothing much else you can call him
without giving offence. No. give me the lad
who plays cricket or tennis, and pays his
few shillings or pence directly you give him
back his bat or racquet.”
My Adelaide correspondent writes: —
Mrs. Dudley Hayward, who left for England by
the Marrhora, was farewelled for days prior to her
departure. She was lunched and tea-ed at the Queen
Adelaide Club by Miss Lea and Mrs. C. Hayward,
and spent a strenuous day saying farewells and
thank-yous for good wishes. Mrs. Dutton Green
sailed by the same boat, likewise Mrs. George
Brookman and her daughter, Mrs. Wilson, and Mr.
Gordon Ayers. The Sydney B. R. Wises braved a
day of vile heat in Adelaide, what time the Mar-
mora engulfed new passengers and luggage and mails.
Miss Grant Bruce, who goes from Melbourne to
London with a new book amongst her luggage,
passed through in the Beltana at the beginning of
the week. She bears a sheaf of letters to literary
notables, wherefore she should see much of the Inky
Way on the other side.

Mr. Frank Toms, who died at Kapunda on Sunday
was a son of the late S. Toms, who made money bv
softgoods. All was sport that came in his way till
1907, when he settled down on the Koonunga estate
and became a keen experimental farmer. His widow
is a daughter of the late Police-Commissioner Peters-
wald.
Died on the same day, at Aldgate, F. Caley Smith a
floriculturist of note, and one of the popular Yalumba
clan. He was aged 48, and had been married for
only a couple of years.
