POLITICAL
POINTS
A EAR-REACHING BLIGHT.
The British Board of Trade reports that the food-
purchasing power of the sovereign is now only equal
to 16s. 3d., as compared with 20s. in 1895. —London
news item.
ABSENTEE- AUSTRALIAN (anti-Sosh of course)
“That Protection again!”
“0.K.” writes: —
The Commonweath’s infant miitary system has
already served to point a moral for the opponents of
compulsory service in Britain. Touching the sug-
gestion that a compulsory force would be employed
to repress labor strikes the veteran “Bobs” remarks
that “if a Labor Government in Australia did not
fear this the wage-earners of Britain need not be
alarmed.” He might have added the argument that
whereas a voluntary army is something of a weapon
in the hands of Toryism, compulsory service would
make it more of an instrument of defence for. the
workers against tyrannical repression of labor risings.
Why, it was even thought advisable to disarm and
disband one or two companies of voluntary troops
at the time of the big strike a year ago. They were
suspected of an earnest desire to join the strikers.
“Hee Kee”; According to the statement
made by Johnson, W.A.’s Minister for
Works (B. 23/l/’l3), the purchase of
steamers by his Government in connection
with the Nor’-West cattle trade gave the
squattocracy such a severe short-arm jolt
that rigor mortis set in immediately. That
is what should have happened. What has
happened is different. About the first cargo
carried by the State was for Gooch, one of
our largest beef and mutton orchardists;
and, later, the modest firm of Connor, Do-
herty and Durack was accommodated by
Bill Johnson’s Department. Then one of
the vessels which was bought to cheapen
W.A.’s meat supply was headed for Maori-
land with a cargo of jarrah for Millar’s
struggling Combine, and now the good ship
has arrived back from the Eruptive Isles
with coal for the anaemic firm of Mcllwraith,
McEacharn and Co. Still, though I didn’t
quite see how this was going to reduce the
price of steak, I took Johnson’s word for it
that the deceased bullock was being retailed
for 2d. per lb. cheaper than hitherto; where-
fore I hurried home and accused Mrs. Hee
Kee of falsifying the domestic accounts. Her
replv to the accusation was lost in a crash
of ironmongery. I haven’t the slightest
doubt, however, that the smothered reply
contained an emphatic denial of Bill’s asser-
tion.
Even the Cold Country is turning dog on
its old fetich of Party Government. A re-
view just to hand puts it this way:—
Tt [Party Government] keeps out of employment a
great mass of precious ability. It puts into place
not the fittest, but the most eligible from the party
point of view-—that is, very often, the worst. Effi-
ciency implies the rule of the fittest; party means the
rule of something else—not the unfittest, but of the
fe»y fit, the accidentally not unfit, and the glaringly
unfit.
The author of this treason, the right-think-
ing people who want to be so-English will be
horrified to learn, is none other than Rose-
bery, earl, ex-Prime Minister, and bluest of
blue Britons.
The time seems to be about ripe for sup-
plying a new definition to the nasty word
“scab.” In Labor circles a “scab” used to
be the man who worked or made a pretence
of working when the official ukase was to
down tools ; and the stigma was more effec-
tive, in most than the prospect of
seven years’ gaol. But those were the days
when Labor’s official weapon was the strike.
Nowadays Labor itself, through its own
Governments, has declared the strike illegal,
and has officially adopted a new method of
getting justice; yet when gas-makers and
others kick over the traces, and tell Labor
Governments and Trades Halls to go to the
devil, no official so far has told them that
they are the new kind of “scab.” It is
about time somebody did it—assuming that
Labor is in earnest in officially adore'-'
arbitration as its weapon. And if it isn’t
in earnest, it had better drop the pretence
overboard.
“Briner” sums up our naval baby:—
There are 3837 senior Australian naval cadets.
Our actual navy has 1616 men, with 216 boys getting
ready on the Tingira, and 28 middies ready for the
temporary Naval College at Geelong. There has been
£7BOO spent on this college—on its classrooms, bar-
racks, recreation grounds, gymnasium and offices,
jetty extension, swimming baths and boatsheds. The
expenditure includes £2300 by the Geelong Harbor
Trust; the nation planked down the other £ 5500.
Meanwhile, work at the permanent college at Jervis
Bay proceeds under much difficulty. Until lately, the
only communication was by steamer, which had to
await suitable wind and weather. Between 300 and
400 men are now at work, including road-makers.
The navy’s recruit resources are Geelong College for
officers, Tingira for seamen, Williamstown Naval
Depot (the old ironclad Cerberus) for stokers and
seamen, and the Encounter for sea-going training.
The officers and men lent by the British navy num-
ber 139 ; and another 120 officers and men, formerly
of the British navy, have joined ours. In addition
to all the numbers mentioned, 900 men are needed for
the Australia, Sydney, Melbourne and the submarines.
The British navy will lend us what we want till we
can grow our own.
The beauty and chivalry of Australia —
one of each —went down a few days ago to
Yassberra. the godless capital that was born
of lies and treachery and meanness and State
Frights, to see a bounding politician with a
large hat drive a spike into the great wilder-
ness. And the Sydney Telegraph man
spoke sadly of the scene that opened out be-
fore him:—
The place has been without rain for a long period.
The past week’s downpour, though fairly general,
did not embrace Yass, and the country all round is
bare and dry, badly needing water to again galvan-
ise it into life. Hot nights, too, have been frequent,
and the dreariness of the surrounding landscape is,
to a considerable extent, reflected in the listlessness
of the inhabitants.
The Telegraph has no luck with its favorite
capital. It was that paper’s fishing expert
who, in a moment of beastly candor, de-
scribed the wonderful Cotter River as a
mere series of water-holes —dabs of moisture
with Sahara in between, so to speak.

Purblind, narrow-minded folk, incapable of looking
into the future, and too small-souled to give hospitality
to a great idea. — Melbourne Argus on the anti-superflu-
ous State Governor’s movements.
Gibraltar, according to Governor Sir
Archibald Hunter, is a state of corruption
founded on a rock. His Ex. likens the town
to an Augean stable.
“R. 8.” writes: —
If Andrew Fisher desires to stay in office, he
should muzzle O’Malley or cashier him. The action
of Hughes in appointing the impossible Piddington to
the High Court was about to begin to be forgotten
when the roaring futility from Canada turned up in
Sydney and talked of “a great scream and a grave-
yard howl and a wail from every shroud and sepul-
chre” and the Fusionists’ false teeth and the other
stock-in-trade of this quack of the market square.
Surely Australia is about tired of this windy ex-
peddler. He makes our country ridiculous abroad,
and raises false hopes in the minds of other political
bunco steerers who have exhausted the crop of fools in
their own land.
MeGOWEN'S NIGFITMARE.
Government House lies heavy on his dreams.
Uncle Samuel, being a territorially remote
Uncle, looked on quietly from New York
while the spitfire Balkan States made the
great but jealous Powers of Europe look
small and foolish by disregarding their hints,
warnings and curses, and continuing to prod
the Unspeakable towards the sea. But
Uncle’s time was coming. Directly Stand-
ard Oil Madero fell off in Mexico, the cable
gravely notified the world that “the Secre-
tary for State has announced that the Gov-
ernment will interfere with a view to secur-
ing a fair trial for ex-President Madero.”
The tremendous statement had just about
gone the cable rounds when the ex-President
was shot dead by his “rescue” party. Seems
hardly worth while being the mouthpiece of
a Great Power these days. The small,
scuffling people who should listen respect-
fully just go on fighting, and pay about as
much attention to Great Powers as they do
to Great Snakes.
Argus remark on the Warrenheip by-
election (Vic.), one of the candidates for
which is Mr. Ben Clark: —
Among those who are displaying keen interest in
the contest is Mr. Clark’s father, an old resident of
the district, who, despite the fact that he is over
80 years of age, is taking quite an active part in
the campaign. Mr. Clark makes no claims to the
higher flights of oratory. He has not Mr. Brennan’s
ability as a platform speaker, but he took 3000 bags
of barley off his farm at Dunnstown within the last
few l weeks.
This opens up interesting possibilities. We
may now expect something like the follow-
ing :
Vote for Mr. Sourgrass. He took first prize for
fat bullocks at the last show.
Give your vote to Mr. Meadow-fat. He drew the
biggest cheque at the butter-factory last month.
Mr. Browns-River is the man for the district. His
spuds went eight tons to the acre, and there was no
blight in them.
Still, it seems unfair of the Argus to aim at
getting two Houses of Fat.
When George Reid, years ago, started out
on his Socialistic Tiger hunt, we were told
it was the most momentous hunt in the his-
tory’ of Australia, and that if Reid came
back without the tiger it was a long good
night to Australian freedom, prosperity,
marriage tie and the right to wallop one’s
own kids. The whole social fabric would go
down crash. Last week Joe Cook went forth
to the wilderness of Paddington (Sydney)
and announced the opening of the Liberal
campaign against the Referenda. He said it
was a campaign the like of which, he be-
lieved, had never begun before. Then he
went on to solemnly warn the people that if
it failed they would have Unification for a
certainty. And there he stopped. What par-
ticular horrors hang round the dread brows
of Unification lie did not explain; presum-
ably not having the imagination to invent
them on the spot, or the time to read them
up before the meeting. Reid made his tiger
yarn a complete novel with a somewhat
lurid finish. Cook’s fish-tale, or snake-yarn,
just breaks off and fizzles out when the fire-
works should be let off. The average man
who has studied the subject sees in Unifica-
tion and Reconstruction the making of a self-
reliant nation free of the tyranny of Trusts,
Combines and a lot of other evils that flour-
ish under the regime of State Frights.

Th red-ragger is a curious freak. He
howls at the Defence Act, and says the
Australian worker would be a fool to defend
a country which does not belong to him, and
alleges that a Japanese invasion wouldn’t
matter, as the invaders would be workers,
and all workers are brothers with one com-
mon enemy, the Capitalist. Yet, as soon as
a small invasion does happen—an invasion
by a few shiploads of immigrants in cheap
tweed caps—the same red-ragger howls at
the top of his voice to the Labor party to
Stop It in the interests of the Australian
workman and his wages.

SHADOW OF ASIA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The E. and A. mail steamer Eastern arrived on
Monday from Japan and Hongkong via ports, and
was quarantined owing to a ease of smallpox having
occurred on board during the early part of the
voyage. —News item.
Far out of the beaten track she lies
Where great cliffs climb to the cloud-flecked
skies;
The yellow flag at the masthead flies.
The clean wind whistles —the white gulls float
Over the anchorage—snug, remote.
(“There’s smallpox there on that Chinese boat!’’)
The blue sea cradles her day by day;
The far waves silently burst in spray;
She lies a thing from the world away.
Her twinkling ports in the night are gold,
When seas are black, and when winds are cold.
On joyous decks is her story told.
And those who were laughing cease and peer
Through the sea darkness that holds the Fear.
Shadow of Asia is here —is here !
N.s.w.
E. FRANCIS.

KALGOORLIE TO PORT AUGUSTA
RAILWAY.
65 feet Car Underframes.
TENDERS will be received until 11a.m. on
26th March, 1913, for the manufacture in
Australia, supply and delivery of sixteen (16)
sixty-five feet (65ft.) Car Underframes.
Forms of tender, specification, conditions of
contract and drawings can be seen after 3rd
March, 1913, at the offices of the Commonwealth
Railways, 84 William Street, Melbourne ; Works
Director, Custom House, Sydney; Common-
wealth Works Registrars, Brisbane, Adelaide,
Hobart; and the Commonwealth Public Service
Inspector, Perth.
The charge for each copy of Specification will
be £1 Is. and ss. a copy for each plan, which
must be paid before the documents can be handed
over.
Tenders, accompanied by the necessary deposit,
should be endorsed “Tender for 65ft. Car
Underframes for Kalgoorlie to Port Augusta
Railway,” and addressed to the Secretary, De-
partment of Home Affairs, Melbourne.
KING O’MALLEY,
Minister of State for Home Affairs.
KALGOORLIE TO PORT AUGUSTA
RAILWAY.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
From a mob of 35 orher stragglers The
Bulletin's tip, Relievo, won the Newmarket with a
couple of lengths to spare. Evidently a good game
colt is Relievo. He may not have run quite up to
his great trial, but under the whip he caught the
grey horse Nuwara Eliya more than a furlong from
home, and with the whip still urging him onward he
sceadily advanced. Though Jolly Beggar (25 to 1)
and the rank ousider Kirn came through the field in
pursuit, and passed the tiring grey, the leader never
looked like getting caught. Had Relievo failed at
the finish, it might have been fairly complained that
jockey Connors made his run too soon. The time
(lmin. 14fsec.) has been beaten in half a dozen pre-
vious Newmarkets, and equalled twice; but consid-
ering that there is bound to be a little blocking in
a field of 36, the time was good.
A few people outside of the stable circle had good
wins over Relievo. For a time the three-year-old was
quoted at 50 to 1; a week before the race he stood
at 14 to 1, and on the day the weight of Sydney
money brought him down from 10’s to 6’s, what time
the local punters in general were doing their dash
on Mala, Volsloane, “You’re a Liar,” Habel, Sweet
Malt and a dozen others. To the writer it seemed
that the grey “You’re a Liar” ran like an over-
trained horse. He got away in front and stayed
there for more than half a mile, but probably he had
left some of his stamina on the track. He stopped
too rapidly.
Notwithstanding his victory in the rich A.J.C.
Derby, Cider has been an unlucky colt in the classic
races thusfar. Undoubtedly he would have won the
V.R.C. Derby for Agar Wynne if the pace had been
better, and Wolawa had not interfered with him.
Again he was unlucky to be seized with pains in the
tummy a few days before his V.R.C. Leger engage-
ment came due. The attack of colic put him out of
condition for a long journey. Cider led the’field for
12 furlongs, and was then completely pumped out,
finishing last of a field of four. Wolawa won in
3min. or 6sec. slower than the record for the
race, and 3sec. slower than Cadonia’s time two years
ago. The colic which practically robbed Cider of the
Leger had caused him to be scratched for the Aus-
tralian Cup.
Piastre’s victory for the Essendon Stakes was en-
sured by a friendly pacemaker, in the old-fashioned
way that owners of late years have mostly departed
from. Stable companion Offa was sent to “bustle the
field along, with the result that the 12 furlongs were
covered in 2min. 36Jsec., and Piastre had no diffi-
culty in beating Eudorus and Almissa at the finish.
If the Melbourne Cup winner had been troubled by
soreness on the track, he kept it carefully concealed
from observation in this go with the colors up. His
performance was brilliant. After the race punters
wondered what sort of a champion mile-and-a-halfer
must Duke Foote have been to be able to smother
Piastre in the A.J.C. Metropolitan last October.
* * * *
A little crowd of wildly illogical book-
makers waited on the Victorian Chief Secretary last
week with an amazing grievance. The V.A.T.C. pro-
poses to raise registration fees by 50 per cent., and
these suffering mortals seemed to think that the Gov-
ernment would veto the club’s resolution if some of
them produced surface evidence of their having lost
money in a year’s bookmaking at Caulfield. Too
many h»«;men are licensed, and too few skinners
come romping home on that particular punting
ground, the deputation moaned. It naturally oc-
curred to even Chief Sec. Weary (who is sometimes
too tired for mental calculation) to suggest that
these “representative’’ bookmakers could rid them-
selves of their grievance by stopping away from
Caulfield. They could go fishing on Caulfield Satur-
days, and thus save their money. It is not as
though they are under any compulsion to seek sorrow
by laying the odds anywhere. They had referred
tearfully to the fact that more books are licensed at
Caulfield than at Flemington. A lot of tag-rag Ikeys
come along and help to bear the burden of loss that
is annually imposed upon the ring by tyrant punters.
Why don’t they leave the tag-rags to do all the bet-
ting, and lose all the money, and thereby dry-up the
uncalled-for competition at its source?
The inflation of the Australian Jockey
Club the last few years has been phenomenal. In
1903-4 the club gave away £27,125 in added money,
last year its little fob disgorged £62,750; this year
—but consider the figures how they grow:—

* * * *
“Snaffle” : For a long while I have been
warning The Bulletin’s public about the determina-
tion of the Victoria Racing Club to charge for admis-
sion to the Flemington Flat. Sports have said to
me: “Oh, dear no! Nothing of the kind!” Now the
octopus is out of the bag. It is a simple matter of
fact that the V.R.C. has anplied to the Government
for leave to charge a shilling to the flat. This, re-
member, is Government land. The V.R.C. cunningly
worked round by fencing in the whole course, and try-
ing to exclude the public absolutely on non-racing
days, and so set up a right of possession. This
embargo was, however, broken down by the Fleming-
ton and Essendon municipal authorities, who estab-
lished the public’s permanent right to cross the
course. Little gates are consequently left ajar. Now
the octopus pushes out a tentacle a yard or two fur-
ther with a plain request to be allowed to make the
public part up. Foolish club! The game is not
worth the candle.
That grim comedy known as “The March
of the Athletes,” which took place on Saturday
afternoon at Sydney, presumably ends the dance of
the advertising Dervishes round the bones of Scott
and those who perished with him down South in the
snow. It was an unlovely exhibition—the blaring
band emphasising the heart throb of the Dervishes
with every drum crash, the surfers in their meat-
exposing costumes, the hoofballers in the colors
that have raised “the push” to stoush and glory, the
wearying Maorilanders with hen feathers in their
ears howling dismally and stamping the dust till it
rose and filled the spectator’s nose with germs and
chaff that had been eaten, fancy-dressed cyclists,
oarsmen with their leg-fur fluttering in the breeze,
and such like. The redeeming feature was the ab-
sence of elderly actresses with the collection plate
and the gallery of raw teeth. And when that is all
a show has to recommend it it’s a darned poor show.
* * * *
J. H. Sinclair, the long S’African cricketer
who was in Australia in 1910-11, is dead, aged 36.
On S’African wickets he scored centuries in Test
matches against Australia (2) and England (1). He
had the reputation of being a smiter, and was ex-
pected to be one of the draws of the S’African tour,
but, unfortunately, like others of the team, the giant
was quite unable to gauge the pace of the Australian
billiard table wickets, and he was one of the disap-
pointments that make life a sorrow and a dead-end.
J. N. Crawford, now of Adelaide, bats
and bowls in spectacles, and R. A. Young, of the
same place, keeps wicket in them ; but more aston-
ishing still is this from English papers (13/1/’13):
“Southampton made a last-minute change, Kimpton
going centre-forward for Dawe, who stood down at his
own request, owing to the rain getting on his
glasses and affecting his sight of the ball.” It was
Soccer football, of course; Rugby would seem im-
possible to the spectacled. Is there any record of
anyone having tackled the Australian code thus
handicapped ?
* * * *
Lacrosse is threatening to burst its swad-
dling clothes in Sydney next season. All the old
clubs are booming, or threatening to do so, and fresh
ones are being formed to seek admittance to the
competition. No doubt the fathers of the game
would be delighted to have them all, but the trouble
is to find grounds for them to play on. The foot-
ballers have spied out the land, and snavelled the
spaces thereof, so that no one small, unoccupied strip
is left. An inter-State visit will be made to Brisbane,
and one will be received from Tasmania. All of
which will serve to keep the bubbling enthusiasm at
boiling point.
Another Gas(tronomic) Strike. Frank Picato
(U.S.A.) gives Alf Morey (W.A.) the
tap after a tough struggle.
Yachtsmen went out on Saturday after-
noon prepared to meet the southerly buster that had
been signalled from Jervis Bay, but found it not,
the buster having apparently fallen overboard on the
road, and thereby made room for a mild east-sou’-
easter, with hardly enough strength in it to blow
the yachts along. The result was slow racing. The
event was the Rawhiti Cup, and nine went out after
it. The breeze was too light for the smaller craft
and it quickly developed into a struggle between
the skyscrapers, and the last two miles was a stub-
born duel between Culwulla 111. and Sayonara. Cul-
wulla won, with Sayonara second; Rawhiti, after
which the utensil in abeyance was named, came in a
medium third.
* * * *
An episode on Thursday morning threw a
weird light over the final proceedings at the Mel-
bourne Six Days Bike Race. It seems there was an
“honorable understanding” among the riders that no
one was to attempt to gain a lap in the cold, raw
hours before the dawn, when they were mostly creep-
ing round at about seven miles per hour; but one
rider ignored this agreement, and had lapped the
others before the shameful villainy had dawned on
them. The field was much annoyed at this, and re-
solved to make an example of the rash youth, who
was speedily lapped and lapped again. After that he
retired. In the light of this it is not surprising
there was no more lapping. At 9.45 on Saturday
the 10 riders on the track were told there were 35
more laps to go—seven miles. They crawled round
gradually for 33 laps, the crowd tossing them borak
and insults in bulk, and then the condensed essence
of the 141 hours’ ride—a two-lap sprint—resulted
in R. Spears beating McNamara by a wheel. The
crowd didn’t go mad with excitement. Most of it
had noted the significant fact that Ikey Noses was
laying three to one on Spears, five to one against
McNamara, eight to one Brown and Clifford, and 10
to one others, and drew its own conclusions. Dis-
tance covered, 2028 1-5 miles. As a race it was a
lamentable exhibition of wasted energy; but the
weather was fine all through, the track was in good
order and the lighting was excellent.
At 9 o’clock the last evening transpired the final of
the once-.mighty Austral, worth on this occasion £55
to the winner, and progressively smaller sums to others
down to a fiver for the 6th man. Eleven almost un-
known men started, nicely spread round the track,
from W. Teale on 40 yards to C. Hunter on 200.
Noses was offering “evens Henry, 3 to 1 Southern,
5 to 1 Atkins and any price others,” and naturally
Henry won, by a length or so. Time 4min., 16 4-5
secs. The Austral has now become a mere detail of
the Six Days’ Race, and if there isn’t any week-
long crawl next year it is more than likely there
won’t be any Austral. And, judging by the cock-
sureness of Noseband the firmness with which many
of him declined $ bet against Henry and Spears at
any price, the chances are there won’t be any more
such events for a while.
* * * *
Sydney is sharpening the carv-
ing knife and strengthening the corkscrew, for Vic.,
S. Aus., and Bananaland will all swoop down on the
City of Darkness at Eastertide. In addition an
army of country trundlers will be in the capital at
the same time, raging between the greens and the
show ground. Fortunately, the Sydney bowlers are
past-masters at the art of entertaining.
it- * * *
James Paddon, the sculling meteor who
soared to the Australian championship recently, had
hardly got there when the voice of Arnst announced
that Richard was coming up to pull the other down.
Paddon passed the matter on to his punters, and they
have decided that Richard’s money shall be collected,
and that Richmond River, North Coast, shall be the
place of the collecting. They say sculling draws
good gates on the Richmond, and they want a good
gate. The decision will probably provoke a howl
from Sydney.
* # * *
A double defeat by Ted Wickham, at the
Vic. Championship Carnival last week, is the first
downfall of burly William Longworth in a scratch
event since Cecil Healy outed him in the 100 yards
N.S.W. Championship away back in March, 1911.
Longworth’s monopoly of State and Continental
championships has been a thing to marvel at. Ap-
parently as he grows more set he is losing that mar-
vellous sprinting ability. Sic transit, etc.
The gas strike was not the sole cause of
gloom at North Sydney on Saturday night. That
afflicted suburb wrapped itself in sackcloth while it
sat in darkness, or cursed by the light of a Bur-
mese candle. For the district XI had failed miser-
ably against Clebe, and been dismissed for a paltry
141, while Glebe had responded with 98 for two
wickets. In spite of the darkness North Sydney can
see the shadow of defeat looming. Aided thereto
vastly by Pite’s 119, Waverley smacked 270 against
Paddington. This was the oniy century to grace the
afternoon. Redfern promises to make a holy show of
Central Cumberland, having dismissed the men from
Parramatta for 162, and then smacked up 196 for
four wickets. Middle Harbor journeyed to Balmain,
and got 278 knocked off its bowling by the time the
sun set. Nevertheless a trundler named Tierney dis-
tinguished himself by taking five Balmain wickets
for 52. Middle Harbor apparently did not discover
Tierney in time. Up at Chatswood Gordon fell on
the invading University team and smashed it up for
92, and then, hurrying to the sticks, made 158. Bur-
wood and Petersham made a rushing sort of a match,
getting through two innings and part of a third on
one afternoon. Burwood vanished for 116, and got
Petersham out for 103; then, rushing back to the
wickets, it smacked up 11 without losing a wicket.
It was a hustling performance.
* * * *
The penultimate round of Melbourne pen-
nant cricket started on Saturday. The V.C.A. has
this season reverted to its former custom of letting
the four leading teams battle out the semi-finals.
Of these four ’Roy is certain to be one, but the
other three places are still open to ’Varsity, ’Wood,
South and P’ran; consequently Saturday’s collision
of ’Varsity and ’Wood was of moment. The latter
won the toss, but made poor use of the batsmen’s
wicket, slowly and laboriously totting up 214 for
six wickets. Some 1500 sarcastic barrackers viewed
the episode, gratis. South, on the other hand, made
the most of its chances, and gave an exhibition of
’Cote by rushing together 335 runs for two wickets.
P’ran similarly accumulated 313 for two wickets;
and if it can get the Saints out for less next Satur-
day it may vet arrive. Carlton raked in 227 against
North, and Richmond declared with six wickets down
for 295 off Essendon’s bowlers; but the encounters
of these clubs no longer matter in the scheme of
things. ’Roy had a practice game with Melbourne
on the Metrop. C. G., the occurrence being of note
only because W. Webb, of ’Roy, emulated George
Bonnor’s feat of long ago by lifting a ball from T.
Armstrong on to the members’ pavilion. He was
endeavoring to better the feat when Armstrong
bowled him. East had the bye, which was just as
well, considering that it is already far down the list
and that five of its players are in the inter-State
team at Adelaide, and had Seitz been able to get
away he would have been there too. It seems pecu-
liar that a team boasting six inter-State players
should be no higher than seventh on the list, while
the “placed” teams —’Varsity, ’Wood and South-
are “allowed” to keep their men at home. But
many peculiar things happen in cricket.
* * * *
Now that the Vie. ’oofball clubs are pub-
lishing their balance-sheets for last season, the pub-
lic learns that the ’bailer actually gathers in from
two-thirds to five-sixths of each club’s revenue; also
it understands why secretaries, treasurers and com-
mitteemen wear a permanent worried look. Whether
they will need to do so in future will rest with
themselves, the League having last week passed a
resolution that there shall be no appeal to itself by
a player to whom his club has refused a transfer.
As this body has an agreement in good working
order with the Association that no player shall
transfer himself without the permission of both
parties, that disaster to the game, the migratory
player, is liable to find his future path somewhat
rocky. If his club won’t play him itself, and won’t
allow him to transfer to another, he just won’t
play at all—-and that ends it. Also, a committee
can pay its ’bailers just enough for dry bread, and
if they don’t like to play on those terms they can
stand idle —and that’s all. Under these circum-
stances next year’s balance-sheets should show a more
healthy complexion, but—there is a large possibility
of injustice to boggers-in. If, say, a committee, or
even a bare majority of one, should get a down on
a player, it might stand him aside and keep him that
way permanently. And that might easily create
arguments, strikes and other unseemly happenings.
Now, a strict scheme of district hoofbail—say with a
two or three years’ residential qualification, would
keep an equally effective restraint on the migratory
player, while withholding from committees the auto-
cratic power the new rule gives them.
* * * *
At Lavender Bay (Sydney) on Saturday
there was wild excitement among the water-nymphs
when Miss Wylie and Miss Durack contested the 100
yards championship of N.S.W. (Ladies’ Section, of
course). At 50 yards Miss Durack was about 3ft. in
front; then Miss Wylie, coming with a splendid
rush, ranged alongside, and for the next 25 yards
they put forth their utmost. It was the famous
crawl stroke that proved too much for Miss Wylie’s
double over-arm, and landed Miss Durack a winner
by approximately 3vds. Miss Kerr was third, about
7yds. behind Miss Wylie.
* * * «
Malcolm E. Champion, of Auckland, is a
Triton among the jelly-fish in Maoriland swimming
haunts. At the recent championship meeting he
romped home first in the 100, 220, 440, 880 yards
and one mile events. He is quite accustomed to
doing that sort of thing, having played the same
trick in 1901, 1908 and 1912. Altogether he has
garnered 32 Maoriland championships, and keeps
them in the locker in which he stowed the 1911 half-
mile and long-distance championships of England.
Australian professional champion swim-
mer Oscar Dickman is off to conquer new worlds in
Germany, France, England and the U.S.A. There is
no great money to be made swimming in Australia,
but on the other side of the world there are lots of
people who will pay good prices to see a professional
twirl his legs through the water. Also, there is no
other professional swimmer in the Commonwealth
like Dickman, so he finds himself lonely. The trip
will either cause his pocket to swell, or take the con-
ceit right out of him. Let us hope it may be the
former.

Those whom the gods would destroy they first
make mad has a special application to the Continen-
tal Tyre Co. This fool-concern has been advising its
clients to carefully wash their tyres after a long
and dirty run, whether the dirt is wet or dry. When
it is a case of dust, the tyres should be wiped over
with a damp cloth or sponge. When they are en-
crusted with mud they should be well hosed till th
last particle of mud is removed, and then dried
fully with a soft cloth. The Continental Tyre Co**
business is to sell tyres, but this advice, if careful!*
followed, will increase the life of the average tvr
by 150 per cent., and the fool Continental Tyre On
advertises the fact. On second thoughts the co i
not the fool it seems. The average motorist will
read, mark, learn and inwardly digest the advice re
mark, “Ry-buck!” and go to bed leaving his t’vrai
exactly as they finished the run; and when the dis
appointed motorist comes along with complaints
the co. can honestly say, “Well, we told you!” S ’
That sterling citizen and genuine fighter
Johnny Summers, is back in Sydney, and was form’
ally welcomed at a small dinner given by Mr. Baker
last Monday night. Summers was always a game
fighter, but some of the wise ones who saw him
stripped for action say he is vastly improved. Speak-
ing on Monday night, he reminded the company that
he really graduated in Sydney, as his first fight was
fought in the Ma capital, and it is his earnest wish
that it should also be the scene of his last.
Alf. Morey, lately a bright and shinim*
amateur boxer in Westralia, entered the professional
ring at the Sydney Stadium against the seasoned but
debonair fighter, Frank Picato, on Saturday night
No one expected him to win—Picato’s fame rested on
too solid a foundation to be overturned by a stripling
fresh from the amateurs. Somehow, the amateur
never seems to acquire ringcraft; that, apparently
comes only with the pursuit of the winner’s end of
the “gate.” With the golden lure for ever gleaming
in his eyes the bruiser declines to know when he is
licked. The spirit of the game seemed to descend
with considerable alacrity upon Morey, for he came
back again and again in a manner that worried the
dark-eved, sad-faced Americo-Italian youth consider-
ably. The Westralian fought with both hands, and
never was so formidable as after taking nine seconds
on the floor. Picato’s personality was overwhelmed
by the sudden ferocity of the attack of the fallen
man, and when he strove to stem the blizzard of
blows by leading on his own account, his attacks and
returns were cleverly blocked. There was only one
thing Morey lacked, and that was stamina. Either
he didn’t possess it or he was out of condition. But
he put up a magnificent fight. The superior ring-craft
of Picato was, however, invincible. On points Morey
was winning. But no one who watched the fight dreamed
for a moment he could go the full 20 rounds. Picato
was bound to win, sooner or later, on a knock-out, for
Morey’s strength was ebbing like the sunlight on a
midwinter afternoon. The 12th saw Morey a very
exhausted bruiser, whose hitherto effective smother
was beginning to wilt and fade very sadly. Picato,
on the other hand, seemed to grow stronger every
round, and, contrary to all rules, he swung an
obscene right instead of a divine left, which
flustered Morey badly, and sent Morey to the floor
for nine seconds. When ly rose, he was very groggy
indeed, but he put up such a magnificent bluff that
even the sturdy Picato backed away from him.
Terrific as it was, Picato knew it was a mere flash in
the pan, and so did Morey’s seconds, who, deeming
he had done quite enough in a hopeless battle, threw
in the towel. The towel caught on the ropes and
waved disconsolately, like a forlorn lover’s signal,
till someone drew referee Scott’s attention to it, and
he promptly declared Picato the winner. Which was
absolutely correct. Mr. Baker, however, was so
pleased with the showing of the defeated man that
he promised him a golden medal to commemorate his
first Sydney showing. It was a fine fight, clean
and vimful from start to finish.
* * * •
John Roberts, the daddy of billiard cham-
pions, has journeyed to Australia, this time for his
health’s sake only. The fine, upstanding professor is
suffering from a serious affection of the heart, and,
seeing that it must have been a mighty sound organ
in the first instance to have kept him going in
public for 45 years, its recent breakdown invites
medical discussion. Is the periodical strain of
match-playing a bad thing for a robust Roberts; or
does the bending over a billiard table for hours every
day in every year for half a century eventually find
him out; or does he help to weaken his heart by
smoking? Roberts belongs, or did belong until
recently, to the smoking section of billiard pros.
There were no teetotal and non-smoking professors of
any game of skill in his young days. Champions
played the game all round, and if their physical
constitution was not exceptionally good they dropped
out.
THE EAR BITERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Two men —a jockey and a horse-trainer—were re-
cently committed for trial in Brisbane for biting a
man’s ear. —News item.
Myself in callow days that some called Youth
Did eagerly frequent the racecourse booth,
And listened to the tale some whisp’rer told
And when the prad lost merely said “Gorstruth!”
A patriot he who now has sought the rod
To scourge frequenters of the paths I trod.
Ten Black Marias scarce would hold the crowd
Were those that bit my ear now borne to quod!
VICTORIA.
D. M. K.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“The Paymaster”: Chloride vf tin, used to impart
“rustle” to silk (B. 6/2/’l3), seems to be having it
all its own way in the trade. In an early issue of
The Lone Hand a simple test was given for discov-
ering impurities in silk fabrics. I have tried it many
times since; and, apart from the silk in a few
good sunshades, “shantung” is the only silk that
hasn't left a metallic framework behind it after the
material has been burnt. And shantung is practically
unprocurable, although when it was “fashionable it
sold at a lower price than silk taffeta, a loaded
article. It is alleged that the shops don’t sell the
real thing because it lasts too long a plausible
enough explanation of a fashion-phenomenon. Loaded
silks, however expensive, and most silk sunshades
crack after a few months, whether in use or not. The
draper guarantees his goods pure silk, and when,
later on, you tax him with his crime, he says: Oh,
yairs—moths!” #
“Lantana”: It . is quite odd to read that the parcel
post has only been established in the United States
this year, having come into practice on January 1,
1913. The system is in existence in 43 other coun-
tries, but it was only after endless discussion and
argument that it lagged into use in the States. It
has been known in Englarld for the past 30 years, and
it is said that a woman was- the cause of it. one
took a pair of trousers to the post office, wanting to
post them at the lower rate allowed for books and
other printed matter. The officials laughed at her,
but she insisted that the regulations stipulated that
articles open at both ends could go by the lower
rates, and that nothing was more open at both ends
than a pair of trousers. The matter was brought be-
fore the Postmaster-General, who decided that she
was right. That, according to the story, or lie, or
whatever it may be, was the beginning of the parcel
post in England. After all, the woman was only
partly right. The most open-at-both-ends article
isn’t a pair of trousers; it is a hoop. #
The B.M.A. practitioner isn’t the only person
barred by “professional etiquette” from advertising
like any other tradesmen. The calender of the
Royal Institute of British Architects (London) inti-
mates that “it is undesirable for architects to ex-
hibit their names on boards or hoardings m front of
buildings in course of construction. Some members
have been disregarding this hint, so the shocked in-
stitute lately notified its allied societies that mem-
bers really must be more dignified in future, it is
a beautiful tail, no doubt, but what a silly °H cat
it is that wears it! Why shouldn’t an architect put
his name to a house, if he wants to, .lust as Land-
seer, or Corot, or Norman Lindsay or any other
artist does to his picture? #
That rare bird, a lady chemist, has been before
en Adelaide Court. She objected to the Board of
Health’s inspector going over her premises, P r ' v 'ate
rooms and all. They sent a lady inspector, but she
still objected. In court the Board offered to with-
draw, without costs, if she would climb down but
6he preferred to “know just what the laws of the
country are.” The law proved to be that the Board
had the right to go over “such portions of premises
as are deemed necessary.” So the lady was fined
and ordered to pay costs into the bargain.

“Brumby”: Re fruit-vendors’ tricks (B. 13/2/’l3).
The dizziest limit is reached by the granite-faced
toughs who slouch round Sydney streets, with a hand-
cart bearing a huge cardboard sign announcing
cheap fruit for sale. As the unwary boob ventures
a purchase, one of the ruffians turns away to execute
his order. Simultaneously his mate' bawls Change f
with such a menacing aspect as to rudely arrest the
buyer’s attention from his confederate. By the time
the disconcerted citizen has paid his money, and
partlv recovered his composure, he finds himself
grasping a bag of inferior and undersized cooking
pears worth 4d. a dozen, topped perhaps with a
couple of the alluring specimens exposed as bait at
16 for Is. It’s useless for the victim to start
arguing the point then, unless he can use his
“dooks.” However, these are but the pettifoggers
of the profession. In spite of Tomatrius Peanutti s
plaintive look whenever the existence of a Sydney
fruit ring is mentioned, I know that the trifling
bonus of £lOOO was paid a short time ago by
one of him, for the privilege of being allowed to
pay the exorbitant rent of very ordinary shop
premises not far from Circular Quay. Both he and
others of his friends, Romans and countrymen in the
trade possess very good grips of Australia in the
shape of terrace houses, etc., on both sides of th
harbor. Yet they’ll tell you that ze pizness not so
ver’ good. Froot ver’ scarce. Damon ver scarce.

“F H.”: Tomatoes, according to the papers, have
had a terrible slump in Sydney. The reports allege
that, by the time the unfortunate growers have paid
their freight, commission and other marketing ex-
penses, there is nothing coming their way which
means that there is a loss, since labor, water, manure
and seed have all to be paid for soon or late. The
grower has my sympathy, lor my own use 1 grew
some tomatoes this year. There were only a ew
dozen of them, but they wore me to a- shadow t a m
ing them in the way they should go. I shudder
when I think of how attenuated the producer must
be who grows his plants by the acre. U P'*°' d + ul
tomato-raising—which certainly does Produce the
goods—means seeding in a hot-bed, planting out,
sheltering, staking, mulching, pinching off laterals,
liquid manuring, hand-watering, disbudding and
spraying. Yet the tomatoist is prepared to do all
this tedious work as a mere gamble, and the odds
are that he will have the cards stacked on him m
the end. Then, having received his pitiful little bit
of money, he will go out and vote No six times
when the Referenda arrive. That is to say, he will
vote in favor of more fruit-ring, more agent-robber
more manure-combine and more heartbreak. on
second thoughts, I withdraw my sympathy.
* * * *
“Sine Joeo”: In these days of reforms, inquiries
and Royal Commissions, it is time the Lnclaimed
Balance was elevated to the rank of the Things
That Want Looking Into. Some States have laws
on the subject of the credit balance which nobody
stems to want, especially the rightful owner; but
these laws are mostly suffering from insomnia
Take the unclaimed deposits in the banks, both
fixed and current account. When the banks in 1890
lav down and took nine seconds breathing time,
thousands of pounds of money left till called for
were mopoed up in the reconstruction schemes and
disappeared into secret reserves, or were transferred
bv the medium of a pink slip to the credit of Bad
Debts Account. Some of the banks carried these
monevs forward to the new books; others did not.
The unclaimed divs. in the hands of legal managers
of mining cos. are rarely properly accounted .for to
the Government, in the absence of the rightful
claimants, and sharebrokers receive large sums as
dividends on shares put into their clerks names, for
snlittinsr up, or to protect dividends. What be
comes of these vagrant divvies? It is certain that
clients who are shareholders forget or never know of
many dividends. What do the sharebrokers do with
these windfalls? Do they pay them into a trust ac-
count? Trade assignees and company liquidators
hold large sums unclaimed bycreditorsand share-
holders, which they should rightly pay to the State
Treasury. Do they? The case of liquidation divs.
in defunct mining companies is specially on< ?
out for attention. Some legal managers who wind
up mining cos. simply ,pay the divs. not
lifted into their personal bank accounts, and there
the* evaporate. For a start, one clause m . Con \
monwealth Banking Bill forfeiting unclaimed balances
to the Crown would do good.

“Y Z ”: Mv friend is a new-chum, a tall, thin,
benign youth' of 24, who has been sent out by his
superior parents to make his fortune in Australia
He is expected home with the fortune towards the
end of 1913, but meanwhile is looking closely into
Melbourne institutions —such as hotels, music-halls
and theatres —with the aid of a powerful, single-
barrelled eyeglass. Towards the end of December last
Algie looked me up to explain that he had just been
apprised of the approach of a Christmas box from the
“deah old matah.” At Algiers request I accompanied
him to receive delivery and investigate the case, it
was 4ft. by 4ft. every way, and contained seven
gross of jumping-jacks (the ordinary pink monkey
on a vellow stick), two and twenty dozen of cheap
hand-mirrors, one gross of colored bead neck-
laces. five gross of brass rings, 100 brass
bangles, a lot of hair combs and one dozen small pot
gods. Algie’s “deali old matah” pointed out in her
loving letter to Algie, that these goods would be of
enormous value to her darling boy in enabling him
to make extensive deals in gold, ivory and arable
land with the simple Australian native races.
* * * *
“William Henry Croesus”: One of life’s little mas-
teries is how a man’s pockets come to be loaded up
with ha’pennies, always including an odd one. Do
you know? The pairs can be accounted for by tram-
change and so on, but what about the lonely odd
one? For, of course, I never buy things that have
an odd halfpenny in the price. So common, y’ know!
Suppose I must unknowingly accept bright halfpenee
in mistake for sovs., for, of course, it’s beastly bad
form for a fellow to look at his change.
* * * *
“Leel”: The oil contained in the lumps of fat
which the iguana wears in front of his dinner waggon
is far and away the best for use on oilstones that
I’ve ever struck. With this dressing the stone cuts
better and quicker than it does with any of the
imported goods that one buys at 6d. per bottle or so.
And it keeps moist and thin on the stone for an
exceptionally long time. What about this for an Aus-
tralian industry? Go. skins are worth about 6d. per
foot, too.
* * * *
It’s a good wind that blows nobody any ill, which
is a fact local cap manufacturers are realising in
regard to immigration. About 999 out of every
thousand immigrants wear caps, and the home-grown
Australian has decided in large numbers that he isn't
going to be mistaken for an immigrant, and has,
therefore, sworn off caps. Consequently retailers of
the item can’t get their stock off their shelves, and
they won’t give their usual orders, wherefore cap
manufacturers who anticipated the usual orders are
disgusted with immigration. Still, if the situation
is bad for the cap makers, hat makers are likely to
pouch a few pounds more.
* * * *
The Vic. Government might, if it were a public-
spirited rather than a self-seeking concern, do a
great stroke of popularity in the country by put-
ting an end to the damnable game of dredging for
gold in the waterways of the State. The loudest
protest against the dredge fiends comes just now from
the Ovens Valley, and people are looking for scan-
dalous reasons why the work of destruction has so
long gone on unchecked. One way and another, Vic-
toria pays darned dearly for its gold output.
FRUIT OF DREAMS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The burdened camel of the day
Lay down upon the tracks of night;
A sleep-wind blew the world away
And left my dreaming white.
With trays of crystal, overwinged
By radiant winnowings of air,
God’s nine tall ladies robed and ringed
Came down the starry stair.
Their clear, unslippered footsteps made
A tinkling music as they stept,
Their wild hair falling from the braid
About their shoulders swept.
The silver cloud on which I lay
Was broidered with their warm, sweet eyes,
And heaped on every shining tray
Were fruits of Paradise.
Not the forbidden fruits that fall
So lightly to the sinner’s touch,
Whose taste of honey, fire and gall
Burns the tongue overmuch.
Not those ambrosial things that grew
So redly when the world was young,
E’er Bacchus and his merry crew
A pine-topped thrysus swung.
Not apples from a convent wall,
Deep bowered in peace, where slow suns shone
And never shadow learned to fall,
In rest-sweet Avalon.
Not fruit of the Hesperides,
Plucked in the shining of the West,
By soft winds of the last low seas
To ripest gold caressed.
Not orchard wonders of the world,
Whose fragrance is a singing mist,
That lie in leaves of myth upcurled
Where comes no botanist.
God's nine tall ladies bore not these
Upon the crystal of their trays,
But such a fruit as best can please
These hard, dull, business days.
For round my cloudy, dreaming bed,
O’erstooped by those nine swan-like npek«,
The clear-writ signatures I read
On several dozen cheques.
And all of these were signed for me
With words and figures speaking joy,
That fifty duns of memory
Were helpless to destroy. .
Oh, it was good to live and breathe
And feel so much of gladness near,
To let my happy spirit seethe
In such an atmosphere!
But world-winds blew my dream away
And left me of delight forlorn;
The burdened camel of the day
Bose in the tracks of morn.
N.S.tf.
GEORGE STREET.
THE VALLEY OF THE SHADOW OF DEBT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They have walked through the Valley of the Shadow
of Debt
So long, till the dim, dead road
Seems never ending, and hearts forget
To groan at their dreary load ;
A moonless dark and a sunless day
They have known too long to regret,
But the twinge of thought still comes on the way
Of the Valley of the Shadow of Debt.
As they walk through the Valley of the Shadow
of Debt
They muse on their hapless day,
And dream of the deeds they may ’stablish yet,
When, free from its stifling sway ;
But sleepless wardens guard all escape,
And never their charge forget,
Not e’en at the gate that is hung with crape
In the Valley of the Shadow of Debt.
Though they walk through the Valley of the Shadow
of Debt
They have glimpses of men above,
With gold for pleasure and gold for sweat
And gold to barter for love;
And they see the children of plenty smile.
Then their own with wan cheeks wet,
So upon their quest for the pass they file
Through the Valley of the Shadow of Debt.
N.S.W.
NORMAN LILLRY.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will
be returned unless stamps accompany.

He’d started with a drovers’ camp.
We were shorthanded and the boss
Said he’d try the pummy grant.
He looked well upon a hoss.
The cattle were a wild mob.
At the second night’s camp out
We put poor chummy on to watch
With Bob the boy, a lazy lout.
Bob always tired said look here old man
I’ve got an awful head,
If you don’t mind being on your own
I’ll have a snooze in bed.
Chummy crept up to the boss’s tent.
I can’t see that white cow, he said.
Go to the devil you silly fool
Growled the boss as he turned in bed.
In half an hour he came and cried —
Boss the baldy steer has gone;
The boss woke up and swore then sighed
Then slipped his moleskins on.
Good Lord, he cried in anger wild,
Where has the herd gone you cur?
Oh ! they rushed about an hour ago
But I thought I’d break it gently sir.

HIGH RENTS IN MELB.
What will become of the poor of Melb.
High rents they’ve got to pay
The Landlords throughout the suburbs
Have all their grasping way
Cribs are raised from Twelve and Sixpence
To fifteen shillings per Week
If rents are raised much higher
There’ll be camps on the merry creek
High rent for a house in Melbourne
No matter how old or rough
God grant that an alteration
Will break the rent boom up.

Public Opinion and
Beale HigH-grade Pianos.
I lie great majority of our Pianos are sold on the personal recommendation of users, and we are constantly receiving letters from our
customers praising the Beale High-Grade Pianos. Our instruments are in use in every part of Australia; in New Zealand, Fiji, Samoa, New
Caledonia, Mauritius, South Africa, India, and on the Continent of Europe; and everywhere they give the same unbounded satisfaction.

We have literally thousands more
such testimonials, from almost every
part of the world. The immense suc-
cess attained by the Beale Pianos is
due solely to their high-grade quality.
From the very first we have given our
utmost endeavor to turn out only thor-
oughly reliable, soundly constructed
pianos, embodying the very best musi-
cal qualities. We have created a won-
derful factory organisation, and we
pay the best brains and the best skill,
so as to ensure a perfect result. We
realise that an Australian-made piano
is subjected to much closer scrutiny
in Australia than any imported piano,
and therefore we make —we are
compelled to make —our pianos much
better than those of our competitors.
At the same time,, by making pianos
in Australia, we are in the position to
make them much better suited to Aus-
tralian conditions. Any competent
cabinetmaker will tell you that timber
seasoned in the cold, damp climate of
Europe and America cannot be ex-
pected to withstand Australian ex-
tremes of dry heat as well as timber
which is seasoned in this climate. This
is a most important point in piano-
making, and is one good reason why
so many foreign pianos develop cracked
sounding-boards, split tuning-systems
and faulty casework soon after arrival
in this country. But no man can say
he ever saw a Beale piano with a
cracked sounding-board. We abso-
lutely guarantee that the sounding-
boards of Beale Pianos will never
crack in any climate, because our spe-
cial system of construction renders
cracking an impossibility, a* the same
time that it greatly enhances the tonal
qualities of the instrument. Beale
Pianos contain the only practical steel
tuning-system in the world —so there
can be no question of splitting of the
wrest-plank. And then our patented
system of cross-plied woodwork en-
sures permanence for the outside case
of the pianos.
Be sure of this: the better musician you are, the more you will delight in a Beale High Grade Piano.
We have always on hand a number of good second-hand pianos, taken in part payment of Beale Pianos.
Call and inspect the Beale Pianos now. Inspection does not put you under any obligation whatever,
whether you purchase.
If you cannot call, write for Catalogue, which we will be pleased to send post free.

bush fire, horse race or any other of a thousand and one incidents that
would make your picture-story a permanent record of one of the jolliest
occasions of your life.

previous experience is not necessary, no dark-room is needed, and we

The Massey Muddle in Maoriland.
The Massey Government in Maoriland is
tangled in the intricacies of a land deal from
which it may not be able to free itself with-
out earning the contempt of John Smith,
a substance which reveals itself at elections
in the form of the Boot. The last Tory
Government in M.L. ruined itself, or at ?fny
rate completed its ruin, through its methods
of handling the public estate, and although
Massey has branded the present crowd in
office with the large word “Reform,” its ideas
on the land question are much the same as
those held by Atkinson and Hall of last
century. In 1907 the Ward Government,
pressed by the Tories on the one hand and
the freehold advocates of its own following
on the other, legislated to permit holders of
leases-in-perpetuity to acquire the freehold,
As the lease-in-perpetuity was for. 999 years
without revaluation, nobody really cared
what was done with the thing as long as it
was vnt rid of. All the same, Ward’s crowd
thought it wise to put a definite clause in
the Bill making it clear that the person who
thus converted his leasehold ihto freehold
was only buying the surface, and that all
minerals below the surface were still the
property of the State. Massey swept this
clause out in his legislation of last session.
Now the party suddenly discovers that the
new arrangement is loaded in fact, it is
double-barrelled, and appears as if it might
°?i at j both ends. Gavin Brighton, of
Southland, held leases-in-perpetuity, one of
which, consisting of 1(3 acres and estimated
brined coal in it to the value
le P u^ c bused for £47. The fame of
the deed spread throughout the country and
alarmed the people in office, so that when
Brighton asked for the freehold of two
more coal-bearing blocks the Land Board,
acting under instructions, hung the mattei
up on the nail of procrastination until it
should be found in which way the cat
jumped or something. It was a burning and
a shining cat, and has up to date jumped in
well every direction. The .rown
, tfiC p m M ellington, by working overtime,
has found out that Brighton s claims can-
not go beyond the rights actually set ortl
in. his lease, and that his title is to the sur-
face value only, which seems like a. desperate
effort on the part of a distracted .1 listry
to advertise that it t be responsible for
its own debts. On the other hand, lawyer
T. F. Martin, when chased by the Opposi-
tion for an opinion, is absolutely certain that
freehold means freehold, and that Brigh-
ton’s purchase includes every blessed thing
right to the centre of the earth. On the top
of all this comes word that Brighton him-
self will institute proceedings at the next
sitting of the Supreme Court to compel the
Lands Department to give him the freehold
of the two sections for which his applica-
tion has not vet been granted. And on the
top of everything comes the further word
that Brighton does not want to make any-
thing out of the transaction foT himself, but
will use the freeholds, when obtained, in ac-
cordance with his “nrincipigs as a Social-
ist.” What his principles as a Socialist may
be he doesn’t explain. Meanwhile Massey
smiles the smile of cheerful rage, nr.d wishes
with all his stomach that he had left that
dangerous and unhappy beast called lease-
In-perpetuity alone. It has proved to be a
sleeping serpent, and when you wake up
an y month or section of a serpent 990
years long, and the whole vast animal turns
I'ound and looks at you, it is a hair-raising
experience.
The raiding of a baccarat club in London
at Christmas time brought some interest-
ing facts and figures to light. Twenty men
and women or “good social position” were
found on the premises. Counters lying on
the table and held in reserve represented;
£lO,OOO. A number of letters which were
seized had brought cheques for losses run-
ning as high as £l2OO and £l5OO, and the'
bank book of the club proprietor showed that;
he had paid £9OOO, as well as a dishonored
.cheque for £BOO, into his account within five'
weeks. This was only a matinge gaming
club, where the pigeons were in the habit of
dropping in to lose their money before din-
ner, and the heaviest losers, seemingly, were
the lady members.
«g t . Kilda-ite” : Things move with great
speed in Melbourne. Only three years ago
t j le R a ii W ays standing committee reported
j G f avor 0 f the extension of the Sandring-
ham line t 0 Beaumaris, but imposed the
con< jition that the residents should find the
j aud — a con dition frequently made with re-
gard t 0 cockspur country lines. The people,
however, who are chiefly interested in the
construction 0 f a line to Beaumaris are the
Melbourne residents who go holiday-making
there. Year after year the awful cars of
the Beaumaris Tram Co. attempt to carry
the holiday traffic. Last Anniversary day
t j ie co m ade a special effort to cope with the
crowds> and actually ran three cars an hour
w ijil e the railways were disgorging their
pac i ced masses of humanity at Sandringham
ever y 15 minutes. Meanwhile, the Govern-
ment j s haggling with the local landowner
tQ ge j. hi m to surrender enough ground for
the cons truction of a line in which he has
on jv a languid interest, and profitable State
business and public convenience are alike
jg Qored . The trail of Tait is still over the
Department, and it isn’t a wholesome trail,
Qr even a dece nt one, but the sort of mark
that m igßt be made by a very tired reptile
when on its way to a i una tic asylum,
Victoria is thinking, and none too soon,
about a re-arrangement of the boundaries of
its State constituencies. They were last
fixed up by Captain Cook, assisted by the
bunyip, and in course of time the old order
of things has become antiquated. Thus out
of 65 constituencies there are eight with less
than 7000 electors and seven with more than
IS.OOO. The smallest has 5793 electors,
and the largest has 20,610. The discrep-
ancies go according to no discoverable rule,
Melbourne City has 7726 voters, while Haw-
thorn, a tram-ride away, has nearly 21,000.
On the other hand, the peaceful country dis-
trict of Walhalla has less than 6000 consti-
tuents, while the dead-slow country district
of Mornington has nearly 13,000. It is all
laziness, neglect and accident.

AN EPILEPTIC FIT.
A famous London physician, who for years
made a study of this disease, has, after a number of
experiments, found a prescription which affords re-
lief in almost a marvellous manner. This has been
put on the market by Mr. I. W. Nicholl, Pharmaceu-
tical Chemist, of Belfast, under the name of
“Ozerine,” and the number of unsolicited testimonials
received speak well for its efficiency as a remedy
against this appalling disease. Your chemist can
easily get it for you.*
When Byron asked “Who would not be a boy?” he
was certainly not thinking of those men who find
themselves handicapped by a boyish appearance in
their efforts to climb the ladder of success. As a
rule, it is always the man with the strong, manly
face who is selected to fill a responsible position.
And yet he may be no better equipped in mind for
the big job than the “beardless youth.” The surest
way out for the man thus baulked is to grow a
moustache. This can easily be done by the use of
Capillarine, which has a 30 years’ record of success.
This preparation is sold by the well-known firm of
Craig and Aitken, 654 George Street, Sydney, at the
reasonable price of 10s. 6d. a jar, post free.*

BRISBANE.
IN BANANA LAND
“Gumlu” from the prolific North :
Re the water supply for irrigation drawn through
spears from shallow depths in the Herbert district.
I don t know that part, but the system has had a
thorough test in the Lower Burdekin countrv. About
18 years ago John Drysdale, of Kalamia and Pioneer
sugar mills, started to irrigate, drawing the bulk of
his water from lagoons; but for six years past
most of the sugar growers thereabouts have drawn
the bulk of their supply from shallow depths tapped
by means of spears. When it is remembered that
the Lower Burdekin runs two mills, and that prac-
cally the whole of the cane supplied to them is
undated, there should be no doubt now as to the
permanency of the water. Also the town of Ayr, a
little south of Townsville, gets its water from spears
at shallow depths; the old rain tank is practically
discarded. Every building has its pump or windmill.
1 know of one instance in that district where a
Dumping supply is obtained at 6ft. and sufficient
water obtained for 300 cattle daily.
This, from a man who ought to know,
suggests that Black Brother, at the Bar-
rambah settlement, isn’t getting a fair deal
trom the Bananaland Government:—
i Th ! death - rate at Barambah settlement is not
less than two per week. The annual Government Re-
port omits the death rate, while publishing other
statistics which tend to show the satisfactory state of
the settlement. The population of the settlement
cannot exceed 500.
(2) The rations provided are not enough to keep
those not aide to work alive. One bullock a week
is killed for meat for the blacks. Flour and tea are
served out, but not enough to live on.
(3) An inquiry was held early in January, 1913,
after complaints had been made by the blacks, and
the report of the Brisbane magistrate (Mr Mac-
donald) criticises the institution severely. The re-
port has not been published.
(4) An attempt was made by Mr. G. Arnell, a
dairy farmer of good standing at Murgon, to show
_ s^a^e affairs ; but the most important parts
of the letter were suppressed.
(5) I have attempted merely to state verifiable
facts. The local farmers benefit by the cheap labor
(a black receives 15s. a week, of which the settle-
ment takes ss. ; 2s. 6d. goes to the settlement’s sup-
port ; 2s. 6d. is banked for the black, who draws it
in stores at the settlement store) ; but the universal
opinion among farmers and station owners all round
is that the blacks are being killed off by starvation
and neglect. Under the present Government control
they are worse off than when allowed their freedom.
The theory of control may be good, but its actual
carrying out condemns the Government.
Here there are two things, at any rate, that
call for immediate attention —the matters
of food supply and excessive death rate.
Black Brother be a poor lot, on the
whole ; but this country can’t afford to sit
still under the imputation that it is getting
rid of what is left of him by cooping him up
in a “settleynent,” and then starving him to
death.
Spurred perhaps by the fact that Mac-
kay, the biggest sugar centre in Australia,
consumed 6884 gallons of imported whisky
last year, an agitation has been started to
resurrect a rum distillery in the district. A
million or two gallons of molasses are run
to waste at the 10 local sugar factories
every season; and the soul of the Economist
is sorely troubled thereat.
Surely they won’t care—unless there’s a
flood of limelight somewhere! The other day
Innisfail cried for help for its submerged and
ruined farmers, and their, in many cases,
hungry families. After some days of pro-
found silence a Brisbane daily reported the
result: “There has been practically no re-
sponse to the appeal for assistance for the
flooded settlers of Innisfail.”
No publican can now serve a Queens-
lander under 21 years of age. It recalls the
fact that, some dozen years ago, an English
Act forbade the supplying of liquor (except
in sealed bottles) to any child under. 14,
whereupon a number of Weary Willie philan-
thropists immediately announced that they
would camp outside a given pub every even-
ing, and go in for the “supper beer” when
the child came along for it with the usual
jug. Perchance Queensland may see the
establishment of a similar industry.
Died, in his 57th year, R. W. Thurlow, a
well-known and reputable Brisbane mer-
chant, one time an alderman and a twice-
defeated aspirant for the State Parliament.
Thurlow once ran against J. G. Drake for
Ennogera, and had the support of a fine,
active committee of Tory dames. An enemy
who knew Thurlow’s bitter antipathy to fe-
male suffrage asked him. towards the close of
a meeting, “What do you think of votes for
women?” And Thurlow answered promptly,
quite forgetting the women workers, who
nearly filled the platform, “I think women
would be a great deal better looking after
their homes than gadding about electioneer-
ing.” The platform cleared instanter, and
Drake galloped in an easy winner.
FRENZIED FINANCE.
The saddest blow ever given to the old
ladies of the Bananaland Legislative Coun-
cil fell upon them the other day when each
one received from the Commonwealth Gov-
ernment a small parcel of forms to be used
in claiming the maternity bonus. It may
have been an accidental gibe; but some of
them spoke about it with extraordinary bit-
terness.
Captain John Maekay, Brisbane’s well-
known waterfront identity, is still as hearty
as when he quit the diggings near Armidale
(N.S.W.) with an armed narty en route to
what is now the town of Maekay, in 1860.
He has seen the population of Sugaropolis
grow from one wallaby to 15,000 people or
more. The river which was named the
Maekay in honor of the leader of that little
party in the ’6o’s was, on the clumsy inter-
ference of Commodore Burnett, re-chris-
tened the Pioneer, he having already named
a stream flowing into Rockingham Bay the
Maekay. Two aborigines took part in the
early explorations to Maekay. One, “Spen-
cer’s Billy,” is still alive. It was he who
said, “Me and Jim Ready was the first two
bloomin’ white men to cross the range.”
Crusaders.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Health Department’s crusade against the mos-
quito pest is being continued with unabated vigor,
tor the fortnight ended 22nd inst., 43,789 yards of
natural breeding places were oiled. During the same
period 1/32 houses were inspected, and 363 gallons
of oil used in the operations.— Brisbane news item.
The old Crusader rode to work got up like
Brown’s Pressed Beef;
llis lady on the battlements wept quarts to
show her grief;
Then through the lonely months and years
she worked with fingers white
A tapestry wherein she wove the valor of her
knight—
Her true, devoted knight!
\Vhile he was pounding infidels with all his
steel-bound might.
Upon brave quests and far they went like
dauntless tins of jam.
To •spread the fight of Christendom, and kick,
and curse, and slam ;
With plain, archaic violence they sought
to save Mankind ;
They used to rack the infidel until he changed
his mind —
His Middle Ages mind.
They yearned to save his soul, so they were
cruel to be kind.
The new Crusader now is here. No armor
is he in ;
Instead of dagger, sword and lance, he bears
a shining tin;
He does not aim at saving souls—he’s merely
saving hides;
leu see him hard at work where’er the sharp
mosquito bides —
Where’er he bites and bides,
And down among the waters still his wrig-
gling hell-spawn hides.
Romance along some lines is dead. The
ancient stunts are done ;
With oil cans and such silly things the mod-
ern fights are won ;
Mild, splendid knights in spectacles are doing
wondrous things—
But not the sort of which a bard with burn-
ing ardor sings.
With great respect he sings!
But there’s scant inspiration in crude oil,
and cans, and stings !
QUEENSLAND.
A. GOODE KNIGHTES RESTE.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, March 1, 1913.
My Dear Tiiargomindaii,—
The Q.T.C. Races to-day were singularly
lacking in the fashionable element. The
Governor, with P. S. Bytli in attendance,
arrived early, and was led boxwards by
Charlie Morris, president of the club. Pretty
Mrs. Morris, Mr. and Mrs. Fletcher (of Eu-
rella station), and Mrs. Hooper also occu-
pied seats among the mighty and the dead-
heads.
The Jap was the principal novelty of the
week’s social events. On Monday afternoon
resident Japanese entertained their brothers
of the Soya and Adzuma at the Museum Gar-
dens, most of the aldermen and their wives
being part of the exhibits. The little brown
folk who paid for the feast stood in two neat
rows somewhere in the background, while the
aldermanic visitors and the Jap naval men
nibbled the feed.
On Tuesday the Chiel Macgregor, with the
Foxton aide as major domo, took the Asiatic
Admiral and his two captains over the Mu-
seum, after which Premieress Denham spread
the friendly teacup somewhere among the
roses in her garden at Ipswich-road.
The river trip planned by his Ex. on Tues-
day afternoon seemed to please our brown
acquaintances immensely. But, then, these
people are credited with dying the most pain-
ful deaths with a glad smile, so it is rather
hard to judge whether they are animated by
pleasure, contempt, physical agony or antici-
patory joy. Most of the Society dames
aboard gushed over the yellow mariners, and
went home with paper Japanese flags flutter-
ing on their palpitating bosoms ; so I suppose
an entente cordiale or something was estab-
lished.
Admiral Tochinai gave a luncheon on the
flagship on Wednesday, and on Thursday
morn the two ominous grey vessels weighed
anchor and put on all steam for Nippon.
Peter Murphy, M.L.C., who has perched
among the elders of the Legislative Coun-
cil for some years, has been made a life mem-
ber of the Q.T.C. for services in the good
cause.
Died this week in Sydney, R. W. Thur-
low, one of Brisbane’s leading merchants, and
one of the best judges of horseflesh in the
State. Thurlow’s jumpers have gained him
many blue ribbons and other trophies at the
Brisbane Show, and his three daughters are
as expert as the next man at taking horses
over the sticks. Thurlow took a keen interest
in tennis, and had been both president and
vice-president of the O.L.T.A. Tennis was
a family failing, and Mrs. Thurlow, Mr. Alf
Thurlow, and the Misses May and Eva Thur-
low (the latter is now Mrs. Tom Burstall,
of Sydney) have represented Queensland at
the game.
Another well-known citizen, George Warry,
passed over last night. His father, R. S.
Warry, was one of the first to don the
mayoral rabbit skin of Brisbane. As a side-
line from football and sailing he was a mer-
cantile broker. About four years ago he
married Miss Heal, and went round the world
for a wedding trip. While in America his
wife became seriously ill, and she died shortly
after their return to Brisbane. He was only
47 when he got the call.
Pretty Mrs. William Collins and her youth-
ful family left by mail train this morning for
Melbourne, where they will join the Medina
for London. Rumor (who oft-times is the
most perjured of her sex) says a handsome
R.N. man and a second plain gold band are
waiting for the widow in the Cold Country.
Mr. and Mrs. Ernie Baines are returning
to Bananaland by the Orama at the end of
the month. Daughter Hilda (now Mrs. Will
Forrest) and her husband will arrive (liv
a week earlier by the Osterley. I hear they
are bringing with them something new and
staggering in the way of motor-carts.
Another startling accident happened this
week. Mrs. Mclntyre, an old lady of 63,
whose son by an early marriage is Mr. M. H.
Finney, manager of the Australian Bank of
Commerce in Sydney, was driving to a wed-
ding reception, when the motor overturned,
and she died in less than 10 minutes as a re-
sult of her injuries.
At the meeting of the Mothers’ Union
district nursing home, on Thursday, an An-
glican cleric urged the nurses to increase
their fees now that Andy Fisher’s bonus has
come to stay. Five nounds is such a huge
sum, Thargomindah, that when the new little
Australian has been provided with the usual
garments, and the doctor and nurse paid, the
misguided mother will in all probability go
gay, and give a champagne christening party.
The Methodist Conference, which has been
with us all the week, blazoned forth into a
garden party to-dav. I didn’t hear any vote
of sympathy to the Q.T.C. on the recent burn-
ing of the tote at Ascot, but I presume the
omission was a mere accident.
Yours in the hour of the supper onion,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Gravel-scratcher”: Speaking generally,
Pearce has made an admirable Minister of
Defence; but he has displayed a lamentable
weakness in bending to the impertinent de-
mand of the Brisbane Hibernian Associa-
tion that no parade of the citizen forces be
held in the Ist Military District on St. Pat-
rick’s Day. The regulations require that
there be certain whole-day parades, apart
from the camp of continuous training; and
what better time for the purpose than a pub-
lic holiday, when a day’s work can be done
in daylight? The Hibernian demand was
admittedly based solely on the expectation
that, by prohibiting any parades on the
Seventeenth of Ireland, the numbers attend-
ing the sports might be augmented ; and this
gate-money appeal shouldn’t have been given
a moment’s quarter. Besides, it) is danger-
ous. The Churches have, repeatedly urged
that there should be no parddes or camps in
the Easter holidays; and this Hibernian
precedent will produce a resurrection of the
ecclesiastical protest, and prompt the Cale-
donians, St. George Society, Welshmen and
Australian Natives to claim “protection”
for the celebration of their own pet days.
The young man who is paid 4s. for compul-
sory attendance at a whole-day parade
prefers to have that parade on a
compulsory holiday, seeing that attendance
at parade on a working day involves a loss
of wages ; and the less direct loss of produc-
tion and wages the system is responsible for
the better. Australian defence is a serious
business, and while the forces are in a state
of transition, and the new system on its
trial, there should be no flaccid concessions
of sprats to religious or other organisations,
in return for what is. after all, a very doubt-
ful political mackerel.

Government Notices.
KALGOORLIE TO PORT
AUGUSTA RAILWAY.
SUPPLY AND DELIVERY OF JARRAH
SLEEPERS.
riIENDERS will be received until 11
X a.m. un TUESDAY, the 11th March,
1913, for the supply and delivery of
Three Hundred Thousand (300,000) Sawn
or Hewn Jarrah Sleepers—Dimensions Oft.
x lOin. x sin., to be of full size, rectangular
in section, cut from large trees and free
from all faults. Price to include delivery
in ships’ slings on railway trucks at Port
Augusta Wharf, but not to include Wharf-
age Charges at Port Augusta.
Alternative quotations may be made for
delivery on trucks alongside ship at Bun-
bury (W.A.).
Tenderers to state date of first delivery,
also rate of subsequent deliveries, and may
tender for the whole or any portion of the
quantity required. No tender necessarily
accepted.
Forms of Tender and Conditions of Con-
tract can be obtained at the qffice of the
Engineer-in-Chief, Commonwealth Railways
Construction Branch, 84 William Street,
and at the offices of the Commonwealth Pub-
lic Service Inspectors at Adelaide and Perth
respectively.
Tenders should be endorsed, “Tender for
Jarrah Sleepers for Kalgoorlie-Port Augusta
Railway”; to be addressed to “The Secre-
tary, Department of Home Affairs, Mel-
bourne.”
KING O’MALLEY,
Minister of State for Home Affairs.
